The Australian in the Tropics.
DR. AHEARNE’S paper on “The Aus-
tralian in the Tropics” (most of which
was here reprinted Sept. 29) justified its
space by its interest. It stated the con-
clusions of a Townsville medico in healthy condi-
tion, with a spouting kidney and a liver not too
much enlarged, who himself shows none of the
signs of race-deterioration which He attributes to
other North Queenslanders. And the conclusions
were worth a good deal as grounds for enquiry,
nothing as final judgments.
Dr. Ahearne’s argument was vitiated by three
things : First, his untenable standpoint; second,
his inadequate, data; third, his biassed deductions.
As to standpoint, no personal fault is found with
Dr. Ahearne for being a good Catholic, and
imagining that a Scripture-text settles contro-
versy. Even a bad religion won’t hurt a good
man very much ; and a good one may make
him all the better man. Heredity and
education are enough to make anybody a
Buddhist, or a Fire-Worshipper, or what not;
and a decent Fire-Worshipper is a much more
useful and agreeable, citizen than an in-decent
atheist. But the Fire-Worshipper (or I)r.
Ahearne) should keep his religion out of his
science. What is the use of quoting exploded
Hebrew cosmogony as a basis for argument about
coloured labour in Australia ? Where is the sense
of quoting an alleged utterance of St. Paul ? And
when Dr. Ahearne wrote that
An intelligent God equipped Man so that he should he
suitable to his environments ; accordingly, He designed
that men should vary in colour and shades of tempera-
ment to fitly occupy the multiple regions of the globe—
where did Dr. Ahearne obtain the credential
to judge his god’s intelligence? and by what
right does he foist upon us, as material for argu-
ment, his assumption of what his god designed
and why ?
Dr. Ahearne compares N.Q. boys with English
boys in respect of chest-girth. He does not say
how many boys were measured; or where, or
when, or by whom ; —consequently his compari-
son may be worth much or little —nobody knows.
In respect of height, he gives a deduction from
the measurement of 58 boys. The number is not
sufficient to warrant a generalisation. And if the
boys measured were all Townsville and Bowen
boys, then nothing whatever is proved or
indicated regarding Cairns, or Herberton,
or Croydon. boys. As Dr. Ahearne puts
forward a scientific argument, he should observe
the scientific method. He should state the extent
of his statistics in proportion to the unobserved
bulk, and say when and where his statistics were
obtained. His figures hint something and prove
nothing : he has started his hare, but not caught
it.
In North Queensland I find [he says] that the mean
length of the bodies of 58 boys exceeds the mean circum-
ference of their chests by 1 15-16 ins. : that is to say, in
North Queensland a boy is two inches taller than he
ought to be.
That is to say nothing of the sort. There are in
North Queensland about 4000 boys aged from 10
to 15 years ; and you cannot judge the height of
4000 from the measurement of 58. Dr. Ahearne
must widen his logical basis before he can erect
his logical superstructure. Possibly his conclu-
sion is right; but it isn’t proven, and he should
not state it as if it were proven.
The portion of Dr. Ahearne’s paper which was
tantamount to a defence of the employment of
coloured labour was here excised for space-
reasons; and only his appeal to the Federal
Parliament not to interfere with coloured labour
remained. That appeal differed from the appeals
of other coloured-labour apologists in that it
assumed a scientific foundation. But it has been
shown that Dr. Ahearne’s scientific founda-
tion is no foundation, inasmuch as his
data are insufficient to yield a conclusion.
His argument is a suggestion, not a proof ; and his
deductions remain at present little more than the
impressions of one alert observer —interesting, and
valuable so far as they go. It still remains un-
proven tha,t the white man—when, he has learnt
to adapt himself better to a tropical environment
—will be unable to perform the hard labour
of tropical industry. .We know, as a
matter of fact, that N. Q. sugar-planters have
hitherto employed coloured labour because
it is cheaper and more tractable ; because they
will not pursue their industry under the conditions
upon which white workers insist. Given a good
wage and an eight-hour day, white men can cut
cane just as well as black. • The proof is that they
do. If the “ sugar-laborer were paid £3 a week,
with an eight-hour shift, there would be no more
difficulty in getting white cane - cutters for
an Ingham plantation than there is in
getting white miners for a hot drive at Croy-
don. Dr. Ahearne may argue that the men
would degenerate, and their children would de-
fenerate ; but that is just the thing which has to
e proved, and which is not yet proved. Gener-
ally speaking, the health of white men in North
Queensland, living temperately and employed
actively, is as good as it is in South Queensland.
It is the women indoors, insufficiently exercised
and often improperly fed, who suffer most. And
for the children, when the inland plateau is
reached, there is nothing to show that their health
is not as good as it is anywhere in Australia.
Dr. Ahearne’s argument must therefore be con-
fined to the coastal districts and to sugar culture.
And even if he could prove all he affirms, there
are much more important things than the sugar
industry to consider. We don’t want the Aus-
tralia of a century hence to be faced with the
negro problem of Southern U.S.A., because a
comparative handful of white sugar-planters
emulate the selfishness of the handful of cotton-
planters who imported the nigger to America. The
preservation of the white breed is of infinitely
more moment than the preservation of one
of the many industries by which the white
breed can live. With all his sense of the value of
a coloured race in the tropics, Dr. Ahearne would
never marry a coloured woman. Why, then,
would he degrade Australia to marriage with a
coloured race ?
These considerations are not intended to detract
from the praise given to Dr. Ahearne’s paper.
The Bulletin wishes there were more enquirers
like him. And it asks other men to send along
personally-observed facts and figures dealing with
the question he has discussed :
Is the white race able to live and work and pros-
per in tropical Australia?
Nietzsche Dend.
YESTERDAY came the elegram from
Weimar announcing the death of Fried-
rich Nietzsche. I happened to be dining
w>h Herr Geheimrath G. There was
another Herr Geheimrath at the table, a Herr
Major, a Herr Doctor and myself. The second
Herr Geheimrath, a lusty white-haired old gentlo-
man, had been relating how he and other officers
in the war of ’7O, out of sheer devilment smashed
the porcelain stoves in a Frenchman’s house by
shying full champagne bottles at them. Which,
of course, is a sign of very high-bred civilisation.
Then someone, tne major, I think, remarked that
“ poor Nietzsche ” was dead. “Ishe ? Now God
be praised ! that’s good news ! So the fellow’s dead
at last, is he ? He should have died 30 years ago !
when he was serving in the Vosges campaign with
the ambulance corps, as volunteer.” I tried to
put in a good word for Nietzsche, but I might as
well have defended the death of Hans
Cordua, shot by Roberts in the Transvaal.
The others were polite enough to turn the discus-
sion to the merits of the two long-necked bottles
of Aar wine which stood sentinel on the table.
But I went on thinking of the sad fate of this
philosopher.
There is in Germany no better-hated name than
that of Nietzsche. And with reason ! for whether
he meant it or not his system spelt anarchy. He
altogether lacked that glum balance and sanity
which has made the Germans what they are. He
hated the Reich and its military system and its
bureaucracy with all his might. He adored
Goethe for the things in him to which his German
admirers shut their eyes. He derided Schiller, and
Von Treitschke, and German beer, and German
piety, and Wagner. He bewailed his misfortune in
having to write for future centuries in the heavy-
footed snoring prose of a land so besotted with
beer and Christianity. And yet—perhaps his
greatest merit is his mastery of German style, the
new lilt and freshness he gave to German prose,
the new verve and finish and incisiveness, so that
like an enchanted sword its thrusts pierce the
hearts of his opponents, through all their armour.
Wagner said his German was Latin. I suppose
Wagner meant that Tacitus-like finish which it
has. The questions Nietzsche asked humanity
are much more valuable than his own answers to
them, perhaps.
He was the genius of scepticism. For him the
whole heaven and earth stood full of marks of
interrogation, each with its fierce tragic signifi-
cance ; and he deemed himself to be the intellect
that Nature had brought forth to respond.
“ Thousands of years hence,” he says in one of his
letters, “mankind will swear by my name.”
After all, you see, he wanted to be recognised as
the benefactor of that humanity he often claimed
to despise. “By Nietzsche ! ” Lord, what fools
we mortals be !
Yet Nietzsche’s proudest claim was to be a
philosopher, and in his ieonoclasm he flung a torch
into the olden temple of philosophy which, though
it certainly has not burnt down the venerable
building as he intended, has at least left some of
its chambers a smoking ruin. He has cast his
spell over modern European literature : poets and
novelists alike are under his ban, and hundreds
of artists and musicians let their work speak for
his influence. Modern Europe dares not as yet
pronounce judgment on him, or upon the problems
and doctrines he put forth.
That Nietzsche’s mental disease was produced
by overwork there can be no doubt, and his
writings will always remain a monument of
human will and energy, a remarkable proof of
what a titanic spirit can do even with
the shadow and darkness of madness over-
hanging it, and in spite of madness. Since
1896, when his Antichrist thundered and
growled over Europe, his works have gradually
stood forth before the world in polyphemic cham-
pionship of a new and aristocratic ideal, and his
name has gone flashing and flickering through all
European thought, burning, enlightening, destroy-
ing, quickening with its darting fire. And all
this in spite of the pumps and fire brigades of the
orthodox. Even afar off, in the hyperborean
South, 1 hear it has been shouted from
Australian housetops, and Bishopscourt, Mel-
bourne, has shuddered at the German giant. For
good dear old Field Flowers Goe (O sweet re-
membered fragrance of Wordsworth and rose-
mary-tea !) recognises in Nietzsche’s principio in-
dividualionis a kindred spirit to his church’s lost
archangel, and never hears N.’s name, it is said,
without repeating a dozen aves and a couple of
paternosters, and asking for her ladyship’s epis-
copal eau-de-cologne to rid the smell of brimstone
from his nostrils. This —this is Fame.
Berlin, Aug. 26.
F.S.D.
“ Ignotus ” discusses a grammatical point—
You lay down the law about “ who and whom ” some-
where, as if- “who” was nominative and “whom”
accusative, and that was the end of it. As a matter of
'fact, usage sanctions such an apparent solecism as “ Who
do you think I saw in George-street to-day?” There
may be here a change of construction after “which.” I
don’t know. But lam much inclined to say that “ who ”
may be used as an accusative when not right under the
nose of a preposition. I mean that you must not say
“ By who was Hamlet written ? ” but you may say “ Who
was Hamlet written by ?” Yet, remember the phrase is
colloquial. As to “ Whom was Hamlet written by ?”it is
dissonant. You must use a paraphrase in heavy serious
writing. Milton, who wrote “ Belial, than whom a spirit
more lewd fell not from heaven,” most distinctly made
“ whom ” a nominative. He would never say “My father is
greater than me.” [“ Paradise Lost ” I, 490, for “ Belial.”]
Take again Mark viii, 27, “ Whom do men say I am?”
Luke ix, 18, “ Whom say the people that I am?” True,
both these are altered in the revised version, and perhaps
for the better, but the old “authorised” version is good
enough in a matter of this sort. There is a similar
puzzle in the last couplet but four of Locksley Hall, “ as
when life begun." Of course this should be “ began,”
only it wouldn’t rhyme. I often wonder whether Tenny-
son claimed as a right the use of a form that in anyone
else’s case would be a mere vulgarism ; or whether it was
a slip and he tried long to And a better line. Cruel of me
to note it? It will jar horribly on you now every time
you see it.
“ P.”—an Australian doctor—actually offers us
free advice—
The premature baldness of bookfellows and others is,
they may be glad to hear, usually due to a disease re-
joicing in the name of Alopecia Areata. Just now Mel-
bourne is suffering from an epidemic of it which has
grievously damaged numberless sweeping moustaches
and ambrosial scalp-locks. For all who travel on their
hair the outlook is serious. Some authorities ascribe the
disease to microbic action ; others give it a neurotic
origin ; but it gets there all the same. A slightly red-
dened bald patch forms the usual beginning, situated
about the top or back of the head, or about the ears.
Later on it spreads, becoming white and smooth ; the
process perhaps extending until a regular “ billiard-ball”
scalp results. At its Worst it can make a clean sweep of
every hair in head and body in a few hours ; but this is
only after a severe shock, mental or physical, thereby
lending colour to the nervous theory of origin. The
hereditary tendencies mentioned depend, probably, upon
a transmitted neurotic frailty, such nerve conditions
almost always (like hysteria, dipsomania, etc.) tending to
descend by cross-sex influence— i.e., father to daughter,
or mother to son.
Pace the authorities recently quoted, the general health
needs first attention, with regular exercise, sea-bathing.
and massage. Bookfellows often let their abdominal
muscles get slack, their livers and other digestive organs
resenting the process with vigour. Properly used dumb-
bells, or the gentle exercises of wrestling, rowing or
boxing, are excellent for coercing a contumacious liver.
A teaspoonful of Fellows’ Syrup of Hypophosphites,
with one-sixtieth of a grain of strychnine sulphate
in it, is an excellent tonic thrice a day after meals. For
local use a little of a mixture of one drachm of chrysarolin
with one ounce of lanoline should be rubbed into the
patches night and morning, due care being exercised with
regard to subsequent cleansing of hands, as chrysarolin is
a poison of power. Perchance some bookfellows are too
deeply buried in the dust of shelves to take all this
trouble for a mere personal ornament, but even they need
not comfortably anticipate the shining pate of wisdom—
or of the orchestra stalls. Occasionally the disease under-
goes spontaneous cure, and the thatch becomes as godd
as new. All the same, if the bookfellow is a young book-
fellow, and has a girl, he will do well not to become a
bald bookfellow if he can help it.
“Notos” explains—
You say that a salary “at the rate of £636 a year” is
too much to pay Jas. Bon wick for searching the London
archives in the interests of the Official Records of N.S.
Wales. But he pays a capable and necessary assistant
out of his salary, which makes all the difference.
Probably the editor of N.S.W. Historical Records
is the only person able to measure the value of
Bon wick’s work. It does not appear that anybody
else knows its amount or quality. If B. con-
tinuously employs another man, another salary
should be paid ; and the public should know
exactly what is paid for Bonwick’s services.
“Mansfield” supplies a translation of “The
Afrikander Volkslied,” a song of the Dutch race
in South Africa, written long before the present
shameful war. It is easily seen that the original
must be a stirring poem—worthy of the Nether-
landers who rolled back the Spanish tide of greedy
aggression, or of the Afrikanders who have been
borne down by the English tide.
Each nation has its native-land ;
We dwell on Afric’s barren sand.
To u 8 there is no spot so dear,
The whole world over, far or near;
Our pride and glory to proclaim
“ South Afric’s Children ” is our name.
Each nation has its native speech ;
As far as Cape and Transvaal reach
We speak one tongue, our Taal so dear,
We need no other language here.
The language which our fathers spoke
Suits well enough South Afric’s folk.
Each nation has a right to be,
E’en though but poor and weak as we ;
There is a power which governs all,
And makes the proudest tyrant fall;
He watches those that press us hard,
But will South Afric’s children guard.
The one great God holds in His hands
The fate of nations and of lands ;
He gives to each their separate speech,
Their home, their rights, their time to each.
Who this forgets will rue the day ;
O Lord, be thou South Afric’s stay !
A SONG FOR THE UNDER DOG.
Now here is a song for the under dog, the weak
under dog in the fight,
For though he is down, and he’s terribly down,
maphap he’s the dog that is right.
It isn’t the cur who is largest, you know, whose
morals are always the best,
And a sanctified pup with a halo, I trow, might
succumb in a physical test.
If might could make right—but it cannot, you
see, and I think you’ll admit it were quaint
If a blacksmith must always the best of men be
and a bruiser must pose as a saint.
The man who succeeds may succeed as a knave
and in morals fly fearfully light,
And that’s why your sympathy kindly I crave for
the weak under dog in the fight.
The martyrs who died for the cause that they
deemed was surely the cause of their God,
From whose wounds, gaping widely, the life-blood
has streamed till, it reddened the blossoming
sod ;
The martyrs who gave—it was all they could do—
their lives for the truth and the right,
What were they, bethink you with sorrow and
rue, save man’s under dogs in the fight ?
Perhaps in the far away end—but who knows ?
And your guess is no better than mine,
For we preface our knowledge always with “ Sup-
pose ” as the great verb “ to live ” we decline.
So putting all guesses straightway to the rear, it
seemeth most certainly right
To take off our hats and to heartily cheer for the
weak under dog in the fight.
So here is my cheer for the poor under dog. He
is not the strongest, but then,
It may hap that lie’s better by far than the dog
who chews him again and again.
His stock may be finer, his loyalty proved, and I
think you will hardly demur
When I say that quite often the dog on top is the
scurviest kind of a cur.
And as the rule runs in the big canine world, so it
runs witli us humans, I knowr—
Too often some cur of a man is on top, with a
really good fellow below ;
And that’s why I’m singing as best I know how
this lame little anthem to-night
To the poor hungry devil who’s clear out of luck,
the weak under dog in’the fight.
Walter Hurd .
MARY O’MALLEY.
Mary O’Malley lives down in our alley,
Upstairs, in the rear of a flat,
With her father and mother, her sister and
brother,
A parrot, two dogs, and a cat.
Her face is a posy, her cheeks are so rosy,
Her mouth is like honey and dew ;
Your heart’s in a shiver, your lips in a quiver,
When Mary is looking at you.
O me ! O my ! O Mary O’Malley !
The neighbours all know you’re the pride of the
alley !
You’re fair as a dream, you’re peaches and cream,
You’re sweeter than clover, a thousand times over!
And would you but marry,—you dear little
fairy !
Is it single I’d tarry ?
Nay, nary !
The first time I met her —how can I forget her !
She was bringing a basket of clothes ;
I looked at her sweetly, she spurned me com-
pletely,
And turned up her beautiful nose.
She’s cunningly saucy and very criss-crossy
And stubborn, yet once in a while
Your heart gaily dances because her sweet glances
Have wrapped you all up in a smile.
O me ! O my ! O Mary O’Malley !
Your glance is the light and the life of our alley !
You’re better than gold to have and to hold !
Be done with your teasing, your melting and
freezing!
O could I possess* you I’d feed you and dress you
And love and caress you,
God bless you !
Nixon Waterman.
A SUNSET.
Adonis-Ray falls wounded through the air:
Great crimson splashes stab his robe of light ;
And from her faint-starred throne bends Venus-Night
Weeping, to hide him with dishevelled hair.
G.C.W.
Homely Verses.
FOR HER DEAR SAKE.
For her dear sake I’d have her skies
As bright as are her own bright eyes,"
And all her day-dreams warm and fair
As is the-sunshine in her hair.
The Fates to her should be as kind
As are the thoughts in her pure mind,
And every bird I’d have awake
Its gladdest song for her dear sake.
For her dear sake I’d have each dart
Grief fashions for her tender heart
Aimed at my own thrice happy breast,
That hers might have unbroken rest.
She feel life’s sunshine, I its rain ;
She steal my pleasure, I her pain ;
Her path of roses 1 would make
And mine of thorns, for her dear sake.
If she should fall asleep and lie
So still, so very still that I
Would know her soul had slipped away
From her divinely moulded clay.
Then, looking in her fair, white face,
I’d pray to God : “In Thy good grace,
O, Father ! let me sleep, nor wake
Again on earth, for her dear sake.”
Nixon Waterman.

SOAP
has maintained its supremacy in the face of the whole
world’s competition. Such a record could not be
achieved without cause. Temporary successes are com-
paratively easy, but for an article to maintain its popu-
larity generation after generation it must appeal to
something more than passing fancy. This is the case
with Pears’ Soap. It is, and always has been, an honest
product. Everywhere abroad it has found a place in
public favour equal to that for so long held in England.
Men and women alike find it good and reliable. The
man who has once tried Pears’ Soap in the form of a
Shaving Stick w r ants no other, he takes it with him on all
his journeys. The woman who travels and fails to take
a supply of Pears’ must put up with substitutes until

“ matchless for the complexion.”

Various Verses.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The Other Side.
Here, on this green old earth,
Which is my dwelling-place,
I share the grief and mirth
And glory of my race.
Por me the roses bloom,
For me the sweet birds sing;
1 am the Prince to whom
Their fruits the seasons bring.
1 laugh with winds at play ;
I wanton witli the wave ;
“ This earth of mine,” I say,
“ My cradle is, and grave.”
And, in the silence vast
Of night, my spirit sees
Grey phantoms of the past.
And ancient tragedies.
I am of cave-men bred
Who looked upon the Flood :
The thoughts of all the dead
Are stirring in my blood.
I come of that high strain,
The men who thought and did ,
Who raised the Gothic fane
And built the Pyramid.
O dead men, long out-thrust
I rom light and life and song—
O kinsmen in the dust,
Your grasp is stark and strong !
It draws me evermore
To banks of dusky green,
Whence Charon plies his oar
Unto a shore unseen.
And so, when daylight dies
And stars begin to gleam,
My deeds and prayers and sighs
I send across the stream.
I go no more on quests
Of profit, near or far,
My dearest interests
Across the Dark Stream are.
For day by day I feel,
Amidst this world of men,
A grander Commonweal
Claims me as citizen.
Is it but all a dream
That, when this life is done,
Across the Stygian stream
There shines a fairer sun ?
I know not, and maybe
The Only God is Chance :
Yet Charon looks at me
With strange significance.
N.S.W.
VICTOR J. DALEY.
Rivals!
.There s a little bird that warbles ev’ry morning at
my door,
And the ditty that he sings is ever new ;
’Tis a tale of tender wooing and of love that lies
before
When the nesting days are due.
And whene’er the song is over—lo ! he spreads a
ready wing
And flashes through the morning shine,
lo pour a flood of canticles that ripple up and
ring
At the window where the roses twine.
There’s a queenly one who lendeth to the song a
list’ll ing ear—
(O ! the happy throat that trembles all a-tune).
There’s a laugh above the roses, loving-low and
silver-clear
As the bells of a night in June ;
Can ye marvel, when they mingle such a melody
a-near.
If a daring dream be mine,
And I venture me a moment in the chorus dear
At the window where the roses twine ?
Will he brook me as a rival—yonder throat of
throbbing brown ?
Will he face me as a doughty feathered foe ?
He is jealous of the blossom that she brushes with
her gown,
Of the kitten she caresses with her toe ;
And well I know he guesses in his tiny loving
breast
How both our hearts incline
To be dwelling by our lady in a dainty little nest
At the window where the roses twine !
Q.
P. LUFTIG.
A Settlement.
If I told you, little girl,
As you sit serene and muse,
With your red lips so compressed,
And that verse-book on your breast,
Of the flower, the rose you are—
Would you pinch me for stale news ?
If his verses please you so,
Would the poet meet your scorn ?
If he sought to lay his head
Where his verses Tie half-read—
Would he wake ycu from your dream ?
Would the rose unsheath a thorn ?
Ah, the verses on your breast!
And the book so gently nursed !
Must a poet ever make
Life a dream for others’ sake ?
Must he pour the wine and stand—
Stand for ever all athirst ?
If the song be worth a coin
(Such a coin as few would miss),
What’s to pay the soul that gave
All its best of gay or grave ?
If a song be worth a coin,
Sure the singer’s worth a kiss !
If the singer, little girl,
Pressed his suit for payment sweet,
I am sure that you who muse
Would not thrust him out—refuse ;
So . . . Ah well, that pays the debt :
And in kind ... I make receipt.
N.S.W.
RODERIC QUINN.

ANXIOUS TIMES IN THE COUNTRY.
Premier Lyne said at the Yass banquet that “he was the only man who knew the site for the Capital recommended by the Commissioner, and he regretted
that he must keep it to himself.”
MAYOR GUZZLEFUNCTION (of Goshen Flat, opening wine) : “ I s[?] e you don’t happen to know where the Capital is likely to be?"
LYNE: “Oh, yes, I have the plans and specifications up my sleeve, but I am not to be drawn—even with a corkscrew.”

SATURDAY, OCTOBER 13, 1900.
A Policy for the Commonwealth—The
Federal Tariff Some More.
Just now the air is full of appeals to the
people of the prospective Commonwealth to
choose between Freetrade and Protection ;
therefore it is advisable to point out that the
people who thus state the case are appealing
to the public under false pretences. The
case doesn’t lie between Freetrade and Pro-
tection any more than it does between
bigamy and Protection, or measles and Pro-
tection. There are no civilised Freetrade
countries in existence, and there never have
been within modern times ; the only places
which practise Freetrade are some of the
kanaka islands, a few hopelessly savage
tribes in the interior of Africa, and possibly
the Eskimo and their like. Even Britain,
with all its boast of Freetrade, does not
follow that doctrine, and, so far as is known,
it last did so about the days when its
aboriginal inhabitants painted themselves
blue and went to war in chariots. For
a country which raises about j £22,000,000
a year by Customs alone to call itself
Free trade is one of the great jokes of the
century. And for New South Wales, with
its protective duties on wine, beer, sugar,
cigars, cigarettes, tobacco, treacle, molasses,
biscuits, confectionery, and other things, and
with a purely customs revenue of £1,300,000,
to call itself Freetrade is a still more remark-
able proceeding. The question which the
electors of the Commonwealth have to decide
is one between Revenue Tariffism and Pro-
tection ; or, possibly, between the mixture of
Revenue Tariffism and Protection which is
practised in New South Wales and the
straight-out Protection which is followed in
Victoria and South Australia. It is as well
to call things by their right names and
accurately define the position. In New
South Wales a party which calls itself Free-
trade has been in power, mostly with huge
majorities, for the best part of 50 years, and
it has never dared to practise Freetrade.
Evidently it knew its own doctrine too well.
It has never dared even to get down to
straight-out Revenue Tariffism. It has
always adopted a certain measure of Pro-
tection, and has been a poor, mean, truck-
ling Ananias, and denied that it did so ;
and while it protected a few of its leading
industries, it used language about Protec-
tion which was violent and full of scorn.
Also it has persistently pointed to any
measure of prosperity that N.S.W. has
enjoyed under its mixture of Revenue-
Tariffism and Protection, and asked the
world to look at the glorious results of Free-
trade. All the days of its life it has been a
fraud, a turncoat and a shuffler, and the
truth has not been in it.
* • •
So when the people of the Commonwealth
are invited to choose between Freetrade
(which isn’t in the question at all) or Pro-
tection, and the examples of N.S.W. and
England are held up before them as matters
that have some bearing on the case, many
points require to be considered. New
South Wales is not Freetrade by prin-
ciple ; it probably drifted towards (never into)
that policy through having a large land-
revenue. It has more good arable and pas-
toral country than any other province, and
it has chief possession of the only big river
system in Australia to water that land and
make it valuable. Also, it has a larger land
revenue through being more reckless than
some of its neighbors in disposing of the
public estate. Thus the land revenue of
the six federating States was as follow’s in
1899 :
Land
Land Revenue
Revenue. per
Inhabitant.
New South Wales .. £2,002,023 £l9 9
Queensland .. .. 575,106 1 3 1
Westralia .. .. 142,720 0 17 0
South Australia .. 175,137 0 9 6
Tasmania .. .. 68,899 0 7 11
Victoria 395,012 0 6 9
Again, New South Wales has built up
the largest debt in the Commonwealth.
It has acquired the largest deficit in
the Commonwealth. And it has used
more loan funds for revenue purposes
(£10,915,047 up to June, 1899, against
Victoria's £3,060,767) than any other pro-
vince in the Commonwealth. It has done
these things because its tariff didn’t yield
enough revenue, and because its Freetrade
rulers dared not levy enough direct taxation
to make up the shortage lest the catch-
word about “lifting the burdens off the
people ” should become a bye-word and a
mockerj 7 . When the other provinces are
invited to follow the bright and shining
low-tariff example of N.S.W. it is ne.cessary
for them to remember that that example has
only been made possible by a reckless
policy of deficits, by a most lavish stealing
of loan funds for revenue purposes, and by
a geographical accident.
* * * *
The Commonwealth being invited to
adopt Freetrade, it is well to figure out, in
the first place, what Freetrade would amount
to. The adoption of real Freetrade would
involve raising the income-tax in N.S.W.
to about 2s. 6d. in the £ and the land-tax to
about sd. in the £ of capital value, and even
this is assuming that N.S.W. goes on appro-
priating at least £700,000 a year of loan
money for revenue purposes. In some of
the other States which are not blessed with
N.S. W.’s great land revenue, and which don’t
steal the loan funds so extensively, real Free-
trade would necessitate an income-tax of fully
ss. in the £ and a land-tax. that would almost
amount to confiscation. Even the adoption
of the mongrel semi-Protective revenue
tariff of N.S.W. would involve rather
serious results. In N.S.W., unless that
State is going to continue heaping up de-
ficits and using about £700,000 a year of
loan funds for revenue purposes—a policy
that must lead in the end to great disaster—
a continuance of the present tariff will in-
volve raising the income-tax to Is. in the £
and doubling the land-tax. In some of the
other provinces the N.S.W. tariff would in-
volve an income-tax of at least 2s. 6d. in the
£ and a land-tax equal to nearly half the
income derivable from the land—say, about
sd. or 6d. in the £ of capital value. All
the brilliant conversation about how Free-
trade would “ lift the burdens off the people”
takes no account of the fact that a more
staggering burden must be put on some-
where else to make good the deficit. Very
few people realise how very little direct
taxation brings in as compared with the
tariff revenue, even in the most Freetrade of
the Australias. In N.S.W. for the year
ended 30th June, 1899, the land and income
taxes yielded £454,000, while the tariff
yielded £1,621,800. After all the deafening
corrobboree which has gone on during the
last five years about establishing Free trade
and replacing the nefarious system of duties
with direct taxation, the tariff yields
nearly four times as much as the land
and income taxes, and times as much as
all forms of direct taxation put together.
It would take about another 4d. in the £
of land-tax and another 2s. in the £ of
income-tax to make even N.S.W.—the
lowest-tariff State —a Freetrade province,
and even that would leave its finances
no more honest or solvent than they
are now. The people who propose
to replace the tariff revenue by direct taxa-
tion apparently don’t understand the full
dimensions of the contract.
* * • •
If Australia, under Federation, goes on
borrowing as largely as in the past (which is
impossible, for its credit is giving out), and
accumulating deficits as liberally as in the
past (which is impossible, for deficits must
end in insolvency some day), and selling
land as vigorously as ever (which can’t be
done, because' the best land is sold), it will
require as much revenue under Federa-
tion as it has now. And as it can’t
do the three things mentioned above
it will presently require more revenue.
Most of the provinces, if they are
prepared to face an income-tax ranging as
high as 2s. 6d. in the £ and a land-tax vary-
ing up to about sd. in the £, can get down
to the N.S.W. level, and raise their required
revenue by duties on, say, 40 articles. If they
are not prepared to go beyond, say, 9d. in
the £ income-tax and ljd. in the £ land-tax,
with the exemptions at the present average
rate, then most of them must tax as
many articles as they do now, and do it
about as heavily as they do now. So the
choice lies between adjusting the unavoid-
able tariff on a revenue basis or a Pro-
tective basis. The difference is just this:
Protection yields revenue and at the same
time it creates industries and supplies em-
ployment and wages ; also, it is alleged that
it increases prices, and, to some extent, it
may do so—for a time at least. A revenue
tariff also raises revenue ; it undoubtedly in-
creases prices; and it is guaranteed by its own
most infatuated admirers not to create a single
industry, nor to supply employment or
wages to anybody. And added to Protec-
tion (which yields revenue, and furnishes
employment, and, it is said, raises prices),
and Revenue-Tariffism (which yields revenue
and doesn’t furnish employment, and raises
prices), there is a third alternative in
enormous direct taxation, which also yields
revenue, and doesn’t furnish employment,
and raises prices. It doesn’t raise prices in
name, but it gets there just the same in
reality. To leave the wages the same and
raise the price of commodities, or to leave
the price of commodities the same and take
away part of the wages by an income-tax, is
exactly the same result in the end.
* * * *
No country ever became a great industrial
State under Freetrade unless it had cheaper
labor than its neighbors, and cheap labor
means degradation and slavery. Britain
was the wealthiest nation in the world, the
greatest manufacturing, mining, and com-
mercial nation and one of the greatest agri-
cultural nations in the world, and the world’s
greatest shipowner, before it abandoned
Protection. It didn’t gain any of these ad-
vantages under its Revenue Tariff policy,
though it has lost several of them under that
policy. Nor can any nation, in these days
of cheap freights, remain a great industrial
State under Freetrade unless it pays as low
wages as the cheapest of its rivals, or unless
its workers can hold their own by exceptional
skill. Australia is young and its industries
are mostly in their infancy. Its workers
are, on the whole, less skilled than many of
their competitors. It doesn’t want to com-
pete in the matter of wages and be one
of the cheapest-labor countries on earth.
And if it opened its ports and reduced
its wages to the level of the cheapest of its
competitors, it would carry an additional han-
dicap, for, by reason of its vast accumulation
of foreign debt, its low-wage workers
would have to pay more in taxation than the
low-wage workers of almost every other land
beneath the sun. The politicians who prate
of what can be done in Australia under Free-
trade are discoursing on mere theory. No
Australian State has tried Freetrade yet.
In this country there is not one industry of
the kind that are exposed to foreign com-
petition which has grown up without being
either protected by a tariff or (what is the
same thing) directly or indirectly subsidised
by the State.
* * * *
The example of Britain has nothing to do
with Australia’s case. That country didn’t
grow up under Revenue Tariffism. Britain,
before it abandoned Protection, was not
only the greatest manufacturing, banking,
commercial, mining, shipowning, and an-
nexing nation on earth, but it had lent
money to almost all the nations of the globe,
and that money has been piling up at com-
pound interest ever since. Britain receives
a private income, or endowment, or pension
of about £80,000,000 a year (say £2 per in-
habitant annually) from foreign investments.
Australia has to pay away to foreign lands £3
per inhabitant annually on its public and pri-
vate foreign borrowings ; the difference is £5
per inhabitant per annum, or about 10s. a
week for every family. A few centuries of
Protection and foreign conquest, the looting
of another India, and the plunder of another
Spanish Empire, might make Australia as
rich as Britain, and then it might try
Revenue Tariffism on the same basis.
But this country can’t get into Britain’s
condition, even assuming it is a wholly
desirable condition, by adopiing Britain’s
tariff policy unless it is possible to adopt
Britain’s circumstances at the same time. The
alleged Freetrade party appeals to Aus-
tralia in general to throw off the “ burden ”
of Protection ; but unless Australia is going
to file its schedule and stop payment it can
only do this by putting on an equally heavy
and less productive burden somewhere else.
Protection may not do all -that some of its
most enthusiastic advocates claim in the way
of creating industries and supplying em-
ployment ; every policy has some over-
excitable adherents. But it does much in
that way, while the other available means of
raising the wind do absolutely nothing.
Taking Tasmania as an example, the Free-
trade party in that heavily-taxed isle is
crusading with great violence about the
money “ exacted ” from the people
by the Protective system as com-
pared with the alleged freedom enjoyed
in England under its alleged Freetrade.
But Tasmania’s real burden is its debt, and
no juggling with the fiscal system will shift
that one iota. Its real trouble is that it has
to find £275,000 a year for interest over and
above the amount earned by its railways and
public works , and that this one item of taxa-
tion—the taxation it has to levy for the
single purpose of paying interest —is as
much per inhabitant as all thts taxes levied
for all purposes in Canada, Russia, Sweden,
Norway, Switzerland, and half-a-dozen other
countries. Every Australian State has the
same trouble, in a greater or less
degree. Debt incurred for unramunera-
tive purposes is Australia’s one great diffi-
culty. If the Freetrade party, which dare
not practise its own doctrines, can show that a
change of tariffwill induce the British money-
lender to accept 10s. in the £ then there
may be some shadow of reason in the promise
to lift the burdens off the people by aban-
doning Protection. But otherwise it is
merely a case of Australia paying as much
for the policy which doesn’t supply employ-
ment as it does now for the policy which
does ; and abandoning the policy which is
so good that its advocates venture to practise
it, for the one that is so bad that its own
most violent apostles have always lacked the
courage to put it into full execution.
PLAIN ENGLISH.
One Penny and Six Months.
In one and the same column of Sydney Daily
Telegraph of sth October appeared two police-
court reports, which cast a lurid glare on the dis-
graceful laws, and the still more disgraceful
administration thereof, in this great country. In
one case an old man was charged with stealing
one penny out of the pocket of a woman unknown.
A casual civilian said he saw the penny stolen, but
the woman from whom it was alleged to have been
taken wasn’t produced, so there was practically
nothing but one man’s evidence in the matter.
And the sum involved was a whole penny. The
accused got SIX MONTHS HARD LABOUR
at Sydney Water Police Court. Same day and
almost the same hour, at the Central Police Court,
two members of a Sydney push were convicted of
a brutal and unprovoked assault. One of the
victims was so badly crippled that, though 22 days
had elapsed since the assault, he still had his arm
in a sling, from which it might be fairly inferred
that he had been at least partly incapaci-
tated for three weeks, and had suffered
much more than one pennyworth of
damage from loss of wages alone, to
say nothing of personal pain. The punishment
awarded to one offender was £7 fine or THREE
MONTHS ; to the other £2 fine or a casual trifle
of ONE MONTH. As both offenders had in-
flicted far more personal loss on their victims than
the man who stole the penny, it can only be
assumed that, in the opinion of the bench, the
brutality shown and the suffering inflicted were
redeeming features, and three months were
knocked off in one case, and five in the other, on
account of the brutality. It is very nearly time
that an inquiry was held to decide whether the
average Australian magistrate is merely a vacil-
lating idiot, or a person who has a natural and
inborn sympathy for brutality, or whether he is
terrorism by the push, or whether the push pays
him a cash subsidy to let its truculent members
down lightly. On the face of things it must
surely be one of the four. At present, by reason
of the “pushes” no Australian city is reasonably
safe for a British subject to live in. The average
Australian capital is far less safe to live in than the
Transvaal was in the Oom Paul days. There are
laws in existence which might suffice to suppress
the push’s chronic conspiracy against law and
order. Buttheyareneverenforced. The magistrates
have power to give the brutal-and-unprovoked-
assault ruffian six or 12 months, but he mostly
fines him a few shillings. When things get a
little worse than usual there is a spasmodic agita-
tion for giving the magistrates power to make it
two years, or one year aud a flogging, or some-
thing of the sort; and in that case he would still
fine the assaulter a few shillings. If the law-
makers of this country ever come to be in dead
earnest about making Australian cities fit places
for a decent British subject to live in, they will
fix a minimum penalty of six months’ hard labour
for assault in company (which is already an in-
dictable and heavily-punishable crime) and make
pushes illegal organisations, mere membership
whereof shall be good for six months. A push is
as distinctly a conspiracy against good order as a
Eenian or Nihilist society. It differs from them
only in that their ultimate intentions are good
and their violence is a (possibly misguided) effort
to attain a high ideal, while the push’s intentions
are wholly bad, and it aims, by bad methods, at
no ideal at all. The Australian law’s tender
regard for its push is wholly unaccountable.
The frequent plea that no special measures
are required because Australia is, on the whole, a
law-abiding country, is mere foolishness. The
law-abiding character of every country is judged
by published crime statistics, and they only record
arrests and convictions. By this method of com-
putation, a country where there are 1000 serious
crimes in a year and only 100 convictions looks
far more law-abiding than one where there are
only 200 serious crimes and 150 convictions. Even
with the most efficient police force serious crime
can be made to appear almost entirely absent if
the country is afflicted with a magistracy which
classes unprovoked assault, verging on man-
slaughter, among petty offences, and disposes of
it along with the drunks by the imposition of a
small fine ; and petty offences may lie made to
look very infrequent if the obscene-language man
and the Saturday night pavement obstructor arc
simply allowed to curse and obstruct without
being run in. The country with the worst
criminal record can be made to appear the one
with the best criminal record if its legal system is
only bad enough.
Justice as it is in Victoria.
That recent remarkable decision by a couple of
Carlton J’s.P., when they sentenced a woman to
two months’ gaol without hearing any defence,
was duly investigated by Solicitor-General
Davies, after his attention had been drawn to it.


It was shown that a summons had been duly
served on defendant (then in gaol, and unable,
owing to the solidity of the building, to break out
and appear in her own defence), and relying on
the case of Regina v. Abrahams, cited by the
two honorariums, Davies declines to interfere.
But this case, decided by Vic. Supreme Court a
few years ago, laid it down that the Court was
competent to sentence in their absence accused
persons who had been found guilty, which is
entirely different from trying a compulsory
absentee, and so finding her guilty. If the trial
of accused persons in their enforced absence, and
without any appearance on their behalf, is legal
in Victoria, then that State wastes a tremendous
amount of money, and expends much useful bobby
in gathering up evidence against accused persons
when it is so much simpler to gaol the defendant
for seven days on a charge of vagrancy, and then
try him for manslaughter or burglary in his
absence, and sentence him to death or 10 years.
A person who is not there to upset the case for
the prosecution with objectionable explanations
can be convicted at much less expense in the way
of investigation and evidence than a person who is
there, and the money thus saved could go towards
buying Union Jacks for the State schools. And,
on tne other hand, if this kind of trial isn’t legal,
then Minister Davies is a shameless and deliberate
law-breaker. The Justices say that they called on
defendant to appear, which, as the Crown had her
securely in gaol, and she couldn’t appear, was a
thrilling formality. Also, they say that they
heard the evidence for the prosecution carefully—
that the assaulted person seemed in a pitiable state
—and that the defendant (according to the evidence
for the prosecution) was a bad lot. All of which
has no bearing whatever on the real issue. Said
issue is that for the Crown to lock a person up on
one charge and then, without producing her in
Court or giving her any chance to defend herself,
to sentence her, in her compulsory absence, on
another charge, is illegal and a disgraceful travesty
of justice. Also that the two honorary J.sP. who
did this unlawful act, and the mean and truckling
Solicitor-General who illegally covered it up after-
wards, are alike a large, straggling, black blot on
any civilised community.
The Federation Question in Maoriland*
A Maorilander writes on the above subject:—
The importance of the question of Federation with
Australia has not quite disappeared from the minds of
Maoriland politicians. Premier Seddon sought to atone
for his culpable neglect in failing to have his province
represented at the Federal Convention by attempting to
establish an Island Federation. But Chamberlain’s reply
to his request that Fiji and the Cook Islands should be
incorporated with Maoriland quickly disillusioned the
optimistic Dick, who begins to realise that, despite his
Jingo fever and tall talk about the Imperial Council with
representatives from Greater Britain to assist in framing
the policy of the Empire, the Australian Commonwealth
is destined to become the controlling power in the
Pacific. Seddon had hoped that the Governor of Maori-
land, vested with the powers of High Commissioner of
the Pacific, would rival in dignity and power the Gov.-
General of the Commonwealth. Chamberlain’s reply
clearly indicates that England looks to Australia to shape
the destinies of the Pacific, and the reasonable inference
is that the Gov.-General will exercise jurisdiction as
High Commissioner of the Pacific. Seddon refuses, how-
ever, to despair. In reply to a recent question in the
House he ventured to predict that “ the tariff of the
Commonwealth would not be antagonistic to Maoriland
interests.” Considering that every country frames its
tariff to suit its own interests alone, and that Maoriland
never showed the least regard for Australian interests
when framing its tariff, i his is a drowning politician
clutching at a straw. In fact, Seddon seeks consolation
for his past indifference in the forlorn hope that the
tariffs of Australia and M.L. will be identical, and that
M.L.’s 750,000 will be able to establish reciprocity with
Australia’s 4,000,000. Herries and Massey, representing
the agricultural districts of Bay of Plenty and Franklin,
recently asked Seddon whether the House' would have an
opportunity this session of discussing the question of
federating with Australia, and some few of the members
thought it a favorable opportunity to air their
ignorance of the subject. Mining boomster Withe-
ford (Auckland) thought it idle and unnecessary
to trouble about so trivial a question as Federation
when the North Island Trunk Railway and other
works of momentous interest were unfinished. Napier
(Auckland), assured the House in all seriousness that
Maoriland would lose her individuality by joining the
Commonwealth. Recently Chief Justice Stout con-
tributed an article to a local magazine in which he dis-
cussed the provisions of the Commonwealth Act from a
Federal point of view, and did good service by showing
the shallowness of those who assert that. Federation
means the loss of Maoriland’s individuality. He analysed
the legislation of the past three years and showed that
the States will surrender very little of their legislative
powers. In 1899 sixty-four Acts were passed by the
Maoriland Parliament; of these 33 were general, 28 local
and personal, and 3 private. Of the general Acts, only
three dealt with matters that -would come under the
pow-irs of the Federal Parliament, namely the Immigra-
tion Restriction, Pacific Cable authorization, and Shipping
and Seaman’s Acts. All the other legislative work of the
Maoriland Parliament would not have been affected by its
being a State of the Australian Commonwealth. In 1898
there were only four Acts that would have been necessary
to have been enacted by the Parliament of the Common-
wealth, namely, the Divorce, Industrial Conciliation, and
Old Age Pensions Acts, and the B.N.Z. Act, as the B.N.Z.
carried on business beyond Maoriland. In 1897 only one
Act, the Patent Designs and Trade Marks Act Amendment
Act would have been required to be enacted by the
Commonwealth. Seddon is persistently burking the
question, but the failure of all his schemes to compensate
for diminution of Australian trade may eventually force
him to tackle the question of Federation in earnest.
Death Under Chloroform.
Puyyaka to The Bulletin :
Certain chloroform-death remarks by “ Layman ”
(29/9/’00) well voice the popular notion of an important
question, but it is curious from a professional point of
view to note how neither he nor the great bulk of his
fellow laymen have yet realised that chloroform is
practically out of date as an anaesthetic, and should never
be employed in the great majority of cases. It is small
blame to them, for most general practitioners, especially
those trained in Scottish schools, have not j'et awakened
to this hard and (to them) heretical fact. Somehow or
other a notion has become deeply rooted in the public
mind that ether is not a real anaesthetic, and that its
effects are not sufficiently deep for painless surgery. As
a matter of fact it is just as complete in every way, and
in most London hospitals it is used to the almost complete
exclusion of chloroform. From the point of view of
safety, ether is more than five times as safe as chloroform.
Ormsby and Juilliard’s statistics on the subject show
that while 214 deaths occurred in 676,767 administrations
of chloroform ; 407,553 ether anaesthesias only produced
25 deaths. The figures are quoted both to show the ex-
tensive use of ether in Europe as a general anaesthetic,
and to prove that the average given is drawn from as
wide a field as can be desired by the most carping critic.
Of all anaesthetics, nitrous oxide (“ laughing gas ”) is the
safest, but the short period of anaesthesia forbids its use
for any but the simplest operations. Then comes ether —
we will leave the ethidenes and other experimental anaes-
thetics alone—and, a long way after it, chloroform. The
more apparent advantages of ether over its opponent are
as follows:
(1) Ether is a stimulant to the heart, chloroform a de-
pressent. Hence there is no risk of the sudden heart col-
lapse which causes so many of the chloroform fatalities.
(2) The deep, audible respiration sounds of a normal
etherisation give immediate warning of any tendency to
failure of breathing or intercurrent asphyxia (e.g. tongue
falling back into throat, &c.). The light breathing of a
patient under chloroform is ofteh very difficult to guage
accurately, and this has caused many accidents.
(3) A patient under ether may be safely placed in any
position for purposes of operation. With chloroform the
raising of the upper part of the body (as in the sitting-
position in a dentist’s chair) throws a dangerous strain on
the depressed heart.
(4) With ether, an overdose can hardly be given, as the
symptoms at once show out alarmingly, and recovery is
rapid on admission of air. Chloroform, on the other
hand, exercises its effects stealthily, and, more important
still, the main cause of death from it is not an overdose,
but sudden heart failure in the early stages of adminis-
tration. A rapid pushing of chloroform is the safest
method of giving it, as even the occasional admission of
fresh air, which “ Layman ” appears to think necessary,
is apt to causa either vomiting or dangerous shock from
a partial return to consciousness. Death returns show
some curious facts. By far the greatest proportion of
chloroform deaths occur in connection with the
simplest operations. Teeth - extraction shows an
especial fatality, and the reduction of a dis-
location of the shoulder is practically never at-
tempted under chloroform by an up - to - date surgeon,
owing to the extraordinary death percentage. The
majority of deaths occur between 20 and 45, more in men
than women, more in vigorous, muscular people than in
the old and feeble—owing, probably, to the great force of
muscular spasm reacting on the lowered heart. Ninety
per cent, of deaths under chloroform occur within the
first 15 minutes, 50 per cent, before the anaesthesia is com-
plete ! The fatal syncope is probably in many cases due
to fright. The drug abolishes muscular power before it
clouds the thinking centres, and with many there comes
for an instant an awful feeling of utter helplessness when
they imagine that they feel the knife, and are powerless to
resist. The horror of that moment acts on a weak heart,
lowered by the drug’s action—and another death under
chloroform is chronicled in the newspapers. Here lies
the danger of the “ whiff of ch 10.,” beloved of the inex-
perienced hospital surgeon, and often of his elders, who
ought to know better. If the writer were a coroner,
he would certainly endeavor to commit any surgeon
who killed a patient with that “ whiff of chlo.” It
spells culpable ignorance of the action of the drug. Bad
chloroform, too, is far too frequent—most of it very bad
indeed. Ether, on the other hand, is easy to test and is
well made locally. Some'medicos who are disinclined —
or perchance unable—to administer ether will speak of
unpleasant after-effects from its use. Writer has seen
many hundred etherisations and has never met any after-
effects worth mentioning, but he has known two patients
to die from the after-effects of chloroform, in addition to
numerous instances of uncontrollable vomiting, &c.
Glover’s inhaler, which provides the safest and simplest
means of giving ether, is perhaps somewhat clumsy to
carry about in comparison with the bottle and mask of
chloroform, but this is its only drawback.
To sum up— ether is more than five times as safe as
chloroform, its anaesthesia is as deep, its after-effects less
dangerous, and it is less likely to contain poisonous
impurities. However youthful the administrator may be,
or be he ever so interested in the operation (which, need-
less to say, he should not be to the detriment of his
patient) it is scarcely possible for a fairly strong adult to
come to much harm under ether. In cases of diseased
heart where chloroform is notoriously inadmissible, ether
may be safely used. One point here—the physiologically
enlarged and strengthened heart of pregnancy enables a
parturient woman to stand chloroform with less risk than
any other human being. Except under similar conditions
the writer’s advice is—take ether every time.

The Brown Confederation.
More news of Premier Seddon’s fixed intention
to annex to Maoriland, if it can be done, the
120,000 niggers of Fiji keeps coming along the
wires. The Brown Confederation of the Islands
is in process of formation as a counterpoise to the
White Confederation of Australia and Tasmania.
And just here there is a fair chance to test the
sincerity of Richard of Kumara on a rather
important part. In refusing to have anything to
do with the Australian Commonwealth, Premier
Seddon has persistently assured the Maoriland
people that they won’t lose any trade by standing
outside the Federal customs barrier, for the
Commonwealth’s tariff will almost certainly be
so drafted as not to injure Maoriland’s trade. He
has almost promised that the Commonwealth will
draft its tariff with a careful eye to Maoriland’s
interests. Certainly Maoriland itself has never
drafted its tariff with the least regard to any in-
terests save its own. It charges on goods
from N.S.W., Victoria, S.A., and Westralia
higher duties than they charge on Maoriland
goods. And in any case most countries fix their
tariffs, whatever they may be, with a single eye to
what they regard as their own interests. Still
Premier Seddon assures Maoriland in general
that the Commonwealth will arrange its tariff
with an especial view to maintaining Maoriland’s
commercial prosperity, and that it can stand out
with a light heart. Under these circumstances
N.S.W. is getting up to ask Seddon a question,
It wants to know, supposing Fiji goes into the
Brown Confederation, whether the Maoriland
tariff will be extended to the islands to
the destruction of N.S.W.’s Fijian trade? If it
is not so extended then it is difficult to see
how there can be any Brown Confederation at all
(for the said Confederation must have a uniform
tariff) unless Maoriland is willing to reduce its
tariff for Australia’s benefit. And if the Maori-
land tariff is extended to Fiji, and N.S.W.’s trade
with the islands is destroyed for Maoriland’s bene-
fit, then even Seddon can hardly keep on assuring
his people that Australia will show Maoriland any
special consideration in framing its own customs
arrangements, and that Maoriland won’t lose any-
thing by being isolated from the Commonwealth.
So the White Confederation rises to ask the
Brown Confederation what it intends to do about
the matter.
The Ever-Revolving’ Wheel.
A Melbourne inventor has, it is claimed, solved
the problem of perpetual motion. The Age re-
ports on his invention with the gravity of a pauper
funeral in a slum on a wet day :
The invention, a model of which was seen by a repre-
sentative of the Age near Mr. Rose’s residence in Wright-
street, East Erahran, yesterday, relates to the construc-
tion of a gravity wheel, with concentric rings, secured to
arms radiating from the centre. These arms or weights
act as balance and driving levers, and are so nicely
arranged—those on the downward grade being two-thirds
heavier than those on the upward grade in action—that a
natural falling of the weights on the former grade ensures
perpetual motion. A cessation of the driving force can
only be effected by preventing the weights from falling,
and this is done by a very simple contrivance in the form
of a spring which acts on all the weights simultaneously.
Taking a wheel 40 feet in diameter and 3 feet wide, it ‘is
estimated that it will carry 1050 weights, and that the
driving power will be equal to 1443| tons from the centre
of the wheel. The cost of construction is about one-sixth
the cost of machinery for steam power, and it is also
claimed that the new motive power will reduce the work-
ing expenses 95 per cent. The model has been subjected
to continuous tests, and it is apparently capable, like the
brook, of “ going on for ever.”
That good old “overbalancing wheel,” as it came
to be called for brevity’s sake, has been invented
so often that to be invented just once more won’t
do it any harm. The famous Marquis of Worces-
ter exhibited a model built on something the same
idea in 1648. Orffyreus, of Hesse-Oassel, did it
again about 1715. In the 17th and 18th century
the British Patent Office was rushed by persons
who had discovered the overbalancing wheel all
over again. They all had an idea that by some
means the same weights could be made" to be
heavier or to have a stronger pull on the descending
side of the wheel than on the ascending side, but,
despite oceans of specious theory, the weight or
the pull remained the same. Out of some 200
“discoverers” of perpetual motion which the
writer has read up, about 150 went on the theory
which has just been freshly discovered in Mel-
bourne. Some others had a syphon principle—
the falling of water was to turn a wheel, and the
turning of the wheel was to raise the water so
that it could fall all over again. The rest were
mainly magnetic crazes, or worked by the
blessing of God. There are many cases in which
we have the assurances of most reliable persons
that the blessed machine did go; in one case there
is what looks like sound evidence that it went for
some months; but the great majority never
started, and all that started stopped again—and
stopped for good. The machines which started
were probably mostly worked by concealed springs.
The wildest previous inventor of the Melbourne
man’s machine was probably George Linton, of
London, in 1821. His wheel had jointed arms,
each with a heavy weight at end. The arms on
the top of the wheel were to lie close, but as the
wheel came round they were to fly out, and the
pull of the weight at the end of the flying arm
was to drag the wheel round. It was a kind of
revolving octopus with a brick at the end of each
tentacle. Linton is only mentioned among the
ruck because his machine was a specially cranky-
looking affair, and better calculated to amuse the
baby than most others. Perpetual motion is, to
put it briefly, the struggle of a man to lift himself
by his own collar by hand. James I. dabbled in
such things, one Cornelius Dreble helping him
and borrowing money off lum, and history is
strewn with names, mostly long and German, of
men who dug up the overbalancing wheel from its
obscurity for the many thousandth time, and gave-
brief spell of notoriety.
Sydney Daily Telegraph made recently .{he
remarkable statement that the people of Sydney
have in a few months given to what it calls
“charity” no less than £124,138. But of this
£41,856 13s. lid. went to the alleged Patriotic
Fund, which may or may not be “charity’’ac-
cording as you look at it (most of the donors no
doubt charged their subscriptions to advertising
account), and to the absurd Citizens’ Bushmen’s
Contingent Fund went £39,181135., which no one
but a lunatic would call charity. Then there are
Mr. Hugh Dixson’s donations to Baptist Union,
See., £11,750, for building churches and endowing
the clergy. That, though benevolent in its inten-
tion, can only be called charity by an absolute
perversion of language. Thus, out of the £124,000
nearly £lOO,OOO is not for charitable purposes at
all, and £BB,OOO is for two war-funds, which imply
the very negative of charity—not the relief, but
the infliction of suffering. So that for real
charity there remains only £24,000 —£9000 for a
Children’s Hospital and £15,350 for an Indian
Famine Relief Fund (into which the advertising
element again largely entered). And as for the
ways and means of collection, the less said the
better, as witness, among other things, the scores
of indignant letters received by The Bulletin
from public-school teachers.
“ M.” to The Bulletin :
Whence all this fuss about our men staying in S’Africa
after the war if the country pleases them and the terms
suit their ideas ? All who know anything of the men
personally were well aware when they went that “ if they
saw a good thing on ” they had no intention of returning.
And who could blame them, when they were glad to
enlist because they were either out of work here, or saw
no chance worth speaking of? The sham patriotism that
sent the contingents away is a bit late in the day in
mouthing its pretentious remonstrance about wanting
them back. The labor-market certainly does not require
them, for there are thousands of good men at present out
of work—not counting those who form the periodical
mass meetings and elect deputations to the Government.
The back-country does not want them, for the cruel
drought has knocked the life out of things.
The city and towns don’t ask for them, Heaven
knows. If the men can better themselves over-
sea it is the truest patriotism to let them remain.
It might benefit employers to have a superabund-
ance of labor to select from, but it can do good to
no one else to urge that the soldiers of fortune whom we
have sent away must be sent back. Besides, should not
these late-in-the-day patriots remember that our men
were sent to help take the country from the Boers, and,
the country having been taken, somebody has to keep it
in order ? Then why not our fellows—especially if they
get good wages and a farm to boot—as well as the men
from the Old Country ?
That sociable family party constituted for, in-
directly, regenerating the rising generation of
Victoria, and, directly, drawing up a scheme of
Bible School lessons that should be acceptable to
parents of all and of no denominations, sent in its
report the other day, with a recommendation that
a referendum should be taken on the matter at
the Federal elections. Why the question should
be delayed six or nine months when a general
election is only six weeks off passes lay compre-
hension ; if the taking of a referendum will settle
the matter with some hope of finality the sooner
the better. But no one hopes for any such sooth-
ing sequence. For, if the referendum says “No,”
then the parson will gird up his loins anew
and get himself once more into the breach.
And if the referendum says “Yes,” the most it
can do is to generally approve of Bible-teaching
in schools, and the never-ending shindy among
the sects as to what is to be taught must start
afresh; and that is just the trouble. After all,
there was some theoretical horse-sense about the
old persecutors who tried to keep the nation solid
in one faith. Where they broke down was in the
fact that they generally allowed a most incom-
petent individual to select the one creed which
was to survive, and they didn’t allow for human
nature.
Vic. Govt. Medical Officer Shields, supported
by Dr. Beattie Smith, gave a lucid explanation
of what he called “impulsive insanity,” in the
case of the man subsequently found guilty of the
Dawson (Gippsland) murder. Said Shields :
It was probable the nature of his act was not present to
his mind at the time of the murder, though it might come
upon him like a flash of lightning. There was a parallel
between epilepsy and a case like this. It might be a
sudden convulsion of idea, and then a feeling of
calmness when the impulse was past. . . . Accused had
been fond of the woman up to the time of the mur-
der ; there has been no evidence of premeditation,
and it must have been a sudden paroxysm, for he
had no time for consideration. If anyone had
stopped him before the act and pointed out to him
what he was about to do, the result would have been
different. Though at present his mind was comparatively
clear his actions and words were those of a simpleton. If
he had any consciousness at the time of the murder, it
was an insane consciousness, such as a person would have
while in a trance. . . He was not intellectually insane
but his conduct pointed to an explosion having taken
place in his mental nature at the time of the deed. He
had been overwhelmed by mental explosion.
It was further shown that the prisoner’s mother
and two brothers were of unsound mind, but the
bucolic jury could not appreciate the nicety of
Shields’ definition, and the Law promptly
ordered the victim of explosive impulse to be
hanged.

A striking commentary on the value of testimony
as to character occurred in the case of the notori-
ous William Buck, convicted at Melbourne, for
the fifteenth time, of serious assaults, unlawfully
wounding and minor offences. E. Redfearn, J.
Manifold and other racing men, who “ had
known him for some years as a frequenter of race
meetings,” said they knew nothing personally
against prisoner’s character. Even detective
Ward intimated that during a (treat, portion of
the time he had known Buck the man had
been well-behaved, except for a tendency to
drink and to quarrel with policemen. These
men, who, presumably, testified in the
hope of mitigating Buck’s prospective sen-
tence, “knew nothing personally against him,”
although his fame as a stousher of policemen and
inoffensive citizens is not merely local, it is all-
Australian. The testimony of Manifold and Co.
amounts, therefore, to this —that they, personally,
have (so far) escaped being “dealt with” by the
gentle Buck. In the case of men of Buck’s
order it is safe to assume that the convictions for
assault are in infinitesimal proportion to the actual
number of offences committed. Many a decent
young fellow is knocked about, and often per-
manently disfigured, but a police court case would
mean loss of a billet, or “ disgrace to the family,”
consequently the assaulter very often goes scot
free. Buck was sentenced to 18 months’ hard ;
just half Judge Gibson’s award to the (so far as
people know) theretofore-unconvicted man who
stole a ninepenny tin of baking-powder.

Softgoods Sargood, who naturally takes great in-
terest in any form of loyalty, even if it is sold by the
yard or the piece, proposes that the birth of Austra-
lian Nationalism shall be celebrated in Victoria by
a general hoisting of the British Flag over the State
Schools. The Duchess of York has been nominated
' by Sargood aforesaid as the most fitting Person-
age to put his brilliant scheme into practice. By
pressing a button at Melbourne Exhibition Build-
ing she could give a telegraphic signal for each
State school to hoist its Flag at the same critical
moment or thereabouts, so that “not only would
the opening of the first Commonwealth Parliament
of Australia by the grandson of our gracious
Queen be impressed upon the memories of
our children, but, in addition, they would realise
the fact of our being part of the greatest Empire in
the world.” The Bulletin is in favor of taking
Sargood seriously. He offers to contribute
200 flags to the Imperialistic cause, and this
chance of making the windy draper pay for the
advt. of his “loyalty” ought not to be missed.
“ IJp with the Gdorious Union Jack ” everywhere
—State schools, town-halls, public buildings of all
descriptions ! Feed the sentiment of Sakgoodian
loyalty with Flag of dear old Motherland until it
be thoroughly nauseated. Educate young Aus-
tralian minds to realise the absurdity of sub-
ordinating the national emblem of this country to
the emblem of a far-away island, and a healthy
desire to assert their ingrained pride of nation-
ality will come all the sooner. Give Sargoodian
loyalty enough Flag-cord and in due course it will
hang itself.

. of Works and all its doings, this terrifying indivi-
( dual wound up by suggesting that it should be
;■ elected by the ratepayers direct, members to be

J system,” said he, “we should not have a chairman
1 who set up as an engineer, a poet, and a local
j aristocratic despot, nor would a district be re-
. presented, as mine has, by successively a boot-
i maker, a tailor, a plasterer, a warder, a publican,
- and an agent.” After firing this pom-pom, he
• went into details, and the further he went the

payers’ money. . . I assume these cables are
paid for by the Board, as they are sent on its re-
. solutions, and the officers refuse to show me the
f vouchers, and I challenge the Board to disprove it. ”
E The witness wound up by a general statement
> that the Board should give up drinking, holding
5 expensive festivities, writing poetry, passing Jingo
; resolutions, and title-hunting ; that it should con-
; sist of paid men—for the man who worked for
| nothing was generally worth nothing ; and that it
[ should stick to business. A shocked Commission
adjourned just here, and went home to pour water
i over itself in order to find out if it was really
• awake.

Victorian M.L.C. Sachse said that he was
opposed to female suffrage because—
He disliked the idea of a woman leaving the quiet
sacred home circle and going into another channel,
putting herself practically in the position of a man,
arguing in public, and attending meetings which were
certainly not sentimental or connected with some of the
finest sentiments of life.
Yet politics (unless man has woefully degraded
them) are the science of promoting the happiness
and welfare of the whole community, and if these
are not the finest sentiments in life, then what
are ? And even the person who has a vote
seldom spends more than one evening in 100 at
political meetings. It is a common Tory theory
when woman-suffrage is on the board, that there
are six political meetings in every town every
week, and everybody attends them—but this is
hardly the case. And as for the quiet sacred home
circle it is an unhappy fact that about one-half
of city women have to live in noisy streets
where they can hardly hear themselves
speak in the quiet home for the howl of
the traffic outside, and the cries of the
family and the adjacent families playing in the
gutter, and the conversation of the adjacent fami-
lies and their cockatoos wafted through the jerry-
built partitions. And in the average Richmond
and Collingwood terrace house the family never
forms a circle because the regular living-room is so
small that if it did one member would be sitting
on the fire, and the one opposite him would bruise
his lower spine against the door-key. . It mostly
forms two straggling parallel lines, one on each
side of the room, though it occasionally resolves
itself into a cross and misshapen triangle. The
quiet, sacred home circle, indeed! Ye gods !
Glassey’s movement to obtain a Royal pardon
for the four strike-shearers- who fell within the
law in ’94, and are still gaoled in Queensland, is
characterised by a sufficiency of justice but an in-
sufficiency of reason. The Labor-party might
long ago have fittingly insisted that the case of
these men should be reviewed. In all countries it
is customary, when a strike fever has passed, to
review' sentences pronounced upon those who lost
their heads in the excitement. But instead of
moving even now along that line of justice Glas-
a Royal pardon because of the advent
of Federation. In the Middle Ages the birth of a
Royal heir was generally accompanied by the
flinging open of gaol doors. If Federation releases
shearer strikers, whom should it not release ?
Queensland public opinion, if rightly directed, is
strong enough to release these men because it is
just to do so, and an agitation in this direction
will be honorable both to the men and their chain
pions.
