Athenjeum Hall Haskett Smith Lectures.
“The Penalty of Crime” duly lifted lip
its voice and screamed at Sydney Criterion last
week. The programme states that it is now doing
its fifth year in the principal dust-boxes of Great
Britain—at least the programme really says the
principal theatres, but that is surely a misprint.
The “ Penalty ” is the dry howl of a very bad
man in an impossible land. Its bad man wades
in gore on a vast, comprehensive scale, and kills,
or tries to kill, nearly everybody ; and nobody
interferes with him to any great extent, but allows
him to rage up and down with impunity and other
articles. .As in 250 other melodramas, thus very
bad man is found by the old gentleman with his
head in the safe and his legs protruding into the
middle of the room, wherefore he naturally kills
the old man, and the latter’s son is promptly sus-
pected on a strong want of evidence. That feeble
old gentleman who comes on a powerful villain
with his head in the safe is a useful but idiotic
old prop that badly wants a rest. His
utter want of strategy makes this paper feel
angry. If lie seized the villain’s feet and
held them up while he called for help, it
is obvious that the villain could do nothing but
try to walk about on his hands and swear some-
thing awful, and the old gentleman has now been
slain so often by the man whom he found with his
head in the safe that he might know enough to try
this device. Failing this, he might either upset
the table on the bad man, or throw himself on
him before he turns round, or cripple him by
hitting his feet an awful welt with his umbrella.
But he does none of these things ; he merely cries
for help in a feeble voice that isn’t loud enough to
bring any help, though it is quite sufficient to
reveal his presence and thereby leads to his own
destruction. The sad and unpleasant old joke of
the funny man whose pants are pulled off under
the table does duty once more in the “ Penalty of
Crime ” ; but that drama presents one novelty in
the fact that Miss Dampier abducts herself
instead of being carried away by the conspirator
and his accomplice. She is chloroformed, with
the usual reckless inattention to the ways and
details of that drug, and remains insensible for a
minute; after which she rises up dead and walks out
with her abductor, and isn’t seen again till she
is found with her hands tied in a public-house.
Old Edmund Holloway meets with a worse fate,
for after being twice killed by the villain he dies
through fever accelerated by frequent death act-
ing on a week constitution.
* » * *
The two best members of the company—
Dampier himself and George Buffer—are both out
of the cast. Dampier is resting, probably because
there is no part in the “Penalty” which is any-
thing like large enough for him. Buffer (a small
dry-looking man with a tendency towards an
astonishing bell-topper), whose remarkable ability
as a character actor has pulled many a stodgy
melodrama out of the mud, is struggling with an
inflamed lung in the sacred privacy of home, and
playing his part badly for the first time in his
life. In their absence the only redeeming features
left are John Forde; Benson North, who plays a
certain kind of villain with some ability, and pos-
sibly Edmund Holloway. The rest of the co.
show up the saddening commonplace of the play,
and turn the limelight on its unprofitable bones
with great perseverance. The moral of the
“ Penalty ” is that the bad man shall have a good
time and a long run for his money, while two of
the good characters die early, and the rest are left
on a selection in Australia, where the hero will
presumably grow to be another Old Dad and spend
his declining years digging dry tanks and trying
to teach the horse to live on nothing.

The postponed drama of “ Ingomar, the
Barbarian,” is to appear at Her Majesty’s on
Saturday night. Nance O’Neil will be Parthenia,
as a matter of course, though Parthenia is gene-
rally supposed to have been a calm and queenly
girl, and how she will suit with Miss O’Neil’s
sturdy methods is a rather interesting problem.
Also, M‘Kee Rankin is to be the tremendous
Ingomar—the untamed and hairy young man
with the big stride and the long sword, which is
another interesting problem. Sydney remembers
M‘Kee Rankin best as the old battered German
father in “Magda,” and with that memory on its
mind the character of Ingomar doesn’t seem to fit,
somehow. Still, Mr. Rankin is a sterling and
versatile actor, and when he is found shaking a
loose and uncivilised limb in the Gallic forest it
may possibly be found that he fits remarkably
well.

This is the last week, or thereabouts, of
the brilliantly unwholesome show at the Queen’s
Hall, Sydney. Whoever desires to inspect the
figures there, and see how many diseases he is
liable to get, and how awful he is liable to look
when he gets them, is therefore exhorted to make
haste to the premises opposite the Criterion, and
get a valuable shock.

The Fitzgeralds’ Wild Beast and Trapeze
Show at Sydney (or rather, Redfem) Railway
Station (Sydney hasn’t a railway station) is drawing
to a close, and in a little while the lunch-eating
elephant, the equestrian lion, and the pony, one
of whose corners was manufactured minus a leg,
will be seen in that locality no more. The last-
named does not appear in the ring, but waits
patiently in the outside show with the tiger that
has the “korff” and other non-performers, who
remain, as it were, in the greenroom all night.

The Georgia Magnet has been doing a
good deal of drawing to Sydney Tivoli this week.
Various distinguished citizens are called down
from the dress-circle to test the Magnet’s bona-
fides, one gentleman having to be awakened
from a heavy slumber into which he had been
mesmerised by the previous performer. The
Magnet’s ability to resist five or six men at once
is interesting in a way, and remarkable in every
wa y, yet it is just as interesting to hear of it as to
see it, and when one strong man has been
unable to lift her there seems no reason why one
should sit through the attempts of some three or
foiir other men to do exactly the same thing. Mr.
W ill Whitburn is an amusing gentleman dressed
most of the time in rags, but it would be better if
his stories were somewhat less archaic than his
clothing. However, he possesses a small dog
which gracefully concludes his farce by hanging
on to somebody’s trousers and brings the affair to
an end with eclat. The evening’s entertainment
is likewise brightened by the fact that the Cake-
Walk is left out.
** * *
At Her Majesty’s (Melb.) the supply of
blood—warm blood—was to be turned off at the
managerial meter on Wednesday of this week.
“The Only Way” deserved t<?run longer than 10
nights, but the performance had moments of con-
vincing pathos, and Melbourne in general doesn’t
care to have its heart shaken-up three or four
times in one evening. There were complaints
that Sydney Carton, the barrister who§e
aspirations got blighted by the bottle-imp,
made people feel sad, whilst little Mimi
was quite too distressing, and this complaint
came to present writer’s knowledge from people
who paid their money regularly to witness the
alleged sorrows of a recent loud tragedienne, and
came away commenting cheerfully on her violent
demise. The one very pathetic point in “ The
Only Way ” was the silent effect of Miss Mimi
Musgrove when her hero put his lips to the sleep-
ing face of the woman whom he loved better than
old port, or life itself. The dramatist here takes
about 75 per cent, of the credit, seeing that the
Mimi character is not derived from Dickens, but
the scene depends for realism upon the person-
alities and careful self-restraint of the actors.
Miss Musgrove can be complimented on having
played the foremost figure in a remarkably fine
little picture.
* * * *
To-night (Thursday) Miss Crane’s feet are
to get another show in the Paris studio, and the
tone-deaf daughter of O’Farrell will tink of no-
thing but Svengali, Svengali, Svengali. The
“ Trilby” revival is to close the season on Satur-
day, after which Her Majesty’s closes for further
improvements, re-opening with “Rose of Persia”
on the 27th.
** * *
“The New East Lynne,” at Melbourne
Royal, is something The Bulletin hopes to be
able to bear with, for the sake of Little Willie.
At sundry times this journal has spoken harshly
concerning the other “ East Lynne ’’—the old,
original stand-by drama which has enabled hun-
dreds of despairing managements to pay the tur-
bulent orchestra a trifle on account. Now, to see
and hear the reconstructed “East Lynne” is to
know that the first offence might have been more
unpardonable than it seemed. The up-to-date
perversion of Mrs. Henry Wood’s “masterpiece”
adds insult to injury. John F. Sheridan disap-
points his faithful friend, the public, by appearing
as an Irish footman at the respectable establish-
ment of Archibald Carlyle, and what time Lady
Isabel isn’t eloping with her glossy Levison, or
coming back in blue spectacles to the
scene of her past hysteria, this super-
fluous Hibernian flunkey is mostly talking
to Gracie Whiteford. When Mr. A. Carlyle gets
chosen by the local snuffle league to stand for Par-
liament in the Joe Chamberlain interest, and
Levison the Betrayer pops up as an unaccountable
Labor candidate for the same seat, Sheridan in-
cites a mob of 10 voters to duck the evil baronet
in a horse-pond for assaulting Little Willie. Also,
Madame Isabel Vine tears off her blue specs, and
upbraids Levison on his way to the poll, and tells
him that she is now guarding her husband’s honor
against cruel attacks on his offspring. After
which Little Willie expires in a hurry, probably
from the clip across the ear that the villain gave
him, . or because his stepmother attends Papa’s
election meetings in a blue-silk gown and an opera-
cloak.
* » * *
Gracie Whiteford represents two items in
the festive portion of the agony. As a rule, she
is a trim-built parlormaid, engaged to Sheridan,
but sworn to remain single on account of Willie’s
cough. Occasionally she is her own twin sister,
who fell a victim to Levison, and is determined to
marry him as a punishment for his having com-
mitted several murders and elopements. Sheridan
and the twin Miss Whitefords are a long way out
of their natural element in any version of “ East
“Lynne,” yet the performance would certainly be
sadder if they were not there at all. The rest of
the cast, with the exception of H. O. Willard, as
Levison, likewise the intelligent damsel who pre-
tends to be Willie, amounts to a horribly con-
spicuous failure. A well-painted railway-accident
tableau is attached to the second act, and the
clear duty of Sheridan is to send his re-constructed
co. off by the Grenoble express, or whatever train
it is that comes to grief, and put them in the
carriage next to the engine, where they can’t
possibly escape their deserts.
m* * *
At the Bijou four members of the late
M'Adoo co. have come to wake up the first part
with their dusky melody and own particular
style of animation. The new quartette of
“corner” folk includes Billy M'Clain, likewise
Madame Cordelia, from Sydney side of Rickards’s,
and just as they were scoring double encores the
management got word that Melbourne’s own
Whitburn had given the Tivolians in Sydney a
good hard knock by way of equalising the ex-
change. Billee Barlow, in four different cos-
tumes, is a promising success at the Bijou, the
two Brothers on the horizontal bars coming next
in popular estimation after 9 o’clock. The bar
act is quite as risky as any of Billee’s songs, but
the lady’s changes of toilette land her an easy
winner at the finish.
*' * * *
Rev. Haskett Smith started a short career
of usefulness, at Melb. Athenaeum Hall, last
Saturday. The fluent shepherd looks as though
lecturing had agreed with him very well since his
first visit to Australia. His voice is in good
plump condition, his photographs are of the
highest moral and artistic character, and he
talks about them in a way that is interest-
ing even when it most resembles the way of a
serious-minded magazine. His first illustrated
yarn dealt with the Paris Exhibition. This week
he uncovers Ancient Egyptian mysteries, goes
into J ingo details re Gordon, Kitchener, and the
Mahdi’s tomb, and will probably have a good deal
to say about the Holy Land of plain fact, which
is so much dirtier than the Palestine of pious
fiction.
* * *- *
Melbourne doesn’t want reminding that
George Musgrove will re-open the Princess’s on
Saturday (13th) with a grand Opera co., who make
a first appearance— i.e. , some of them make it —in
“II Trovatore.” On Tuesday the other half of
the forces will mostly appear in “Faust,” and
for further particulars see next issue. The scale
of prices has been raised to three half-crowns for
the circle, where Wealth and Fashion are sup-
posed to gather: reserved stalls have gone up
one shilling, and if Melbourne can’t stand these
reasonable additions to the ordinary rates grand
opera is not likely to come again.
** # *
George Musgrove follows the policy of
MartinSimonsen in opening his grandopera season
with “ II Trovatore ” —but, Oh ! what a difference
in the mounting there will be. The idea, seem-
ingly, is to bring out the Italian tenor, Salvi, in
his most effective singing part, and impress old
opera patrons with the gorgeousness of the show
as compared with previous productions of “ II
Tro.” The “Faust” can hardly be put on more
handsomely than when Nellie Stewart played the
heroine long years ago, and Federici, as Mephis-
topheles, immortalised the occasion by dying of
heart disease two minutes after the curtain fell.
** * *
Marshall Hall did not attract big crowds
to his Adelaide Town-hall lectures during the last
week in Sept. On the first night the gross takings
were £8 10s., and on the second night but £B.
Prof. Ives, of Adelaide University, and Bryan
Wibberley, a musical Methodist parson who once
studied under Marshall Hall, were the chairmen,
and each slanged the people who came because
others stayed away. The expenses of the lectures
—including the fee paid to the peripatetic pro-
fessor—were about five times as great as the
takings.
One of Gov.-General Hopetoun’s success-
ful society achievements is a facile and effective
rendering of the Lord Chancellor’s patter-song in
Sullivan and Gilbert’s opera.
Writes Corporal Logan, of the first Queens-
land contingent:—
All through the roughest part of the campaign, out of
our company of 116 men, there were only 21 men. The
rest had all gone sick, or were knocking about some-
where. . . . About 100 of our chaps joined the police and
about 300 of us volunteered for the railway service.
For an infraction of the coal-weighing law
at Newcastle (N.S.W.), a colliery-proprietary has
been fined—in April £3, in Aug. £5, and in Sept.
£lO. The infraction was the same stubborn
offence in each instance, and the Crown pressed
to have the full penalty under the Act—£2o—in-
flicted. Would any other class of stubborn law-
breaker receive such kindly consideration ?
Queensland sugar figures for the past year
are none too lively. The South gave 612,86 ft tons
cane and 62,043 tons sugar. The North, 512,262
tons cane and 59,946 tons sugar. The Central
produced 12,335 tons cane and 1300 tons sugar.
About Mackay, the area is decreasing—that centre
showing 3007 acres less cane and 14,259 tons less
sugar. Bundaberg and Gin Gin showed a decrease
of 9789 tons sugar. Cairns, Childers, Bowen, Ing-
ham gave an increased output. Total yield for
1899, 123,289 tons—a decrease on previous year’s
production of 40,445 tons, or 25 per cent. Average
number of tons of cane per ton of sugar, 9'54,
against 9 42 for preceding year.
“NEW BABYLON.”
There’s a sinking ship and a grey-green sea
Somewhere about longitude 23 ;
And the calico waters howl in wrath
While a monster liner of painted lath
Picks up villain and hero and heroine—
The others drown in the yeasty brine.
The tale of a vessel lost and gone
Which never came back to Babylon.
There’s a hero, youthful and gay, of course,
Whose cash is staked on the wrong brown horse.
With a tall, bright hat and a troubled face
He lays his beans on the coming race.
He leaves the girl that he ought to wed—
The maiden with hair of golden red ;
And the girl he loves—she cuts him dead
When the horse that all his cash is on
Is licked in the race in Babylon.
There’s a rusty dame who thirsts for gin,
And some cows are inserted this drama in ;
There’s a girl who has lost her identity ;
A detective, a Chow, and burglars three.
The beginning is neither there nor here,
And the ending is very far from clear;
But the bad folks die or disappear,
And in act one-hundred-and-twenty-one
The good ones get married in Babylon.


The Austral Otis Engineering Co. have just
completed a concentrating test on the Wilfley Table
installed at their South Melbourne works of a parcel of
crushed ore representing the second product from the
concentrating mill at the Conrad mine, owned by Mr. J.
Howell, which hitherto has all been stacked on the mine
waiting the advent of a machine that would profitably
treat it without recrushing. The result of the Wilfley
test is a high-grade concentrate, and tailing-waste show-
ing very little values, and the Otis Co. have despatched
the tables for erection on the mine.*
* * * *
The Colonial Mutual now ranks amongst the
very best life assurance offices in the British Empire, both
as regards security and benefits and in the moderation of
its premium rates.*
The quantity of sulphides now treated per week
at the “ Barrier” compares favorably with the output of
any silver-lead mining field in the world. The difficulty
in treatment of sulphides is being successfully grappled
with mainly owing to improved systems of mechanical
“dressing.” In this the Luhrig Vanner plays a very
prominent part. A large number of these machines are
now in use there, and fresh orders are still being given to
the agents, Messrs. W. and J. Lempriere, Melbourne. The
South Mine people are remodelling their mill on up-to-
date lines, and will install twelve or more Luhrig Vanners
of the 1900 pattern. The British Co. is also erecting more
of these superior machines.*

pumps, flumimr, patent Nelyambo earth-scoops or tank-
cleaners, rabbit exterminators, refrigerators, ice-making
machinery, bolts,nuts,forgings, andallsortsof iron-work.*

The WILD CAT
COLUMN
Under the new N.S.W. Municipalities Bill,
municipal councils are given power to recover rates on
properties the owners of which can’t be found, by leasing
them for not more than seven years. The proviso seems
utterly worthless. If the property is a small suburban
allotment—and that is the kind of property that the
trouble is usually about —it can't be let, as a rule, for any
purpose except for building on (it is too small and barren
for cow-grazing), and no one would put up anything save
the worst kind of weatherboard hutch when he only had
a seven years’ lease. Very few people would put up even
that. What is wanted is power to sell the property for
unpaid rates. Nothing else will meet the difficulty.
* * * *
The State revenue and expenditure accounts for
last financial year are now all in, and, subject to some
small adjustments and alterations in one or two cases,
the results are as follows :
Revenue, Expenditure, Surplus,
1899-1900. 1899-1900. 1899-1900.
Maoriland .. £6,580,386 .. £5,140,128 .. £440,258
Westralia .. 2,875,396 .. 2,615,675 .. 259,721
Victoria .. 7,450,676 .. 7,318,945 .. 131,731
Tasmania .. 943,970 .. 871,453 .. 72,517
Queensland .. 4,588,207 .. 4,540,418 .. 47,789
Deficit.
S. Australia .. 2,765,858 .. 2,779,317 .. 13,459
N.S. Wales .. 10,223,392 .. 10,341,293 .. 117,901
Treasurer Holder, of S.A., turned his deficit into a paper
surplus by reckoning as ordinary revenue £15,000 donated
by Brookman to build a School of Mines, which was a remark-
able piece of sharp practice. The school not being built
yet, there is no corresponding outgoing, and Holder’s
accounts thus look £15,000 better than they really are.
Premier Lyne of N.S.W. turns his deficit into a surplus
by not reckoning £242,436 of war and plague expenditure,
which, for a man who is so solid on the Cash Basis idea, is
rather astonishing. He puts these sums into the
accumulated deficit without passing them through the
expenditure account, which makes his accounts look very
much better in his own mind. Premier Seddon makes
his surplus look larger than is shown above, by annexing
650,400 of sinking-fund moneys and putting them into
revenue. As far as is known, N.S.W. is the only State
where the war expenditure wasn’t paid out of current
revenue. If any other Treasurers have smuggled it into
the deficit or the loan fund it has been done with great
secrecy.
* « * •
Six provinces have also issued their estimates
for the current financial year :
Estimated Estimated Estimated
Revenue, Expenditure, Surplus,
1900-1901. 1900-1901. 1901-1901.

Maoriland .. 5,410,200 5,441,523 31,323
Victoria .. 7,482,350 7,563,263 80,913
Maoriland’s Treasurer turns his estimated deficit of
£31,323 into an estimated paper surplus of £21,477 by
including as revenue £52,800 to be annexed out of the
sinking fund. This is a regular device in Maoriland,
nevertheless it is a most objectionable one. Seddon
deliberately proposes to wreck his revenue this year by
the abolition of a number of revenue-producing duties.
He isn’t struck by necessity, or unforeseen disaster, or in
any way a victim of circumstances. He has devised, de-
fleliberately, a scheme for reducing the actual revende
below the expenditure, for abolishing the wise practice of
-paying part of the cost of new works out of revenue, and
for driving Maoriland back to the old.folly of depending
wholly on loans for its new public works. And why he
did it Providence only knows.
Also Treasurer Shiels, of Victoria, has turned
his prospective deficit of £80,913 into a prospective sur-
plus of £lOB7, by proposing to pay about £82,000 of this
year’s expenditure out of last year’s surplus. By this
notable device the £82,000 won’t appear either in this
or last year’s accounts, and the two surpluses will look
like £132,818, when there is really only a joint surplus of
£50,818 on the two years.
As for Premier Lyne, of N.S.W., he did exactly
what this paper, some weeks ago, suggested that he would
do. The Finance Committee, by whose advice he pro-
mised to stand, made, among others, three important
suggestions. It proposed, though not very forcibly, that
there should be a thorough cleaning up of all the various
deficits, and that, among other things, the Superannua-
tion Fund shortage should be made good. It proposed,
very forcibly, that the war and plague expenditure should
be charged to the deficit (not to ordinary expenditure).
And it proposed, as a counterpoise to fchis latter, that the
annual payment in reduction of the deficit should be in-
creased from £150,000 a year to £400,000. Lyne fell in
with the second idea so far that £117,901 of the war and
plague expenditure is left to add to the deficit, but he
didn’t increase the payment in reduction of the shortage
to £400,000; he simply left it at the old £150,000. And
for this year he also leaves it at £150,000, though there is
talk of a Bill providing for annual payments of £400,000
hereafter. Also Lyne leaves the bankrupt Superannuation
Fund to slide. There was room to make a name as the
Treasurer who Cleaned Everything Up, but Lyne has
failed, so far, to seize the opportunity. He simply joins
the ruck of Treasurers who Let Things Slide.
Tasmania, Westralia and Queensland all used
their surpluses honestly in reduction of the old deficit—
Westralia’s deficit being now wiped out. And for the
present year Tasmania stands out conspicuously as the
one State which is making a big courageous effort to clear
off the rest of its shortage and get square.
The most startling thing about the estimates is
the Queensland Treasurer’s assumption that he will get
£6OOO more this year than he did last. So far one-fourth
of the financial year has gone, and his revenue is £43,033
1 ss than it was for the September quarter of 1899.
“0.T.E.” (Parragundy): (1) Institution is
Quite sound. (2) Premium for £lOO with profits at age
EB, ± 2 4s. sd. (3) Depends on age at entry. Entering at
age 25 the minimum surrender value after 10 years would
be about £7 ss. though it might amount to a good deal
more. (4) Bonuses are only declared every third year.

The Provident and Industrial Insurance Co.
(Maoriland) completed its eleventh year on 30th June,
1900. Its progress during the last three years has been
as follows:
Loss on first eight years (to 30th June, 1897) £509
Taken from
Revenue. Claims. Expenses. Funds.
1898 ... £8,673 ±2,530 £7,837 £1,694
1899 .. 9,177 2,835 6,462 120
Added to
Funds.
1900 .. 9,797 2,936 6,195 666
Forfeited capital added to Assurance Fund 60
Loss on business in 11 years £1,597
The Provident and Industrial Insurance Co. has, however,
certain quaint methods for making its accounts look
better on paper. In 1898, when things were far from
well, it suddenly wrote up the value of its premises from
£1268 to £4soB—in other words it put £3240 on to
the nominal value, and alleged that the office had become
worth 3) times its previous price. As Tiik Bulletin has
said on several similar occasions, as regards other like
institutions, the thoughtfulness of the office in thus more
than trebling its value when the assistance was so badly
wanted can’t be too highly commended. But this paper
has very little faith in the sanguine view that a co. takes
of its property just when some outside assistance is badly
needed to paint the haggard countenance of the accounts,
so it prefers to estimate the position of the Provident and
Industrial with the office property at its old and perhaps
more reliable figure. In addition to this, an alleged asset
of £2599 consists simply of expenses not charged, but
carried forward to be written off at some time when the
paper aspect of the co. can better stand the shock. This
sort of thing is held to be allowable in the early days of
an industrial life concern, but one that is 11 years old
should not have such dubious features in its accounts.
* * * *
So The Bulletin estimates the position of the
Provident and Industrial Insurance Co. thus :
Liabilities. Assets.
Capital paid up ..£4,765 Fixed Deposit with
Less apparent loss 1,597 Government .. £5,000
Property .. .. 1,268
£3,168 Furniture, Outstand-
Advances and De- ing Premiums, &c. 1,479
posit (apparently Cash .. ... .. 17
money borrowed) 4,086
Sundry Debts .. 510
Assurance Fund ' .. Nil
£7,764 £7,764
The best feature in the case is that the Provident and
Industrial has, in the year just closed, so far reduced its
expenses as to save £666 out of a revenue of £9797. But
there is no evidence that the liabilities under its policies
haven’t increased during the year by over £666. The situa-
tion of a co. which, in its eleventh year, and after making
great economies, still spends 63 per cent, of its total
income in expenses, is very lamentable. The P. and I.
is liable under its policies for insurances to the extent
of some £200,000, and towards meeting this it doesn’t
seem to have really accumulated a copper. It
rather seems to have lost £1597, leaving as its certain
and reliable funds only the £3168 remaining out of its
paid-up capital, and even that is represented to some
extent by the office chairs and tables —assets that are
liable to shrink a good deal in realisation. What the pre-
sent value of the Provident and Industrial’s liabilities
may be this paper doesn’t know, but it seems reasonably
probable that they are more than £3168. On paper and
by its own peculiar methods of accountancy the Pro-
vident and Industrial has an accumulated assurance fund
of £4243, and the board speaks of this with some pride ;
but this alleged sum is only represented by the expenses
that haven’t been charged yet, and the alleged sudden
increase in the value of the office. And yet this concern
pays 5 per cent, dividend to its shareholders ! Why any
decently-governed country allows such an institution to
do business passes comprehension.

The South British Fire and Marine Insurance

The difference (£128,318) has gone to various reserves. In
1892 the total reserves of the South British Co. were
£95,325 (reserve fund, £30,000; reinsurance fund,
£58,890, and undivided profit £6435). They are now
£223,643 (reserve fund £120,000; reinsurance fund
£BO,OOO ; and undivided profit £23,643). This is a most
remarkable record of progress, especially in view of the
very handsome dividends which the shareholders receive.
The South British has a paid-up capital of £64,628, so its
reserves are equal to nearly four times the capital.
Maoriland is the only province of Australasia where they
grow fire insurance companies like this. In Australia the
one ambition of the shareholders is to sell the business to
the first English company that offers an advance on the
market price of the shares, and a number of insurance
companies which might have grown into 20 per cent, in-
vestments sold out in this fashion at a low figure just
when the business was becoming profitable. Then many
of the shareholders put their bit of the purchase money
into bogus banks, and finished up in the place best suited
to persons of their limited intelligence.

The Victorian Permanent Building Society
(Melbourne) shows, for the year ended 31st July, 1900, a
certain apparent improvement, though even in its
improved state things are very bad :

At 31st July, 1892, the Victorian Permanent Building
Society had a reserve fund of £63,276. It transferred
£10,333 of forfeited capital to reserve in 1897, and after
deducting the above losses £232 still remains. The ap-
parent trading loss has been growing smaller every year
since ’94 ; still, for the 12 months to 31st July, i9OO,
ordinary income was only £5849 against ordinary ex-
penditure (even with deposit interest at the very low
figure of 2£ per cent.) of £B9lO, and with such a huge gap
still to fill it seems certain that there will be losses for
some years yet, and the Victorian will have to commence
eating away.its capital (£231,490—5ti1l intact on paper).
And though the Victorian, when this paper last heard of
it, was supposed to pay 21 per cent, interest oh its de-
posits, it certainly does not seem, from the accounts, that
it pays any such amount; the sum due on deposits,
mortgage and overdraft is set down as over £300,000
(fixed deposits alone are £240,000) and the alleged actual
interest payments for the year are £4llO, which is un-
accountably small. The Victorian has assets of a nominal
value of £645,000, and the gross income is £sB49—less
than £1 2s. per cent. Assuming that the assets earn
even the very small amount of 4 per cent, gross on their
real present value, they are apparently worth £146,225,
at which rate the capital is wholly lost, and there isn t
enough to pay 10s. in the £ to depositors—unless, that
is, a large proportion of the mortgage money has been
advanced on vacant land which may have a value though
it yields nothing at present. On the whole, it is a most
hopeless-looking situation, unless by some means or other
the return from the society’s properties can be about
trebled, and even then it would look none too good.

The Colonial Finance, Mortgage, Investment,
and Guarantee Corporation (Sydney), which got to the end
of its 12th year on 31st August, 1900, is a relic of the boom
which has made tremendous efforts to keep out of liqui-
dation and pay everybody in full. The following is its
record for the past three years :
Losses at 31st August, 1897 .. £124,318
Ordinary Working Trading Loss on Total
Incom’C. Expenses. Loss. Properties. Loss.
1898 .. £760 .. £4,841 .. £4,081 .. £2,799 .. 6,880
, 1899 .. 987 .. 4,167 .. 3,180 .. 4,356 .. 7,536
1900 .. 629.. 4,068.. 3,539 .. 1,351 .. 4,890
Accumulated losses to date .-. £143,624
Any ordinary concern would have collapsed ■ long ago
under these conditions, but the Colonial Finance, Mort-
gage, Investment and Guarantee Corporation (Bruce
Smith, chairman) has a very unusual body of share-
holders who are evidently resolved to wind up their
disastrous concern honorably, and pay everybody, except
themselves, to the last cent. At 31gt August, 1898, the vi-
sible accumulated loss was £131,198 and the paid-up capital
£127,859, so there was a visible deficiency, as regards out-
side creditors, of £3339. In the next two years further
losses were made to the extent of £12,426, and to cover
this the shareholders paid £18,947 in calls, so the accu-
mutated loss is now £143,624 and the paid-up capital
£146,806, leaving a surplus of £3IS2 for the protection of
outside creditors. As already mentioned this is a very
unusual kind of company ; it seems to have almost the
only conscience in Australia just now.
* » * *
The announcement of .T. C. Moulden, manager
of the Junction, that pyrrhotite (magnetic iron) occurs
in the ore in that mine has attracted a good deal of
notice, though the discovery does not appear to be new ;
the Australian Metal Co., which runs the Broken Hill
zinc-works on the magnetic separation principle,
announces that it has been long aware of the existence of
the metal. But the Metal Co. isn’t in the habit of telling
people what it knows ; hence the value of Mr. Moulden’s
find. The presence of pyrrhotite explains why the re-
covery of silver by concentration is so small (while 70 per
c?nt. of the lead is obtained, as little as 35 per cent, of
the silver is won by some mills); for much of the silver is
found in association with the pyrrhotite, whose
specific gravity is only 4.6, so that it is not
saved with the lead concentrates. Of course,
the discovery of the mineral does not solve the
sulphide problem ; but anyhow it indicates more pre-
cisely the difficulty to be overcome—or, at least, one of
the difficulties. Also, since pyrrhotite is susceptible to a
weak magnetic field, it seems to suggest that most
success will be along the path of magnetic concentration.
All the same, the Metal Co.’s magnetic separation works
(the Australian Metal Co. is an offshoot from the Frank-
furt-on-Maine Co.) are almost idle, the explanation given
being that it does not pay to give the high prices for
freight now asked—that, in fact, it hardly pays to ship
the zinc concentrates except as ballast or for stiffening
at a nominal rate Incredulous people are not, however,
swallowing the explanation whole.
* * * *
At the Broken Hill Consols the vein of almost
pure silver cut into is only 6in. wide, with 2 or 3ft. of fair
ore on either side. These deposits occur in pockets con-
nected by an indicator ; but the present one is the richest
since ’9l.
* » * *
Great Central Freehold, the Mt. Hope (Cobar
district) show, which a strong Melb. crowd has got hold
of, is to be put under the management of J. O. Armstrong,
who leaves the B.H. Prop.’s 5000-ton mill to take it over.
Armstrong is a thoroughly good man, experienced in both
concentrating and smelting work. The.mineis6o miles
from railage ; and the idea is first to inquire into the
feasibleness of smelting, and if that is out of the question,
to concentrate. The B.H. Prop.’s present total mill
capacity is about 8500 tons a week ; when the new mill is
fully running the total will be close up to 11,000 tons.
The present difficulty is shortage of a uniform ore supply.

N.S.W. coal. —Says the Sabbath tipster :
“The success which has followed the advice we have so
repeatedly given in regard to coal-shares has been gratify-
ing to our readers, and we are glad to know that many of
them have participated.” Then he tips Stocktons—says
one of the shrewdest men on ’Change bought ’em by
the thousand, and asks his readers to imagine the divs.
Stocktons will pay when coal is sold at 11s. a ton. Now,
though the Stockton colliery has an immense area, it has
perhaps the most precarious seam of the district. Be-
sides that, it has entered into a three years’ contract with
Melb. Gas Co. to supply 160,000 tons of coal (the bulk of
its output) a year, commencing—ominous date ! —lst
April next, at Bs. per ton. That is why Stocktons have
not risen like other coal-stocks—and a very good reason,
too.
• * • •
Peak Hill, N.S.W. —“The smelting furnace
resumed operations, but after running three days a mishap
occurred with the blast-pipes and the repairs will take a
fortnight to effect.” Seems that this concern is just
starting its troubles instead of ending them as many of
the call-paying shareholders had hoped. Smelting is an
excellent thing if you have the things to smelt with ; if
not there is the devil to pay, as witness the Overflow and
other N.S.W. amateur-run mines.
• * » *
N.S.W. Dredging.—“ Shares continue very life-
less.” No wonder, considering the many mistakes
already made. Clarence River dredge’s first returns have
been so unsatisfactory that shares have dropped right out
of the market. One enthusiastic big holder in this con-
cern often declared that it would be worth £5OOO a year
to him alone. Federal Centrifugal, near Braid wood, had
a good return last month, a very poor one this. Another
call, the 15th. Bad sign, shares ss.—less than half
what they were a few months ago. The Turon
has had a spurt—“ appears to have entered upon
the profitable mission predicted for it on its
inception.” The returns are suddenly doubled.
Now, if a dredge works for many months at a certain
average return that is suddenly doubled, which is the
more likely—that it will go hack to the old average or
keep up to the new? No newspaper anticipates the
former, which nevertheless may happen. All the same,
the Turon people have shown themselves good stickers
and deserve all the luck they can possibly get. In most
other instances all sellers, no buyers. So far, the failures
and mistakes in N.S.W. dredging largely outnumber suc-
cesses, and have effectually squashed thie boom.

N.S.W. Ironworks : £lO,OOO placed in N.S.W.
Treasury as a guarantee for the erection of ironworks
provided Govt, will undertake to take 209,000 tons
steel rails within a certain time. Many people and news-
papers have jumped to the conclusion that this has
something to do with the Careoar or Wallerawang iron-
deposits. ' Nothing of the kind. The owners of an
enormous iron-deposit within a few miles of Emu
Bay, Tas., have determined to ship the ore
to N.S.W. for smelting purposes, probably to the
Illawarra district, where coal is cheap, and have put
up this money as an evidence of their bona-fides.
There are what look like extensive iron deposits in
N.S.W. Western district, but it is quite doubtful if they
are as big as they seem on the surface. First
appearances are most deceptive in re iron outcrops. In
nine cases out of ten at least they are simply floating
deposits, disappearing or becoming sihcious at d*pth-
Too often this has been found to be the ca.se whe P
specting for iron flux. Writer heard recently of a place
where ft had been determined to tunnel through «nai
appeared to be a hill of ironstone. The tunnel had no
gone far ere it got into country-rock, a od "hen it was
examined by one of the the boss
was asked why he turned the tunnel into •
only reply was—“ Well, I thought it best to follow the
iron, si P r!” Which illustrates the position of most
N.S.W. iron deposits. # ,

Great Fingall Consolidated. This is the old
Dav Dawn mine on the Murchison ; it has had a
development within the last few months. Has <O,OOO
tons ore reserves above No. 6 level, and another large
quantity will he exposed within the next few weeks.
Average value in a big lode, over 2oz. to the ton, and a
big mine established bevond doubt. Shares doubled in
value in less than a month. Market valuation to-cla,y
upwards of a million, and likely to go much higher. This
co. has, in addition, 23,276 shares in the Sons of G walla
mine, W.A., which is making from £ll,OOO to £12,000 a
month profit. After a very bad time the firm of Bewick,
Moreing and Co., the founders, are now having a good
one in W.A. Whether they have really earned it is quite
another matter.
Fraser’s*Gold Mine, Southern Cross, W.A., is
taken over by the Australian Gold Recovery Co., at a
price which will pay 10s. per share, and the new owners
will put up £30,000 for machinery and mining improve-
ments. The tailings-heap was the great inducement for
purchase. It averages just under 4dwts. per ton and
that should mean about half-profit, even in so dry a camp.

SOCIETY
Nobody seems to have noticed the resem-
blance of the “ Breelong Blacks” case to one
imagined years ago by Mark Twain. He supposed
a white elephant escaped in the U.S., with the
police after it. Every day it passed through some
village, doing much damage or little. Everyone
had seen it except the detectives. Finally, Bar-
num made a large offer for the privilege of paint-
ing his advts. on its side “ until the police find it.”
It ultimately wandered unseen into the detective
office, went down the cellar and died. The ’tecs,
only found it a month afterwards, when the odour
of six tons of very bad elephant became so
obvious that even the force couldn’t overlook it.
When this was written the satire seemed exagger-
ated and severe. It doesn’t now.
* * * »
It is more than probable that Jacky
I nderwood—recently death-sentenced over the
Breelong slaughter—acted under fear of being
himself murdered by the masterful Jimmy
Governor. The real facts of the case will pro-
bably never be known, but it almost looks as if
the man the police caught will have, in some sense,
to suffer for the men they can’t get hold of.
Re the outlawing of the Free-long blacks.
The summons commands them to appear, etc.,
“ under penalties mentioned in the Felons’
Apprehension Act.” Much Jimmy and Joe
know of the terms of an Act of the very existence
of which the Government itself was ignorant till
a few weeks ago ! Why not say at once “under
pain of being shot at sight ?”
Dear Bulletin. —Re N.S.W. Education-
Minister’s alleged intention (on account of the
Breelong affair) to withdraw all female teachers
from outlying districts. A recent Govt. Gazette
advt. invites applications from persons (male and
female) desirous of being appointed to charge of
small schools in isolated parts of the State. — Fair
H.

The Melbourne Saturnalia is connection
with the visit of the Duke of Edinburgh in ’6B
Was something like Mafeking-night continued for
several weeks. When Micnie, Q.C., afterwards

• * * »
“The engraving we give of the Duke of
York is in the simple and unpretentious uniform
of a captain in the Royal Navy,” said Melb.
Paunch, the other week. It is a melancholy fact
that the illustrated Jingoistic press, the press
which talks eternally of Britannia Ruling the
Waves, and refers to the gallant Boys in Blue as
the Empire’s most particular glory, and so forth,
can actually offer a sort of apology for publishing
the photo, of a prince in an “ unpretentious ”
R.N. uniform which he is only about half-qualified
to wear. The Duke of York knows that the rank
of Navy captain is several sizes too large for his
actual nautical self; the ordinarily “loyal” pub-
lic knows it; even among the 0.1. p, there is a
pretty general feeling of contempt for the policy
that confers unmerited distinctions upon the acci-
dent of birth. A prince is entitled to princely
“orders ” —ribbons, stars, &c. —as long as he is an
item in the monarchial system. They are
“ honors ” only in the sense that a Crown of State
is an honor to its hereditary wearer who doesn’t
pretend to have won it by personal effort. Yet
the Paunches of this world have the impudence
to suggest that the Duke of York is unpreten-
tiously attired when he masquerades as a first-
class mariner and a responsible fighting man.
* * * »
N.S. Wales’s most garish M.P., Dan’l
O’Connor, recently asked in N.S. W. Assembly if
Lord Roberts had been invited to attend the
Federal celebrations, and when Arthur Griffith
inquired whether Paul Kruger had also been in-
vited Dan’l said that Griffith had “an ignorant
and vulgar mind.” A good many people will
probably think that Dan’l’s mind is also of the
vulgar order. Roberts is a weary old man of
nearly 70 years, who doesn’t advertise and hates
display. To be brought out here in the hot
weather, and dragged about from one common-
place entertainment to another, and made to
swelter in public places while thousands gazed
and gaped and yelled at him, and taken to weary
banquets where tawdry, yawping, blaring Daniels
made dreadful speeches at him by the hour about
Demosthenes and William Charliss Wintworth,
and Daniel Hinry Deniehy—to be blighted by the
glare of Reid’s eye-glass, and to be introduced to
hundreds of perspiring nobodies—think of the
misery of it! Also think of the cruelty of it!
Roberts doesn’t want to see our Daniel! The
grand old warrior is doubtless the lion of the hour,
but that animal had one Daniel thrown into his
den already and he probably doesn’t desire any
more. The man who has lived in the limelight
for 20 years isn’t likely to yearn after the penny-
candle glare of D. O’Connor; and Roberts has
heard so many languages that even Mr. O’Con-
nor’s perfectly new variety of “Frinch” would
hardly interest him. Kruger might be invited.
His political day is over, and if he perished in the
seventh hour of Daniel’s great speech he would
leave no perceptible gap. But to expose a valuable
man like Roberts to the brassy boom of our fatal
O’Connor would be grievous folly. If Roberts
does come, the bearded Tribune should be exiled
to Thursday Island till the visitor is safely away
again.

Maoriland Jingo Govt, has perpetrated a
weird mistake. It made great and costly pre-
parations for the Declaration of Peace, and voted
a large sum of money, never dropping to the fact
that there can be no formal making of peace, be-
cause there is no Boer Government to make it
with. The Boer country having been annexed
the war must just flicker out, and it can have no
definite ending. Chamberlain has now explained
this fact, and the M.L. Ministry, finding itself
with a perfectly useless celebration on hand, has
decided, by way of a lame and impotent conclusion,
to let off its fireworks on the Prince of Wales’

Birthday instead. It is to be hoped it won’t
repeat the dreadful mistake of previous Jingo
celebrations, when the insulting Dutch red-white-
and-blue flag was hung out all over its cities.
Half the people either actually didn’t know the
British banner and hung out the enemy’s flag by
mistake, or else one half the community was play-
ing a horrible game on the other half’s ignorance.

declaration of peace is possible, owing to the fact

will greatly interfere with the proposed rejoicings
already elaborately prepared for in several quar-
ters besides Maoriland. At the Military Offices
in Sydney is stored a huge assortment of gas-
devices, piping, &c., intended for use when the
declaration was made. The unsightly scaffolding
by the Queen’s Statue, erected at Mafeking time
for a bandstand, can now be removed.

* * * *
The Medical Bill which has passed both
N.S.W. Houses is mainly a Medical Titles Bill, a
Bill which surgeon Fortescue advocated in Sydney
over a quarter of a century ago, and which would
have been law long ago had not the local medical
fraternity overreached itself. In one respect the
present Bill, as passed, creates an anomally, inas-
much as it acknowledges foreign diplomas gained
after three years’ study, while insisting on five
years’ study for a N.S.W. diploma.

Concerning death-under-chloroform :
Ed. Bulletin,—Your correspondent “ Layman ” writes :
“ More than 50 years have elapsed since chloroform first
came into general use, and doctors have not yet learned
to administer it to patients with safety. The number of
deaths under the anaesthetic has greatly increased durin«-
the last few years in Australia.” This is hardly fair to
the doctors. “Layman” forgets to mention the extra-
ordinary increase of late years in cases where an anres-
thetic is administered. Antiseptic surgery has proved so
successful that the fashionable surgeon is to-day a bigger
man than the physician, and surgery without anaesthetics
b , a ? be „ come incomprehensible. The administrations of
e blorofor ™ must total much more than 100 per week in
major and minor, performed in the Sydney hospitals
during 1899 only one case that resulted fatally can be
attributed to the anaesthetic, and in that case death was
9 ue fco . ar ? obsf;l / re condition of heart-disease practically
impossible to detect pre-mortem. Accidents, of course,
happen occasionally, and sometimes an inexperienced
medico may make a mistake ; but it is a sweeping asser-
tion to say that because a few patients die on the
operating-table, doctors have not learned, etc. Of course
£ octor . s are not infallible, and never will be. Driving a
ho^ s ® ls a s ™pler business than administering chloroform,
and the world has been learning it for some thousands of
y ears longer. Yet the cart-practitioner hasn’t vet
learned to drive a horse with safety. Nobody has
learned to do anything with absolute safety,
*-* * *
The split in N.S.W. medical ranks, caused
by the onset of the A.N. A., seems widening. The
right has the doctor who attends an Oddfellows’
lodge (without income restrictions) to cry “ Black
sheep” to the doctor who attends an A.N A
lodge (without income restrictions)? At present
the medical dog’s in an illogical manger. By the
way, the Sydney A.N. A. has got hold of a doctor
who, in a short letter to the daily press, t’other
day, twice alluded to himself as “a medical
gentleman.”

A nurse, who had done some months of
hard work among Sydney plague patients, was
enjoying a well-earned rest witli her relations at a
remote Vic. township, when her experience made
her suspicious of the state of a little girl in the
house, and she advised calling in a doctor. The
medicine-man, after examination of the small
patient, called in sundry professional brethren
with the result that the case was found to be one
of veritable plague. The important point is that
ltis hereby seemingly demonstrated that Death
may be carried on the clothes of “contacts” with-
out themselves developing the malady ; and this
despite disinfection and fumigation, for it is to be
supposed that the nurse in question (who is
deemed to have transported the germs from
Sydney) underwent both processes before leaving
that city,

THE POLICE ARE IN PURSUIT.
Oh, hard on the trail of the Breelong blacks
The police are in pursuit,
Thro’ ti-tree swamps, o’er mountain tracks,
They stick to the niggers like cobbler’s wax—
The police who’re in pursuit.
If they strike the remains of a smouldering fire,
The police who’re in pursuit
Will stop with a caution we must admire,
From the nearest station then quickly wire—
“ The police are in pursuit.”
The Breelong blacks have been most unkind
To the police who’re in pursuit.
They’ve called them asses, and left behind
Insulting notes for. the traps to find—
The police who’re in pursuit.
Some day, perhaps, those blacks will die,
The coppers will follow suit,
And the wand’ring swaggie with startled eye
Will see their ghosts come hurtling by,
The police in full pursuit.
JING LE.

a decrease in the numbers of both Houses, and a
diminution in importance of the local legislature
in the immediate future, this outlay seems, to put
it mildly, unnecessary. Surely members are elected
to work, not to play !
• * * *
A quack-doctor named Fitzgerald, who
recently toured N.S.W. and lectured and sold his
obscene book, was stopped in Queensland soon
after crossing the border, and fined for selling
indecent literature. His stock of books was also
confiscated. Rather rough on N.S.W. !
The Indecent Advertisements Act lately
passed by N.S.W. Parliament will not hit home
for some time. The obscene traders, anticipating
the carrying of the Bill, flooded the country, as
far as directories enabled, with leaflets on their
precious practices, and an appended note to apply
to X., Box so-and-so, for further particulars. The
Govt, must obtain power to supervise the G.P.O.
boxes before the intention of Parliament can be
adequately realised.
m • * •
Every holiday the railway officials on the
Illawarra (N.S.W.) line curse the short-sighted
policy of the past, which only saw the necessity
for a single line of rails over Como bridge. The
delays caused to trains both on holidays and ordi-
nary days must have figured, within the last 10
years, twice over what duplication of the bridge
would have cost.
-Parson Gladstone, of Nathalia (Vic.),
backed out most ingloriously from his undertaking
to name a long list of local females who have been
“ ruined ”by dancing since ’93. When the Sun-
day came for Gladstone to put his unholy threat
into execution he stated that he had never meant
to do it. He had meant to say that lie knew of a
number of ruins which he “ain’t a-goin’ to tell.”
So Nathalia, which was lately said to be maddened
b/ the prospect of Gladstone’s scandalous allega-
tions, is now feeling more wild than ever because
he didn’t allegate at all. It serves Gladstone
r'ght for talking of matters which he knows com-
paratively little about. He is notin the confidence
of the local dancing community. Now, if he were
to start an open Confessional for the purpose of
revealing the names of maidens who have gone
aitray at Sunday-school picnics Gladstone’s minis-
try might he a good paying concern. Picnic !
The very sound of the word has an immoral ring
in it. Think of the hundr eds of almost despairing
spinsters who have joyfully met their fate ere
returning home in the van with the author of
their “ s-shame !” Here the paragraph dissolves
into tears.

A Melbourne suburban resident who had
fought the subtle bottle-ho-man for years,
and had been beaten badly every
time, recently derived an idea
from reading how the nigs of Cen-
tral Africa trap big game. He
accordingly duga trench 6ft. deep,
filled it with kitchen offal and
slush, covered it with laths, and
over these spread earth and grass.
Then he stacked a heap of bo'.ties
in a corner, and left the back
gate open. In the early morn-
ing he arose and watched.
Bottle man arrived, looked over
fence, sighted the boodle, entered
to attach it, and went plug i.ito
the hole. Householder calmly
approached. “Hulloa ! what’s
you doing there?” “Help me
out, for Gawd’s sake ! ” said the
bottle-man, “me bloomin’ leg is
broke.” And it was, and house-
holder had to scrape his enemy
down, send him to hospital in a
cab, and “part” £lO damages.
And now if anybody mentions
the fact that an Englishman’s
house is his castle the suburban resident calls
him a liar, and says that an Englishman’s house
belongs to the bottle-man.

* * * *
Woman’s everlasting search for a man
under the bed was rewarded in a Melbourne
suburb one night last week, when a “married
lady,” whose name is rigorously suppressed, took
the usual precautionary peep, and discovered the
man, who was lying low in the expected place.
The result was a series of piercing shrieks, and a
wild struggle in the darkness, the masculine in-
truder having extinguished the candle in his initial
rush. A lady friend reposing in the next room
spread the alarm by rushing into the street,
attired in a blanket, and uttering irrelevant re-
marks in a loud voice. Three men and a constable
entered the house, not quite sure what had hajJ-
pened, fire, murder, ghosts, burglary, or an ex-
plosion—and found the “married lady” still
struggling with the wretch from under the bed.
They were restrained from interfering by reason of
the lady’s admission that the marauder was her
own husband, from whom she had been parted for
two years, and who had resorted to strategy with
a view to the assertion of his privileges as a hus-
band and a man.
* * * *
“Confession,” according to the Bishop of
Ely, “is certainly lawful in the Anglican Church,
as also the burning of incense—if not ceremonially
used.” Anglican clergymen, it is added, are not
bound to obey the Thirty-Nine Articles—which
are merely statements of doctrine. What a howl-
ing this will raise in the Evangelical camp !
THE DEBUTANTE.
FLUNKEY : “ Miss Horsetralia!”
How do the Mafeking sham-siege shriekers
feel now? “I could liave slipped away at any
time with my garrison,” declares Barnum-Powell
per cable ; “all that I did was to divert a few
Boers from Natal.” And a siege where the be-
sieged can escape at any time is hardly worth cal-
ling a siege at all. Brand Mafeking bogus and
bury it in oblivion as quickly as possible, ye Jingo
donkeys!
* * * •
The proposal to establish a Sydney Univer-
sity or Technical College bursary in memory of the
late Henry Parkes is languishing for want of
Government subsidy. N.S.W., in fact, is only
liberal through its Treasury. It is public-spirited
only out of the public pocket, and though it takes
a lively official interest in the memory of Parkes,
it isn’t going to part up any of its private cash
over a mere matter of sentiment. Thumping sub-
scriptions for any object indeed in N.S.W. nearly
always spell simple advt., and when anything of
the sort is proffered without published name of
giver, it’s safe to bet that the anonymity will be
short-lived. Rumor is sure to credit the “ munifi-,
cence ” to the wrong man, whereupon, “injustice
to himself,” the blushing donor is fain to peep
from behind his veil. Tell us—when was there in
N.S.W. a really anonymous charitable gift of large
amount ?

In response to a persistent demand,
Maoriland Parliament has at last legalised mar-
riage with a deceased husband’s brother. Maori-
laud is one of the very few places on earth where
the old man is such a success that his wife is
inclined to try another from the same family—or
else it is one of the very few places where a lady
can stand her brother-in-law’s inspection. It can
be taken both ways.

children.

• * * *
A Bulletin correspondent:—
Re Sydney University Library. Before any Parliamen-
tary Committee recommends any expenditure for a new
University Library it should thoroughly inspect the pre-
sent library and accommodation, which is ample for the
next 10 or 15 years at least. Sydney U. Library and
branch rooms are three times the size of Melbourne ditto,
yet want of room is complained of. Why not remove all
useless volumes—then there would be ample space for
requirements, if there is not already sufficient. It would
be a scandalous waste of money to build another Uni-
versity Library, when dozens of productive public works
are urgently required. Further, the “ Varsity ” Library
is only used by about 300 people—mainly students and
professors, the public not being permitted to enter same.
Also, why do the “ Varsity ” authorities waste money on
expensive works and then let same go astray ? At present
there are over 1000 vols. out, some having been borrowed
over 10 years ago—yet none are ever written for. As
Govt, grants a large annual subsidy, it should see that
dishonest persons are not allowed to deliberately retain
expensive books. Let a new University Library wait
until it is really wanted.
* * * *
Afc the annual Melbourne Diocesan Festi-
val, Lieut.-G-ov. Chief Justice Madden, who pre-
sided, gave a layman’s views of the reasons for the
diminution in devotion to the Church. “He
asked, where was the old devout clergyman,
simple-minded man, single of purpose, full of
faith and devotion, such a man as was a friend of
the household, and into whose ears the head of
the family poured all his griefs with as much
sacredness as to the Almighty ? Such a man was
never met with nowadays. He seemed to be
swallowed up in the psychological, sociological,
political, physical, controversial clergyman. Yet,
after all, if they minded not these things, and
preached only Christ’s simple, loving message, a
humble and contrite heart, they would do much to
bring back the loyalty and devotion of which he
had spoken.” Where has that good old clergy-
man gone? Well, he has gone to keep company
with the nice, fat, silver-haired, pink-cheeked,
honest old family solicitor, three generations of
whose family had never, never, never had a single
penny short in their trust-accounts.

Old (iron)St. Stephen’s Church, Macquarie-
st., Sydney, which until lately served as the home
of the lending branch of Sydney Free Public
Library, is again in ecclesiastical use. After being
pulled down it was re-erected at Itookwood
Asylum, and now serves as a place of worship for
the old men inmates.

“D.J.” offers a suggestion :
Sydney has reached a critical epoch in its history. In
all probability the course of events during the next is
months or so will decide for many years if it is to continue
a conglomeration of hovels and dog-boxes, or become tne
great gem city of the South Pacific. There are few cities
in the world with such natural advantages, and it lsdouot-
ful if there is any white man’s city built in the last two
centuries where indifference and want of taste have clone
so much to neutralise those advantages. The streets are
narrow, and stumble about at all angles. The houses are
badly built, and you can find miles of them without a
trace of beauty in either design, construction or surround-
ings, so far as these latter are artificial. The result is
that a large section of the population is growing up with
warped tastes, and prefers to live in dog-boxes, and unless
reform comes quickly, the future Sydneyite, or an
ever-increasing portion of him, will be an object bvmg 111
the kennel by preference. The upheaval of the bubonic
plague led to the destruction of the City Council clique
responsible for this state of affairs. It roused the public
to a sense of its danger, and the vacillating Premier was
moved for once to play the stat small in a sort of a way.
The spasm of reform commenced to (lie as soon as it began,
butere it went right under, it brought about the resump-
tion of some awful slums and signed the death-warrant
of the Council aforementioned. Now, all that is re-
quired is a good, strong leader with energy and imagi-
nation who has some idea of how to make a
city beautiful and healthy. The new franchise
will be a splendid weapon forged ready to his hand. A
strong City Council could soon move the Ministry to
submit the question of referring the creation of a Greater
Sydney Council, with powers analogous to those of the
London County Council, to a referendum. But at pre-
sent there is no leader in sight. In the interests
of quick reform it is very desirable that Loyd
Beauchamp should stay 7 in the State and devote his
municipal experience, his unquestionable taste and
talents, to the great work of re-constructing the city. It
is a great opportunity, and Beauchamp may not have
such another in three life-times. Why not offer him a
special engagement as Mayor at £5OOO a year ? By a few
years’ devotion to the work he loves he can leave behind
him a monument of fame in the shape of a beautified and
re-constructed metropolis. He would succeed as a
reform leader where another would fail. Opposition
would simply wither away ; the inborn tendency of aider-
men to grovel would make them follow his lead, and he
has had experience in an up-to-date British municipal
government, and has some idea where to lead. This idea
would actually make grovel a useful thing. Think of
that!

“ M. Turlutu ” to The Bulletin :
There is a slight error in the statement that the Vic.
detective Mercer owed his life to the turning of a bullet
by a regulation pocket-book. As a matter of fact, he
really owed it to the careless loading of the thief’s pistol,
a 32-cal. cartridge being used in a (probably) 34 or 36-cal.
“ squirt.” The track of the ball was a curious one.
Striking him in the throat, just below the chin, it slipped
round the neck under the skin, and was extracted from
just over the apex of the left lung ! Bibs often turn the
course of a ball. Years ago writer saw a man who had
been struck by a heavy revolver bullet, fired from a few
paces distance, just over the heart region. The bullet
passed out close to the spine on the left side, apparently
traversing chest and heart. The man was much col-
lapsed and both wounds bled pretty freely, but in a week
he was about again. The ball had slipped round a rib
and had never entered the chest cavity at all. Again,
saw a Kanaka who had attempted to carve a white man
with a cane-knife. Latter pulled a ‘450 Colt, hitting the
nigger plumb in the centre of the forehead. The man
fell senseless, but lived to serve a term of gaol. The
bullet, having been deflected by the hard frontal bones,
had slipped along under the scalp to the back of the head,
and to the end of his life that Kanaka will haye an almost
perfect “ parting,” the hair-roots having perished along
the track so formed.

“F. 0.” : The wholly outrageous “ boots ”
Eicture on French post-cards appears to
ave originated in an etching by that unclean
master of black and white, James Gilray. His
collected caricatures, in 2 vols., political, and mis-
cellaneous, for long adorned a shelf in Melb.
Public Library, but the officials of that institution
have grown sadly moral of late, and the miscel-
laneous volume can now only be seen on special
application. Even then it is mostly “out.”
Seems as though members of theY.M.C.A. and
Purity League go religiously for the Gilray book
to keep it from the gaze of the sinful multitude.

Miss Sutherland, the outspoken Melb.
philanthropist, in a fierce mood, talking of the
youngsters at her Children’s Home: “Let me
tell you how much the Church cares for the
children. My children have been going to the
Presbyterian Church for six years, and not a
single person has spoken a word to them during
all that time.” Suffer little children —provided
they are strictly within the law, of course.
