A WOMANS LETTER.
Mr Dear Mm- S?d ” r ’ ° cloW! ’’ 1000 -
As Commonwealth Day approaches, and the
nrst of January looms hotly near, Sydney begins
to realise that the art of making thoroughly joyful
in wide, loud, comprehensive and exhaustless
national numbers is not the simple agreeable thing
it sounds. lo work up all this hundredweight of
joy requires an innumerable string of committees
—committees of artists and architects to decorate
trie city ; committees of theatrical and amusement-
making men to titillate the public mind by night
as well as by day ; a committee fifty strong to set
the above going ; and so on until there is scarcely
a public man in the place who has not got to go
on some committee or other so as to be thoroughly
amused when the Commonwealth comes along.
Of course, when the great day arrives, all mis-
givings will disappear, and the historical Great
Burst will roll on greased wheels, and we'll all be
joytuJ in a spontaneous and irresistible way that
will emanate from our heart’s core, and steal out-
wards through our eyes and lips and pockets. If
anyone on earth knows how to take his pleasures
madly !t « the Australian. Which recalls that
last Bight-Hours Day no less than 10,000 people
tluTriy 1 - Wn "^ a^ to go picnicking on
But yet, looking ahead three months to a time
when we have to be merry for a week, the awful
(or, perhaps I should say, merciful) limitations of
the human animal rise up and refuse to be denied.
As a poor millionaire with a lavishness becoming
m a poor millionaire once put it: “No matter
what s in your stables you can only ride one horse
at a time. And no matter what the occasion we
can only make a limited amount of noise. There-
fore, there are several light-hearted people who
feel an anticipatory clamminess at the thought of
the required Pre-eminence in—well, after all
perhaps it will not be necessary to change the
historic word.
I lie swearing-in of the Governor-General takes
place on January Ist. You will be glad to hear
that it mat) happen in the Centennial Park—
Parkes Park. Could there be a more graceful
setting ? I pray fervently against the narrow Quay
where thousands crush, and only a few hundreds
see anything. But great, broad Centennial Park,
with the trees of Parkes casting slight shades over
its young slopes—young, a little crude, but typi-
cally Australian, and trying bravely to grow' up
and be a blessing to the city—surely that is the
place above all others where Federated Australia
might most fittingly begin its first toddle.
Among the doings spoken of are free theatres
all over the city, a Great Art Exhibition, and a
Horticultural Show. While on the subject, what
a chance to rejuvenate our Gardens and Domain
m the Continental fashion! Music and little
tables Or should I put it, little tables and
music? We want them so badly. We are so tired
of our long summer evenings, the Gardens locked
up, and no chance of hearing music except in
sweltering halls under bright, brutal lights that
effectually dissipate nine-tenths of the artistic
joy music can transmit sometimes. Surely among
all the innumerable committees there is someone
who will give us our open-air music and little
tables, and bless us in a way that will bless him
some day.
Speaking of music, our Amateur Orchestral
Society would scarcely overstep the limits of
truth if it now dropped the first epithet and
leaned wholly and hard on the second. The
houses become larger and larger, and the Amateur
becomes less and less obvious. Witness the fine
performance of Tachaikowsky’s Symphonic Pathe-
tique, last Thursday. The A.O.S. played this
sonata last year ; and played it with added affec-
tion the second time. On hearing it again, the
great soul of the Russian loomed through all four
movements, Russian beyond disguise. To me
this work is full of Steppes and snow, the illimit-
able patience of the Russian nation, its profound
sufferings, and, above all, its future, with its
mighty promise, lschaikowsky, as a composer, is
a long way after Beethoven, whose breadth he
emulates, but he soars high as a philosopher,
in spite of the comments on the A.O S pro-
gramme that still persists in alluding to one chord
as the fateful gong, ’ this work breathes out the
highest metaphysics, and leaves one with the
ardent desire to hear it again soon.
• Mis s Fanny Bauer was the vocalist of the even-
mg. She wore a pale blue silk frock, and was
well decorated with floral tributes. Her first
contribution, “ Piano Piano,” (Weber) proved too
piano for the Town Hall, but the sweetness of the
voung singers clear, light soprano tones had a
better opportunity in “ Mia Piccirilla ” later on.
By the way, Miss Bauer’s facial expressions while
singing detract considerably from her natural
charm. To appear to do a thing easily-that’s
one of the fundamental secrets of everything great
and a soprano who warbles as if her notes were
teeth being extracted should not, in kindness, be
allowed to go unwarned.
Socially the great event of last week was the
Harris-Salmon wedding. The lively, dark-haired
brides popularity had a high tribute paid it last
Wednesday, when the streets near St. Stephen’s
yhurch were lined with dense crowds, who burst
into cheers as the bride drove through with her
bridesmaids. As a wedding, this was a great
success, even to the onlookers. The church went
into a wealth of white wedding-bells. A special
programme, printed in silver on shiny paper be-
u -j he tedlou ? time in the vestry, and while
the bride was writing herself Nance Salmon for
the first time, Sydney Hoban played the organ-
accompaniment to his new “ Wedding Song ”
the words of which were written by Mr. Edwin
Brady and sung by Miss Aarons in a pale grey
suit. From the organ-loft also wafted songs by
Kate Rooney and Henry Weir, and from the
closed doors of the vestry wafted—but no, I can-
not say wafted in this connection. Let me say
rather, rattled the notes of one of the most famous
sounds in Australasia, the laugh of the best man.
In fact, that laugh was the dominant note of
that wedding. At the reception in the Town
Hall afterwards people gathered in little groups
to gaze at L' Homme Qai Rit. Everything said
about that laugh is true ; moreover, everything
has not yet been said. Every time I heard it I
started, thinking a table and a lot of glasses had
fallen over and smashed. Then came the long ha-
ha-has. Indignant at being deluded, everyone
said at once : “ Mr. Peacock again !” As it is a
public property—but an asset to its owner, I am
sure—l feel justified in giving you a minute de-
scription of the famous sound. First a loud—in-
conceivably loud—report. An instant’s silence ;
then a series of shouts, uttered almost as if in
pain. All the afternoon, all the evening, that
laugh permoated the Town Hall, and the bravery
of the average bridegroom was multiplied over and
over by the courage of Dr. Carty Salmon (who,
by the way, is tall, fair-moustached, and inclined
to be stout) in choosing a laugh and W. J. Pea-
cock to give him so loudly away on his wedding-
day.
n *' e la y° r Mayoress certainly did things
well.” At the afternoon reception refresh-
ments were delightfully served, and the bride
and bridegroom shook hands in a white bower
leading up to a magnificent display of
wedding presents. The gifts, by the way, were
guarded by ’tecs, and included everything, from
the wedding-cake given by Mr. and Mrs. Baumann
to an abnormal display of fish-knives and carvers
—out of compliment to the bride’s new cognomen,
possibly. One never knows what insanity prompts
the burden of fish-knives and butter-dishes that
weigh down the early years of married life, and
the above sounds as senseless a reason as any
other.
Atthewedding, Mayoress Harris was f rocked in a
becoming pale-pink brocade, with a toque trimmed
with dull green and purple grapes and leaves, and
a showy bouquet of roses. At night, the Mayoral
Bill filled the hall with low frocks, the Mayoress
wearing grey brocade and a fresh bouquet.
Since my first mention of it, I have had time to
thoroughly examine the “Twentieth Century
Cookery Book,” and now feel justified in highly
recommending it. The compiler, “Thalia,’
is a Sydney pen-woman, who has made a study of
the House, house-keeping, house-decoration, and
cookery, and tiie only fault I can find with her
book —which departs from the ordinary class of
recipe and has troubled to find some attractive
novelties—is that the chapters on Home Decora-
tions are all too brief. Mrs. Panton, who wrote
“From Garret to Basement,” has treated the sub-
ject exhaustively for English homes, but condi-
tions are so altered in Australia that a special set
of ideas is necessary here, and these will be found
in “ Thalia’s ” book. Mr. Souter supplies some
vivid illustrations.
Mrs. Bland Holt’s last At Home in Sydney, for
a year, filled Needs Rooms on Friday with a
mixed society and theatrical crowd. The vivacious
hostess received her crush in a pretty grey frock
with turquoise ornaments. The “What Hap-
pened to Jones ” Co. were largely in evidence, —
Boy Denton with the excellent stage laugh,
“ Professor ” Sharp, in black clothes, dainty little
Dot Frederic, in black, and Mrs. Thomas, —Agnes
Knight,—also wearing black. I was glad to
have an opportunity of hearing little Violette, a
fair-haired child with an oldish face, play her
mandolin.
Mrs. Holt’s “At Homes” have been quite a
feature of the season, the only disappointment to
local “ perfesh’’-hunters being the absence of a
certain fair-haired Star whom everyone is dying to
meet personally, but who is never to be met except
on tive-three-and-one terms. •
Madame Adelina de Lara’s concert had been
preceded by so much talk that I was not surprised
to find the Centenary Hall well filled last night.
In the street, Mrs. Kingston looks a carelessly-
garbed young woman, with a swarthy skin. On
the platform, in an evening gown, she transforms
into a lovely Spanish - looking creature, with
glorious shoulders, melting black eyes, and a
highly artistic ensemble. She wore at her concert
a dead white satin and a rich lace polonaise, one
red rose at her waist, another in her black hair
dressed low on her neck. A long string of big pearls
hung loosely round her throat, and 1 never saw a
piano-player so fascinate a house with her personal
attractions. Even Mark Hambourg’s mesmeric ugli-
ness paled before Mdme. de Lara’s dark charms.
The galleries stood up and craned. And the
house resolved itself into a series of opera glasses,
steadily levelled whenever that bewitching
Spanish vision appeared.
As a poem, as a picture, the pianiste was an
immense success. As a pianiste? Well, she is a
pupil of Clara Schumann, and has a London repu-
tation. All the same, she lacks sensation and
virility, and lacks, in a marked degree, the
capacity of losing herself in her work. Perhaps
she is too much of the pretty woman, and too
conscious of it. Her black eyes wandered
over her frock, over the house, here,
there and everywhere. In the Kreutzer Sonata
she was quite ‘outside,’ playing mechanically,
with an eye on her patrons. Now and then there
were hints of a warm temperament. Her touch
is delicate and springy. In a concerto by Arensky,
a Russian, she played with more spirit and
abandon than in her solos, and Stael and Volmar
lent valuable aid with fiddle and ’cello. My im-
pression of the new player is that she could give
a much better account of herself. The concert
was finished off by Miss Jessie King in an orange
frock, under two lace overskirts, with touches of
brown on her corsage. Miss King warbled four
ballads with matured art and delightfully clear
utterance.
I would like to draw your attention to our
Varsity’s production of an Ibsen play, “ The
Enemy of the People,” at the Standard Theatre
next Thursday, Oct. 18th.
Also, I must ask you to admire the balance-
sheet of our Poster Ball. It cleared £2020 7s. Id.
Every ticket was accounted for. Not a single
ruffle disturbed the abnormal harmony of the
working committee. Expenses were kept at the
low figure of £224, which included the rent of the
Town Hall. Those directly responsible for this
happy result were Miss Eadith Walker, Miss
Martin, and Mr. St. Vincent Welch. They had
perhaps learnt a thing or two from the unhappy
recent experiences of a certain unselfish but com-
mercially-untutored worker in a similar field.
A well-known homoeopath who died last week
was Dr. Benjamin Simmons, of Alberto Terrace.
He was a dreamy old gentleman, very popular
among his patients. His (second) wife is well-
known in musical circles, and a son of liis is a
well-known Sydney medico.
I have been thinking over modern jewellery,
and have come to the conclusion that it suffers
from a deadly monotony. What do you think?
The same old bauds and shields of gold, with the
regulation number of red, white and blue stones
gleaming, according to price. Certainly the opal
is a thing of beauty for ever; with its delightful
variety of fire it reminds one of miniature sun-
sets. But even miniature sunsets want a little
variety of setting. Our brooches are the everlast-
ing doves set with pearls ; or hearts gemmed with
sparks of diamonds and opals or set with rubies.
But hearts of gold—it was ever so—don't appeal
to one; they conjure up stories from the
dissecting-rooms, and call to mind the dreary
lectures on physiology at school, and 1 hail
with joy signs that a good time is
coming, and that in the near future designing
jewellers will pay good prices for suggestions for
tasteful and original jewellery. Paris, of course,
is responsible. In Paris, one Rene Lalique has
brought about a revolution. The whole of the
jewellery in the gay capital is being recast. His
designs are marvellously artistic. He has now
attained such fame that he is altogether occupied
executing orders for kings, emperors, millionaires
and other councillors of the earth. Naturally, no
one who is not a millionaire can lay hands on his
beautiful creations, but a vast school of imitators
is springing up, and in a few months we may
begin to see the work of some of these imitators
in Sydney and Melbourne. And then
For instance, what do you think of this for a
brooch ? A big dragon-fly, natural size. Its body
is made of three amethysts, cunningly jointed
together, the outspread wings are a marvellous
filagree of iridium, blackened silver, and cloisonne
of opal, amethyst, and chrysoprase; they are
jointed to the body with little gold springs, so ad-
justed that, pinned on the breast, with every
respiration the beautiful creation lifts its wings
and undulates its body. Oh, Ouida ! Is the chance
too late for you ? A comb for the backhair is an
ivory nymph clinging to a tree of brown horn,
looking, with a terrified expression, down from
the dizzy heights of her lady’s kopje. A beautiful
locket is a profile of ivory, surrounded by a cloud
of hair made of blackened silver, that streams out
as though floating on water over a spray of red
coral, from the end of which gleams a tiny emerald
that gives the whole creation an atmosphere of the
sea. This is supposed to represent Sappho beneath
the waves. Lockets are made of tiny landscapes
of gold and enamels, and flowers are formed from
all manner of rare and beautiful stones and
enamels. At present, this wonderful Rene is
engaged in designing and building a new Im-
perial diadem for the Czarina of Russia, and has
refused all other work till that is finished.

“ The old story ! Before sailing with the onety. j
oneth contingent, a smart young officer introduced
his sobbing girl to his best chum. ‘ Look after
her,’ he implored brokenly, ‘she’s a tender-hearted
little woman.’ Then the two men shook hands
warmly, silently, in the close grip of friendship,
and parted with the best of intentions. The tear-
ful girl was tenderly escorted back to town, and
Tommy’s chum began his campaign of kindness.
He hugged her by proxy, kissed her as deputy,
and embraced her under power of attorney.
Result, a brisk engagement.

The opera-people, it seems, did not see the
Arcadia on fire. Joseph Tapley was the most
popular spirit. All the others dressed, talked,
and acted like so many members of a suburban
needlework-society, and their co-passengers, who
had prepared to be enlivened or shocked, or both,
were completely taken aback. At last they came
to the conclusion that the ‘ stars were trying the
rest-cure.
“ For the average woman, the prevalent dress-
notions seem to be skirt, tucked downwards,
bodice tucked on the cross, chiffon boa, under-
sleeves of muslin or chiffon displayed by * bell-
sleeve ’ and tulle-heaped hat. Some people wear
two chiffon boas, white and black respectively,
tied at the rear-waist by tiny ribbons. Pink coral
and amber are in favor for trinkets.
“A Victorian backblocks Anglican parson tells
this story:—He has a hall attached to nis church,
and he was asked to allow the place to be used
once a week for a dance during the winter. He
saw no harm in the lads and lasses having a hop,
so he consented. But the Wesleyans denounced
him from the pulpit in scathing terms. Dancing
was unholy, said they, and it excited the passions
unduly. After a while, however, so many of the
young folks went to the C. of E. in consequence
of the weekly dance that the Wesleyans got
alarmed, and determined to start a rival institu-
tion in their hall. They wouldn’t encourage the
ungodly and exciting sport of dancing, but, on
the suggestion of a lay preacher, they hit upon an
alternative amusement. So, during the last
winter, once a week at the Wesleyan Hall, kiss-
in-the-ring and hunt-the-slipper were played.
‘ What do you think,’ asks this paper, ‘of kiss-
in-the-ring as a more godly exercise than
dancing ? ’
Yours affectionately, Gouli-Gouli.

' On Monday evening last a neat little
“send-off” supper was given at Power’s Hotel,
Sydney, to J. A. Philp, well-known journalist
and frequent contributor to The Bulletin. Mr.
Philp, who goes to take up a position on the
Brisbane Courier, was entertained by a gathering
which, though naturally chiefly composed of
brother ink-slingers, embraced enougli repre-
sentatives of other walks in life to show the esteem
entertained all round for a straight-goer and real
good fellow—the general opinion about whom was
as hereunder happily voiced and versed by Victor
J. Daley:—
“FAREWELL, PHILP.”
Read at the A. J. Philp send-off, Oct. 8, 1900.
We march along the road of life,
And each his own small tune is humming,
But could we hear a merrv fife
Sound clear above the dull humdrumming—
Would we not wish the fifer gay
To march with us upon our way ?
The road is rugged at its best,
A dusty road, and long, and weary;
But if a man with quip and jest,
Should make us almost think it cheery—
" owld we not try to keep that man
A member of our caravan ?
Behold our guest, at sight of whom
Black care runs headlong to the forest.
His countenance with smiles abloom
Is like the window of a florist.
We want to keep him, but— eheu !
They want him up in Queensland, too.
Alas ! When on the road we find—
As we for sympathy are yearnin'* —
A comrade to our heart and mindr
He leaves us at some sudden turning.
We drink his health to hide our grief
And wave a hurried handkerchief.
Farewell, Jim Philp ! Our hearts to-night
Mould blither be to give you greeting
Mayhap m Melville’s “ Land of Light ” g
We all shall have a braver meeting.
We wish you, with a cheer and sigh—
Good health, good friend, good luck, good bye !
Victor J. Dalev.

recent sectarian events.

While collecting the electoral-rolls in a
N.S.W. country district t’other day a man con-
fessed to the policemen that he entertained a
desire to become a voter, and asked the date of
next election. He had, he said, a second name,

“Ananias ! ”
Seeing that Jimmy and Joe Governor are
as much white as black, why shouldn’t the Euro-
pean half of them get some of the credit for their
crimes? In common fairness they should be
called “the Breelong whites” quite as often as
“the Breelong blacks.” Why not use the two
terms turn-about ?
The present Mayor of Sydney, though a
University graduate, often addresses his council
as “yous.” There are points about the word
“ yous.” When you look towards two people and
say “you,” you may really be addressing only one
of the pair. But when you say “yous,” there is
no doubt about the inclusion of both your auditors.
Julian Ralph complacently observes :
Of gallantry, as we Anglo-Saxons know and appreciate
it, the Boers have not a trace.
Just as well for the Boers, if Anglo-Saxon gal-
lantry is consistent with lying about women as
Ralph did about Mrs. Cronje. The American
posing as an Englishman was ever a spectacle for
gods and men.

valuable by-and-bye.

Characteristic story told in Canada of the
late Duke of Edinburgh. A flunkey in some
excursion made the pointed remark: “I had the
honor to act as guide Prince of
Wales over this path, and he presented me with
this magnificent gold watch.” “Ah, indeed!”
replied the close-fisted Duke, “that is more than
he ever did for me.”
The report that Princess Henry of Pless
was the author of that charming book, “Elizabeth
and her German Garden,” is now contradicted,
but London Chronicle announces as a verified
fact that the authoress is “ The Countess Arnim,
an Australian, married to the son of the famous
ambassador.” But who is the said Australian
lady, and what part of Australia did she hail
from ?
Labor member Trenwith (Vic.) is now a
cockatoo squatter. He holds an estate in the
Mallee, of over 1200 acres, and tells in the House
about “ my experience ” in the cultivation of the
soil. Up to now this has not shaken his belief in
the rightness of the land nationalisation theory.
“We’ll start by nationalising your own land,”
say his squatter friends. “I’m i-eady,” answers
Trenwith ; “ let’s do it together.”
Sydney’s old acquaintance, Gilbert Parker,
the novelist, author of “When Valmond came to
Pontiac,” lias been elected to the House of Com-
mons for Gravesend division. When Parker first
came to Sydney he joined the staff of S.M.
Herald under the name and style of the Rev.
Horatio Gilbert Parker. When he left Australia
he went to Yankeeland and married the gilded
daughter of a millionaire trader in Japanese
curios.
Mr. “ Bob ” Pennefather, Attorney-
General of Westralia, is mentioned for the vacant
Justiceship in that State. Mr. Pennefather,
whose bar-call at Melbourne dates 1873, is in the
East best known as the counsel who defended
Cordini, before Judge Windeyer, for the murder
of a hawker near Deniliquin; and who, after the
conviction, addressed the press strenuously ex-
pressing his belief in the man’s innocence. Cor-
dini died denying the crime.
Touching Tas. Premier Lewis’ suggestion
that the Australias should unite to send a cablegram
to Chamberlain praying for the success of the
Tory party at the British general elections, in
order that the results of the Imperial S’African
victories might bear full fruit, what would Pre-
mier Lewis say if the Imperial authorities, during
the progress of a general election in Tasmania,
advised the voters of that somnolent island to
stick to Braddon and drop Lewis ?
Somebody’s London correspondent appears
convinced that Malcolm McEacharn (who has
Federal Parliamentary ambitions and longs for an
organ of his very own) really means to start a new
evening paper in Melb. The Bulletin presumes
that S’Malcolm has heard the story of the man
who sold his soul to the Devil on condition that
the latter would manage a daily paper for him ;
the end of the thing being that after trying the
billet for a week the Devil gave back the soul he
had bought and cried off his bargain.
Old Hurtle Fisher, once owner of Mari-
byrnong estate and stud, and long one of Vic-
toria’s foremost and straightest sportsmen, ap-
plied recently for the license of an obscure South
Melbourne hotel. He imported the great Fisher-
man, and bred and raced Fishhook, Seagull,
Angler, Lantern, Charm and Sylvia, among others
But the rose-and-black has not been seen on the
turf for many a day. The land boom dissipated
most of Fisher’s pile, and now the old sport sells
beer where he once drank fizz.
The self-satisfied “ Seppy ” Stephen's
resignation of his seat in N.S.W. Leg. Council
will not seriously impoverish the legislative power
of that Chamber. Mr. Stephen’s enthusiasms
were not with politics. Like most of the Stephen
family, he believed in official as opposed to demo-
cratic rule. A few months back, in a lecture he
delivered in London, he reiterated his old belief
that N.S.W. obtained Responsible Govt, at too
early a date—“fully 20 years too soon,” lie
solemnly informed Lord Jersey and others at the
Colonial Institute.

tralian official fancied his long-looked-
for title within measurable distance
when, in reply to his latest message ot
sympathy or something to the
he received a reply signed Vic-
toria.” Previously his greasy con-
dolences and sich had been acknow-

through the local Governor, and the
message from “Victoria"’ caused an
appreciable shortening in the odds
about the official's chances of an im-
perial bauble. The cable, in a large

cumber the walls of his office.
But it has lately been whispered

tralian racing man, now in Lon-
don, hearing of the condolatory
message sent by thetitle-hunter,
thought to perpetrate a joke by
wiring a bogus reply “from the
Queen.” Anyhow, the framed
cable message has disappeared

Lady Randolph Churchill seems to be
much gone on her new husband, who is somewhat
younger than her son, Winston, or perhaps it is
that latter has been snorting at the union. Any-
how, she took the trouble to announce, through
the London papers, that her name is now Mrs.
George Cornwallis West.
Should Chief-Justice Way be translated to
an Imperial Court of Appeal, it is likely that
Senior-Judge Boucaut will get his place on the
local S.A. Supreme Court Bench, and that Insol-
vency-Commissioner Russell will fill the other
vacancy. Attorney-General Gordon hopes other-
wise, but it would be a public scandal if Russell
were passed over, for he acted as Judge during
the absence on leave first of Way and then of
Bundey, and he filled the position excellently.
Reported that Henry Lawson has “ settled
permanently ” in England, and has made a good
beginning with sales to Blackwood’s Magazine
and elsewhere. The Bulletin sincerely hopes
that he will maintain his standard of work, and
not drift out of Australian recollection like others
who have gone before him. In no English-speak-
ing country is there a keener reading public than
in Australia ; and the unanimity with which they
drop an old favorite, when his work deteriorates,
is amazing.
Purtell, the Melbourne bard, who gets a
living by having his “ poems ” hawked round the
street at a penny a time, is not, as might be sup-
posed, an elderly crank, but a young man of, say,
30 years, who wears beautifully-barbered love-
locks, an exquisite moustache, a magpie tie,
irreproachable linen, and a most impressive
boutonniere. Purtell has cold-bloodedly adopted
“ poetry ” as a profession—has organised the dis-
tribution of his works on the milk-walk principle.
He has just “dreamt ” Cornquist as a winner of
Caulfield Cup, and has set forth his vision in a
penny rhyme.
Broughton Chapel, about to be transferred
bodily from Liverpool, N.S.W., to the Theo-
logical College at Newtown (Sydney), was built
over 40 years ago as a memorial to Australia’s first
Anglican bishop. The Duke of Wellington picked
up Broughton in the first instance, giving him a
curacy in England and afterwards the arch-
deaconry of N.S.W. Bishop Bioughton’s son
died in Sydney, but he himself died in the house
of ex-Gov. Gipps in England and was buried in
Canterbury Cathedral. Broughton spent his
early life as a Govt, clerk in the Treasury in
London, and all through his career exhibited a
good deal of the manner and style of such officials
at that date. He died at 70 years of age, and the
chapel now in question was erected to his memory
by voluntary subscriptions.
“ The washing of the blood-spangled
khaki,” writes The Bulletin’s London corres-
pondent, “has already been begun, with the
world looking on. Discredited General Warren,
immediately on arrival here, blamed Buller firstly
and Thorneycroft secondly for the Spion Kop
disaster —which really was a first-class muddle.
T.’s clique took his part and retorted, and now
Warren asserts he ‘ never said a word ’ —for pub-
lication. They never do, these ‘ stood-down ’
Generals. There is a hot time preparing for
Buller, despite the smearing of glory his recent
efforts have brought him, and it’s only * that
circus-man,’ 8.-P., French and one or two other
colonial leaders whose linen is not yet khaki-
colored. One grand, good thing, ‘ Bobs ’ as
C.-in-C. means the War Office’s requiem.”
Maoriland Premier Seddon is far from
being the man he was. Things are being left
more and more to Ward, who seemingly looks
after as many departments as Seddon used to, and
works at the same terrific rate. Seddon, too, has
apparently lost some of his backbone. His tariff
proposals were completely revolutionised by the
“Boxers,” as the malcontents were termed, and
he did not complain ; and now he has introduced
his Technical Education Bill without providing
assistance for the R.C. schools, although last year
he withdrew it rather than pass it in its emascu-
lated condition. No one will be surprised if, at
the close of the session, he does finally decide to
take the place of W. P. Reeves in London, where
lie believes, that his howling Jingo policy, as em-
bodied in his last-man-last-shilling offer to “the
Empire” will secure him a vociferous greeting.

Aboriginalities.
Dr. Ahearne’s theories re the influence of
Northern climatic conditions got a nasty knock
t’other day from the neighborhood of Trinity Bay.
Wrote a woman to Q. Lands Department :
If you would come up and see what women do on a
selection you would not be against women taking up land.
Two women I know have fallen several acres of scrub.
One woman cleared several acres. I saw a woman and
her 14-year-old daughter harrowing coffee three weeks
ago, and another woman, a widow with six children,
sows corn, plants potatoes, keeps coffee clean, and the
other day killed her own pig. Some of the men about
here lie about reading all day and leave women to look
after the land.
And this comes from Cairns, the most sweltering,
oppressive hole in N. Queensland, the city of
Oriental preference, the Paradise of the cheap-
and-nasty alien, the town of the Yellow Back-
bone ! Apparently, the virility that has oozed
out of the soul of the Cairns white man has gone
to stiffen the other sex—in some cases, anyhow.
A woman who could buekle-to and fell several
acres of Cairns scrub is a heroine who should
rank with Boadicea and Joan of Arc. The writer
of above letter got the concessions she clamored
for—and one feels glad she did. All the same,
would not that other grafter who “killed her
own pig ” be a heavy responsibility as a life-long
partner ? She mightn’t stop at that.

44 Puy.” : Chatting with a travelling
photographer (a well-educated German) the talk
turned on certain Australian peculiarities.
44 These beeples are very strange,” he said. “Last
week I struck a small town und did goot biz.
Just as I go to pack up and make for the hotel a
small girl come running up to me in great ways.
4 Will you come and take mother’s portrait, she is
dying,’ she say. I say, 4 Why does she her
bicture taken want that she is dying, what is it
that she is dying of ? ’ 4 She has an abscess on the
heart ’ says the liddle girl 4 and an abscess on the
liver and inflammation and a lot of oder tings and
doctor says she will not live till to-morrow.’
‘Very well,’ I say, ‘I will then come.’ So I go
and there is a woman, sick, very sick, and I take
two goot plates and go away to the hotel. Next
morning joost as I finish developing my plates dot
liddle girl coom running oop to me again.
‘Mother says will you show her a broof,’ she
says. 4 The doctor says she will not but a little
while live and she vant to see a broof before
that she is dead. ’ So I say very well, and make a
rough broof and take it along to the sick woman.
4 It is not a goot picture at all, ’ she whisper when
I show it her; 4 it make my gomplexion look all
muddy.’ Then she tell me I must take another in
brofile this time—and I could see she vas joost
about to die, but Igo to get my gamera. Before
I got back she was dead, und ven I ask the
• hoosband for the money he say 4 O go to blazes !
she has enough money cost me already,’ and trow
the picture in the fire. They are a funny people
these Australians.”

“T. Mac ” : Where did Gov. Phillip bury
his horse (B. 6/10/’00) ? This probably refers to an
old Sydney-schoolboy lark. In the Domain, over-
looking the Harbour, is a very steep grassy incline,
any spot at the foot of which is pointed out to the
“green’un ”as the last resting-place of the gee-
gee in question. At the same time he receives
a sudden shove behind which either sends him on
his nose or compels him to rush top-speed down
the slope in order to avoid falling.
* • • *
Again the mosquito. An important recent
scientific discovery is that the parasite causing
elephantiasis is a habitant of the proboscis of the
mosquito. Aforesaid parasite is a small worm
which lives in the lymphatic vessels and occludes
them. At this rate there’ll soon be a big boom in
mosquito-curtain.

Journalese in Bunbury, W.A. :
When the streets of the town away from the main
streets were seas of mud, and when the infant moon had
not the ghost of a show of casting the feeblest ray through
the umbrageous canopy which vaulted the heavens, not
a single municipal lamp was lighted. The writer urges
that the lamps should be lighted longer in winter than in
summer, so as to give the baby Luna time to grow strong
enough to light the dark places.
* *. * *
In wayback news-offices where the editor-
Eroprietor is also comp, and galley-hand, pub-
shmg night is a time of fear and trembling until
the marvellous sheet, with its “literals” and
turned letters, is rattled off at the rate of 250 an
hour. Thus does Avoca Free Press vent its
indignation because a local meeting to discuss the
commonage question was convened for the even-
ing of going to press :
To hold it at a time when our limited staff is otherwise
engaged was a paltry trick, and one which is but too
frequently indulged in on occasions demanding fair com-
ment in the public interests. In this instance, no matter
what the inconvenience may be, our reporter, at least,
will be there, if for no other reason than to watch the
manoeuvres of the President-elect in his efforts to gauge
public opinion and sail with the strongest crowd.
has neither natural nor acquired ability to deal with what,
in some respects, may be called an abstruse question. He
has hitherto relied on his gift of low cunning to humbug
those who at one time thought he was fairly capable of
doing efficient work, but he has been weighed in the
balance and found wanting.
• * * *
“Tomahawk ”: N.S.W. Big Scrub timber-
getters have often to scaffold a big tree to down
him, leaving a stump of perhaps 20ft. high. The
Moreton Bay figs and spurway trees have spurs at
the base which render it impossible to fall them
stump-high. The faller, therefore, cuts steps up
the spurs until he reaches the barrel of the tree,
in whioh a notch like a mortice-hole is cut 10 or
12in. deep, and the platform is inserted. This
consists of a board about sft. by Bin., by 2in. thick,
having a horseshoe-shaped piece of iron bolted on
to one end. The iron has in the bow a clip, turn-
ing upwards, which bites into the top of the notch
mentioned, while the bottom of the board rests on
the bottom of the notch. When the faller mounts
his weight keeps the platform in position. I have
seen a man step from his platform on to the stump
of a scrub-giant to which he had given the coup-
de-grace, and calmly watch the monster’s crashing
downfall. Nerve, eh !
“Ne’er-do-Weel”:
The mild-looking little
angel again. Camped
on the Condamine a
short time ago, with
some bullockies and
horse-teamsters. There
were two teams very
heavily loaded with
wool—top tiers over-
hanging the lower. One
bulloeky had his wife
with him, a quiet-look-
ing little woman, but
when a dispute arose
over some trifle and
words ran high, the
lady took a leading part
in the proceedings, and
finished by suddenly
grabbing a tomahawk,
jumping on the pole of
the adversary’s wag-
gon and cutting the
belt-ropes, so that down
toppled the bales —her
opponent just clearing
in time. Her husband
calmly sat on his heel,
smoking. 11 Ain’t she a
holy terror?” he said.
“ But it’ll all be over in
a minute, mates —take
no notice of her.”
“That’s all blanky
fine,” said the owner
of the other waggon,
“but look at my load 1
why don’t yer take a
spreader and lay her
out?” “For God’s
sake, don’t talk like
that,’’said the husband,
excited for the first
time, “or she’ll stiffen
the whole blanky lot of
us !”

Re intestinal worms in
wallabies (“ Wat T., ”
B. 4/B’/00). Such worms
are quite common in all
the Maeropodce, hares
and rabbits being also
much infested with
them. ’Possums and
native bears, however,
are, in my experience,
very free from such
parasites, though all our

our eels and many spe-

Clancy, the cattle-man, struck the pub.
and asked a “stool-pigeon” that was in the bar
to have a drink. Then they had another, and
another, and the stool-pigeon bore up every time,
but when the fifth drink came on, cattle-
education came to his rescue. He had a “Jimmy
Woodser,” and remarked to the stool-pigeon,
“ Old man, I’m going to wean you.”
* * * •
“ Rito ” : Re savages reverting to their
original instincts. About 30 years ago a backblox
squatter lost his way, and, coming to the junction
of three tracks, did not know which to follow. A
dirty aboriginal in a filthy blanket sauntered out
of the scrub at that moment. 4 4 Which fella for
Coonema ” (the squatter’s station) asked the man
of wool. The blackfellow struck an attitude and
replied with dignity : “ In medio tutissimus ibis
—(the middle course is the safest.) The querist
nearly fell off his horse with fright, thinking he
had “got ’em”; but the explanation was simple
enough. The dusky denizen of the forest had
been educated at a Sydney college, and had gladly
returned to his native lair.

Dear Bulletin. —“P.H.C.” (B. 25/8/’00)
is absurdly wrong in stating that “the dingo was
brought to Australia by the Dravidians of
Southern India.” Fossil remains of the dingo,
discovered about 10 years ago and exhaustively dis-
cussed by the late Prof. M‘Coy, prove the animal
to be strictly indigenous. For further con-
firmation of this fact let “P.H.C.” refer to J.
Douglas Ogilby’s “Mammals of Australia,” re-
viewed some years ago in The Bulletin by me.
— M'Cooey. [What about the possible fossil
Dravidian who brought the fossil dingo to Aus-
tralia ? —Ed. B.]
* • * *
H. J. M‘Cooey : Re “Yarrum ” on water-
lizards (B. 18/8/’00). It’s quite true that 44 Leseur’s
Lizard ” can’t climb down a vertical object; but
he can travel down any incline not greater than
25 deg. Upon level ground this reptile is the,
fastest of all our 200 species of lizards, running
upon the hind limbs only, with the body almost
erect and tail off the ground. Living or dead-and-
preserved specimens of this lizard (precisely,
Physignathus leseuri) are much desired by both
Sydney and Queensland Museums. Though, in
1890, I collected over 600 lizards, I never suc-
ceeded in getting a specimen of Leseur’s lizard,
notwithstanding that 1 had over a dozen shot-gun
negotiations with him. Is it possible to shoot
this reptile while “on the wing”? What saith
“Yarrum ” ?

“Quiet N.” : Was taking about 30 head
of cattle round the coast, near Cape Terawhiti
(M.L.), and, from the start, two big bullocks—a
red and a yellow—fought for the lead, there being
only a narrow track through the boulders and
heavy sand. Eventually, the red beast shoved
t’other off the track, whereupon the yellow got
sulky, and made a rush for the sea. Swain con-
siderable portion of the distance to be traversed,
which was four and a-half miles, and landed ahead
of the mob ; then coolly waited till they caught
up, and took the lead again. Never gave it up,
either, though his flanks and quarters were bleed-
ing when destination was reached. Near same
place, also, I was once with a team of provision-
laden pack-horses. One horse objected to making

like a lamb.

“F.” : The “manna” mentioned in 8.,
8/9/’OO, can be found at certain seasons beneath
the gums in Western Victoria. On the Barwon
(N.S.W.) a different kind of “manna” can some-
times be found on the leaves of box-trees; but
this seems to be the covering of an insect and is
much thinner and more leathery than the Vic.
substance.

“ F.H.” : Calcifer (N.Q.) had at one time
six private houses—and three
Now there are only three private
houses—but still three pubs. Two
constables are still stationed there
to keep order at the three pubs, and
to mind the three houses ; and though
the gentle “ two-up ” has been played
in the yards of these pubs, regularly
every Sunday for ages past, only one
arrest has been made within the
memory of man—when a big bettor
was found using a “grey” ; but even
he was shortly let go again without
serious trouble. And all this in the
face of Queensland’s sweep-abhorring
morality.

DearBuLLETiN. “Gobung’ (8.,
18/8/’00) says he “stumbled on” a goan-
na duel. Probably he did “stumble”
a good deal before coming across two
goannas, 6in. and 9in. respectively.
This district is fairly overrun with the
black and black-and-yellow iguana,
and yet I never saw one less than 15in.
“on his own.” Commonest length is
from 3 to 4ft., and as a matter of
fact the goanna must be nearly 6in.
long ere it sees daylight. As for the
9in. chap settling t’other in one act—
this is rot, for, like most Australians,
the iguana is a “hard die-er.” Even
after its head is chopped off it will
wriggle about and try to get it on
again! Also, unless “ Gobung ” hails
from a warm part it could not be very
“recently” that he witnessed this
deadly conflict, because the iguana’s
curfew rings when the cold weather
begins, and he has a snooze in a hollow tree until
Spring is fairly set in, before “settling on the
land ” again.— Wallan Dooly.

“G.”: Struck Innainineka Bill a few
hundred miles north of Adelaide. He was knock-
ing down a big cheque. Asked him why not go
to Adelaide and spend his money there on the tit-
bits of civilisation. “Adelaide be d d, and
many other things,” said 1.8. “They’d feed
me on cabbige and pertaters and things like
that down there. Me innards couldn’t stand up
to it. I’ve touched nothin’ in the way o’ vege-
tables but marsh-mallus for the last two year.”
“W.M.F.”: Black sheep may be “hardier
than white” (“Eller,” 8., 8/9/’00), but a black
sheep in a mob of white is almost always the last
sheep to fatten. No experienced person will
touch a “nigger” in picking “fats” for market.
A station near Walgett had, two years ago, bred
almost the whole flock black, and the wool brought
more per lb. than white wool of same quality and
condition, on account of requiring less dye and
consequently producing a stronger and more
lasting fabric.

Re massacres of blacks. Died last year
at Bonshaw, Dumaresq Riyer, Q., “Ashby .Tack,”
the only survivor of what was called the Sovereign
River Nigger-liunt. He was then a child, and
was saved by a squatter called Ashby. “ Ashby ”
went sometimes by the name of “ Mineral Jack,”
because he showed to Snider the Texas copper-
mine which led to the discovery of the Silver
Spur.
• * * *
The annual report of N.S. W. Aborigines
Protection Board for year ending 15th Oct., ’99,
shows that there were then 3203 full-bloods and 3689
half-castes; a total of 6892. Among full-bloods
there were 139 deaths and 79 births; half-castes,
161 births and 61 deaths. The real aboriginals
are gradually dwindling, as in ’B2 there were (>540 ;
now there are but 3203. On the other hand—go
down blackfellow, jump up whitefellow !—-the
half-castes are increasing ; there were 2379 in ’B2,
now there are 3689.

“ Mac.” : Re opossum “ naturally minus
the hind-legs” (“Index,” B. 4/8/’00). Could
“Index” state the sex, and were the genera-
tive organs deformed or normal ? Malfor-
mations, though very rare among Australian
mammals, are by no means unknown. Some
years ago, near Wombeyan Caves, I was shown a
dead native bear (p. cinereus) in which the two
hind legs were amalgamated into one very stout
limb. This was quite well-formed, and the foot
and claws, though exceptionally large, were per-
fect. The animal was an aged male, and had
evidently been geueratively efficient. This is the
only malformation I ever met with among Aus-
tralian mammals. Has “Index” or any other
Bulletin reader ever met with an hermaphro-
ditic Australian mammal ?
• * * *
“ J.T.1.” : To “P.” (B. 4/8/’00). Had a
dog once in Cook District, Q., which would wake
me during the night when lie was soruffing a
dingo, and could not kill. 1 used to go out with
revolver and light to assist, and usually found
dingo down and dog on top. At 2 o’clock one
morning I found a dingo down flat and apparently
dead, so called dog off. He came reluctantly a
little way, when off went the dingo again with
dog after him. Followed up and found dingo
spread out again. Thought to make sure this
time, so I stooped down, placed pistol muzzle
against the heart and fired. Result, dingo got up
in such a hurry that he hit me in the face and
knocked me flat on my back. When I arose I
found the dingo lying across my legs, and this
time he was real corpse.

According to Gabriel, Q. Inspector of
Distilleries, the following liquor adulterants are in
vogue in the North: —“Aniline dyes, sulphuric
acid, oil of vitriol, olive oil, and copperas. A wine-
maker of Bundaberg had doctored his wine with
aniline, logwood, copperas, and sulphuric acid.
While making tests at an hotel near Brisbane
Gabriel saw a publican pouring contents of a
vessel into another containing spirits. Some of
the liquid was spilt on the publican’s cuff and
burnt holes in it. Once he came across a cask
containing Blb. of solid matter to 11 gals, liquor.
The solids consisted of tobacco, cigars, cloves,
pineapple, fibre, and pieces of straw.”

Harry Stockdale on saddle-slipping by
bucking horses: —
“ H.G.” (B. 18/8/’00) says : “The bones of the horse’s
withers are 3in. higher than the remainder of the dorsal
vertebrae, and the projecting elbows are in the same line
with the withers, thus it seems impossible for girth to be
misplaced in manner stated. And what has belly to do
with girth ? ” Well, take any average healthy man and
note his power of expansion and contraction in stomach
and chest. But, as the two must act together—so it is
with the horse. Also, many horses are so devoid of
wither that it is hard to keep a saddle off the neck with-
out the animal bucking at all. Further, as all saddles
have a hollow space from pommel to crupper-D (some-
times 2in. high) this would assist an animal possessing
ordinary withers and make the feat of saddle-slipping
still easier to the horse possessing low or virtually no
withers. I can, in any case, assure “ H.G.” that it is by
no means only the low-withered animals that are capable
of this feat, which is, in my opinion, chiefly due to power
of muscular expansion and contraction. Wither is of
much less effect in keeping a saddle in position than
depth of brisket, between and a little back from the fore-
legs. Here, unlike the narrow shoulder-top, there is
great substance, and to this part girth and surcingle sit
tight, whereas the pommel of the saddle rides high and
clear of even the fairly-withered horse. It can, however,
be noticed when a horse is bucking, that the forward
position and rigidity of the front legs, coupled with the
position of neck and head, do reduce the size and alter
the angle of shoulder-resistance, the brisket resistance
being also affected.
* * * *
The dingoes are playing havoc in S.W.
Queensland. What the drought has spared in the
way of sheep is now become the prey of the wild
dog. Bransberry station reports losing 4000
sheep out of 5000 from dingo ravages in twelve
months, and other stations are in a bad state.
The drought killed off great numbers of rabbits,
and the wild dogs were driven consequently to
raid the more settled districts. When they have
luck in the West, they do have it—with a venge-
ance. A big part of the population of W. Queens-
land lives on tinned-dog just now.

“ Brew” : Horses will, in sand-fly season,
travel miles to get into the smoke of a
fire, but when no fire is available they
pair off, head tptail, and mutually dis-
pose of the fob. And—here’s the
point—a bob-tailed nag is always a
“ hatter,” a fly-bitten outcast who
makes overtures to every member
of the mob in turn, only to be re-
pulsed by ready heels. There’s
no silly golden-rul e business about
horses in fly-time.

H. J. Mac. : Re “Bushie’s"
statement (B. 18/8/’00) that 100
eggs were never found in the
nest of a brush turkey (Talle-
galla). Gould himself speaks
of finding over 200, and I once
found in the Bland district,
N.S.W., 122 eggs and 16
empty shells in one mound.
A Q. bakblox pro-
visional school-teacher who
was transferred still further
back, and at the same time
got notice of an increase in l .
salary of £5 for extra cost of
living —in acknowledging the
departmental bounty, stated that
“ his cup of joy was now full to the
brim.” The official censor severely re-
plied that hyperbolical metaphor was out of place
in official correspondence, and the teacher meekly
answered, disclaiming the use of either hyperbole
or metaphor, but pleading guilty to the employ-
ment of irony.

“EL”: Was recently in a Mt. Malcolm
(W.A.) pub. when in rushed a storm-beaten
wreck. “Been drinking three whole days !” he
exclaimed. “Nushing t’eat—give us breadun’-
cheese.” Having swallowed a mouthful, he hung
himself sideways on the counter and embracing
the company in a thick smile said, “ Did y’ear it
splash ? ”
• * • •
“Lar” : Once sent a nigger across to a
timber-clearer’s camp to borrow a cross-cut saw.
He forgot the name of the thing en route, but his
native wit served him. “ Say, boss, you lend ’em
my boss that big pfeller yan-and-come-back-
again—yan-and-come-back-again ? ” and he hissed
through his teeth —“that the which way, boss.”
He got the saw.
SHE DOUBTED HIM.
HE: “Darling! I worship the ground you tread on!”
SHE (squatter's widow): “ You forget, George, my ground ends at that fence; this is Crown land.”
Concerning a bush race-meeting :
“ H.G.” : Attended an outback race-meeting once, the
chief event of which, the “Quart-Pot Handicap,” was a
decided novelty. Prize, a half-guinea bridle ; distance,
twice round the course (about 5 miles) ; winner of gallop
to haul a ton weight once round immediately afterwards.
If first horse failed to do this, then second to have a try,
and so on ; successful hauler to be declared the winner,
even if last in the race itself. Entries, “ draughts ” and
wiry bush-hacks ; course, mostly up hill, thick with red
dust and dotted with stumps. The winner turned up in
the local butcher’s crock, which, however, after putting
the ton round the course, dropped dead from exhaustion.
I had my money on an old grey paek-ncddy that led to
within 10 yards of the winning-post (a tree), when the
rider of the butcher’s moke suddenly leant forward and
dealt my fancy a slash across the nose with the greenhide.
The grey lost bis stride and threw his head back, allow-
ing the other to “ pip ” him on the post by a few inches.
The rival jocks had a fight afterwards, but there was no
official inquiry, though the blood was streaming from the
unfortunate grey’s nose. Judge and stewards had backed
the winner and gave a verdict of “ two lengths.” The
only cash event was interesting, inasmuch as the club
pocketed three times the amount of the prize-money in
fines. The racing wound up with a spin in total darkn •■■■

Be the pukaki or pukeko :
Dear Bulletin. —Your contributor “ Pukaki’s” pen-
name should properly be spelt “ Pukeko,” meaning the
“ blue-bird ” or “ swamp-hen ” of Maoriland marshes. The
Union S.S. Co., however (which makes a point of giving
aboriginal names to its vessels), also uses the incorrect
form “ Pukaki.” The pukeko, by the way, is known in
parts of Australia as the “ baldcoot,” and there is reason
to believe that it crosses the Tasman Sea. Anyhow, it is
on record that one boarded a steamer 400 miles from the
Maoriland coast.— Kiwi.
There’s an essence of tobacco
In the land of Joe Kanaka,
There’s a swill that’s half of copperas and half of aniline ;
If you like your liquor placid,
There’s a blend of gentle acid
That would make a corpse remember what a drunk he
might have been.
It’s a lovely sort of lotion
Is the tropic pint o’ potion,
Where the sulphur juice is tempered with the dyes of
aniline;
It can buzz behind your forehead
Till you drop to regions torrid
In a condensation vivid of the benders that have been.

stamped. Name and address must appear on MS.

handed in ; and in no case will MS. be returned un-
less stamps (of any province) accompany.

Mrs. Fawcett-Story’s “Australian Eco-
nomic Cookery-Book” (price Is., published by
Kealy and Philip, George-street, Sydney) differs
from any manual of the kind The Bulletin has
hitherto struck in that it lays down as a pre-
liminary precept that “no collection of recipes
will supply the place of experience and observa-
tion in the production of a wholesome and palat-
able meal.” As far as recipes can go, however,
this little book is copious, up-to-date, and lucid in
its directions.
The Bulletin prints this circumstantial
indictment for what it may be worth, and sincerely
hopes it isn’t true: —
Sydney is admittedly dirty; Brisbane is far dirtier.
No hospital in Australia could possibly be in a worse sani-
tary condition than the General Hospital, Brisbane. Take
ward No. 15, and we find that it contains 20 beds, which
are nearly always occupied, that a side door opens into
the lavatory, which is also the pantry, and that this com-
bined pantry and lavatory, which is 8 x 10ft., opens into
a closet containing two pan-closets and a urinal. The
doors of the closet are always open, and the effluvia that
arise therefrom and pass through the pantry into the ward
are simply horrible —no words could convey any just idea
of the effect on a sick, nervous patient. More than this,
the combined pantry and lavatory is used by patients as a
smoking-room, and, consequently, the floor is always
covered with spittle. These are simple facts, told without
prejudice. The food supplied is plentiful, well-cooked,
and the very best obtainable. But Black Death sits in
that combined pantry and lavatory, and probably rejoices.

SIS: "Bother! What would you do, Son, wash

Announced by the British Govt, that per-
manently-disabled soldiers will receive life-
pensions from Is. 6d. per diem. Where does
officially “permanent disablement” begin. The
loss of a finger is a very permanent disablement,
as far as it goes, but the lopping of at least a leg
or an arm will be necessary to carry the pension in
question.

London, Thursday.—The Kaiser’s reply to the Chinese Imperial edict
has been warmly praised throughout Great Britain and the Continent.
The latest indications are that a fresh German proposal will be made.
The idea seems to be for the Ambassadors to inquire into the pro-
posed punishment of the instigators of the recent outrages, and superin-
tend its infliction, after which peace negotiations might be commenced.
Unless the punishment is carried out on Chinese lines it will not be understood,
and therefore have no effect.

POLITICAL
POINTS
The Victorian Assembly has decided that
a politician shall not be allowed to sit in the State
and Federal Parliaments simultaneously, and now
it is about “up to ” N.S.W. to follow suit.
The report of the N.S.W. Commissioner
concerning the site for a Federal capital is now
in Premier Lyne’s possession. The Premier in-
dicates that Bombala isn’t first favorite. A good
many people are guessing Blayney.
A Melbourne commercial traveller on the
Vic. House of Landlords
I have made a point of inquiring wherever I have
gone, and I find that, in nine cases out of ten, the people
in the country do not know the names of the men who
are supposed to be representing them in the Council.”
The Victorian Leg. Council rejected the
womanhood suffrage measure because Wrixon
told them that it would mean giving to women
to be elected to the Federal Houses.
Ihink of our wives being sent to sit beside us ! ”
gasped one legislator.
If Samuel Griffith wishes to enter the
Federal Parliament, let him come down from the
Bench and do so with the pluck of a patriot.
Apart from Queenslanders, he will be welcomed
by Australia. But if he hobbles into the arena,
leaning on the crutch of an unconstitutionally-
manoeuvred pension, he will be suspected and dis-
trusted from the start.
The N.S.W. Old Age Pension system is
again promised, but the fulfilment is postponed
till Ist July, 1901. Whether it will happen then
or not nobody knows, but the job of finding the
money is put off for another year, anyhow. It
isn t likely that Lyne will know any better how
find the money then than he does now, but
sufficient for the day is the evil thereof.
Reid’s dead- and - forgotten promise to
amalgamate the two N.S.W. Saving Banks is
again heard of. It will probably eventuate this
time. The parochial Lyne is horrified at the idea
of the N.S.W. P.O. Savings Bank being handed
over to Federal control along with the Post Office,
and proposes to make it a part of the Barrack-
street institution in order to avert that calamity.
In Victoria there are now over 50,000
women with votes at municipal elections. No anti-
suffragist proposes abolishing woman’s municipal
vote, because that vote is exercised mainly by the
female with Property. It is the horrid idea of
enfranchising the propertied and unpropeitied
female alike that makes the few grey hairs of the
Upper House staud up like the tail of the grat lied
cat.
THE REASON WHY.
IMPORTED LEAD: “Did you notice how tie
audience wept at that death-scene of mine ”
IINDIGENOUS COMEDY SUPPORT : “ Yes, they knew
you still lived!”
S.A. Leg. Council contains 13 Conserva-
tives and 11 Liberals, or, with Baker in the chair,
a dozen stiff-necked Tories. Yet, by means of
this majority of one cemented together by the
powerful cement of selfishness, the Party of Stagna-
tion sets the Assembly and the country, with its
150,000 Assembly electors, at defiance. Truly, a
new reading of the democratic principle of maja-
rity-rule.
The “Chinaman’s luck” of the Boodle-
wraith has again got it out of a very tight corner.
The report of the Royal Commission on Q. Works
is an awkward document, and now the Works
under-secretary dies suddenly, thereby making
most of the criticisms quite harmless ; for the man
who’s dead will got his full share of the blame,
and at least a little over. The Ministry’s luck is
simply appalling.
The Westi’alian Government proposes to
make it compulsory for every civil servant to
I '‘ s i u . vt j his life for a sum proportionate to his salary.
Which is good enough, save for one thing only : No
Govt, should compel a man to insure his life
unless it supplies a State-guaranteed office for him
to insure in. Compulsory State insurance should
he accompanied by a State guarantee that the
policy will be paid on maturity.
Some of Queensland’s new immigrants
from northern Europe are blamed by the kanakas
on farms and canebrakes with causing a reduction
in local wages. Many kanakas have levered up
the wages scale to about £1 per week, but the
new-chum immigrants, brought out by the Boodle-
wraith s hired agents and liars to what is de-
scribed as a Paradise, and then driven to cut-
wages through sheer hunger, are buckling-to at
15s.
The Referendum has come to stay. It is
the only sure method of getting at the popular
opinion on any one subject. The General Election
method tangles up 20 subjects and the personal
merits of 100 or 200 candidates together till no
coherent verdict is given about anything. The
latest proposed application of the Referendum
is m Westralia, where it is proposed to submit
the question of Payment of Members to the people
at the coming election.
An amusing phase of the Jingo develop-
ment is the Tasmanian Govt, proposal that the
Australian should endorse and jointly cable to
London a resolution of the Vandemonian Parlia-
ment, enthusiastically approving of Judas
Chamberlain and all his works—this to help the
lories at the British general elections. Just as
there is nothing so terrible as the rage of a sheep,
so there is nothing so ferocious as a Elyspeck when
it is once thoroughly aroused.
In obtaining the consent of N.S.W.
Assembly to the despatch of the China Contin-
gent Premier Lyne assured the House that as Great
Britain was bearing the cost the House need not
allow the question of money to influence it. Still,
a trifle of £15,000 appears on the estimates for ex-
penses in connection with the jaunt. As the
money has gone, Parliament can only protest at
the item. And even the mildest of protests will
come as a surprise from the present obsequious
House.
Times are changed with a vengeance when
the Irish Home-Rulers are looking to the Tories
for success. The casting of the Irish vote for the
Tory candidates in certain English constituencies
“is expected to increase the strength of that
party by at least one-third.” Expediency indeed
is the god of the Milesians if they vote for Cham-
berlain and Co., after Salisbury’s brutal avowal
that England’s experience of a Home-Ruling
1 ransvaal had confirmed her in her opinion as to
how Ireland ought to be treated.
The scheme for syndicating the Adelaide
tramways is causing much bitterness, and the
advocates of Nationalisation openly accuse poli-
ticians, press and municipal councillors of having
been bought with a price. The Councils in-
terested not only refuse to unite to municipalise
the trams, but they are casting all possible
obstacles in the way of their purchase by the
State. And Premier Holder does not seem very
anxious to push the Nationalisation scheme, which
finds its chief friends among the Labor-party.
South Australia gets out of a great diffi-
culty and saves much expense by making its State
one constituency for the election of members for
both Federal Houses. As a result, it will send in
members who will represent the interests of all the
country, while in the Federal Lower House
N.S.W. and Queensland and Victoria won’t be
represented at all—only the detached fragments
of them. The S.A. Representative will say, “ I
embody the will of the 370,000 people of S.A.”
And from the N.S. W. crowd will come the small
voice that says : “I speak for 50,000 people in
Pyrmont.”
If the little game in the interests of Sam
Griffith be carried out, and that worthy functionary
succeed in collaring a State pension of £1750 per
year plus £4OO Senatorial salary, it will then
remain to Bananaland to settle an immediate pen-
sion on Hugh Nelson, President of the Leg.
Council, so that his “ unrivalled experience in
legislation ” may also be at the disposal of Aus-
tralia. There are, besides, several other influential
citizens in Queensland of much public experience
and much soaring ambition who might likewise be
pensioned for a similar reason. Let us be logical,
and carry out the thing to the bitter end.
The Federal States of Australia now pay
£30,500 for Governors in direct salaries. In most,
extras are allowed. N.AW. grants £1374 extras
and Queensland £l3OO. S.A. and Westralia each
gives £4OOO salary, but the former no extras and
the latter several. A Governor-General on the
top of this, with £lO,OOO, shows Governors’
s daries alone amounting to over £40,000 a year.
Incidentals and extras added bring the item to
nearly £50,000 a year for ornamental head-pieces
for 4,000,000 of people. The necessity for cutting
down these State salaries is therefore obvious, and
the cutting down should be no timid paring.
Melbourne Argus, in its issue of 29th
Sept., made the wonderfully unusual statement,
re Victoria’s House of 48 Rich Landlords, that it
would “regard‘anyone who objected to a large
substantial reform of the Council as the worst
enemy of the community.” It is one of the signs
of the times that many Vic. Tories talk like that
now. But when it comes down to particulars,
they all draw the decayed whale of subterfuge
across the scent by advocating a great widening of
the Council’s franchise. And that will do no
good whatsoever. It is the absolute abolition of
the property-qualification for M’s.L.C. that - is -
wanted. So long as the people are only allowed
to select their M’s.L.C. from the same little hand-
ful of rich Tory property-owners, the Council
suffrage might be extended to every man, woman,
child, dog, and deadbeat in the land, and the
Upper House would remain the same as it has
always been.
Tasmania is struggling with
the periodical renewal of its
Electoral Act. By some strange
craze there is no permanent Elec-
toral Act in that island; the
statute merely gives the people
the right to elect M’s.P. in certain
ways for a certain number of
years, and if by some accident
Parliament dissolved, forgetting
to renew the Act, the whole
machine would presumably stop
until the British legislature
authorised Tasmania to start
afresh. There are great chances
for a democratic Assembly in
this system, if Tasmania ever
had such an Assembly. When
the Act is about expiring it
could introduce a new one on the
basis of adult suffrage for both
Houses, and throw on the Coun-
cil the responsibility of accepting
it or leaving Tasmania without a
Parliament—going back to chaos,
so to speak. The Upper House
has much property, and property
is apt to get lost in chaos, so it
would probably cave in ; but the
chance has always been missed
somehow.
Kingston, having con-
verted Svmon, Q.C., to a be-
lief in adult suffrage for the
S.A. Council, is now doing his
best with “Paddy” Glynn, M.P.,
who shows signs of grace. Glynn
has just told the Assembly that
there is not a Constitution in the
world under which the Second
Chamber so persistently and so
impudently thwarts the will of
the people as expressed by Refer-
endum and in the Assembly as
does S.A. Leg. Council.
No wonder, as telegrams
from the Northern Territory re-
port, the folks there are kick-
ing against the country being
made the plaything of specula-
tors ! Last year, according
to the Government Resident,
no less than 153,472 square miles of country
were applied for, of which, apparently,
not a single acre was stocked. One crowd—
the Lands Development Syndicate, Ltd. —acquired
119,860 square miles in various parts, paying
merely a few hundreds for the concession. The
Resident says “ there is little doubt these lands
have been acquired for speculative purposes.”
Apparently the jumps in the wool and stock
markets led to the huge grab.
Both the candidates for the Northern Ter-
ritory—the election taking place at the end of this
month—are solicitors. C. E. Herbert (who once
resided in Sydney) is a Soloinonite, and Mitchell
(who was formerly in partnership with Nesbit,
Q.C.) is a Kingstonian. Herbert has for many
years lived in Port Darwin, where personally he
is much and justly esteemed, but Mitchell has
never seen the constituency he seeks to represent,
which places him at a disadvantage. At the
Referendum in 1899, the Territory declared in
favor of Household Suffrage for the Council, and,
if it is consistent, it will vote for Mitchell now.
The S.A. electoral law provides that every
advt. relating to a man’s Parliamentary*candida-
ture shall be authorised by somebody whose name
and address must be printed below. But since
the advts. are mostly “authorised” by profes-
sional advertising-agents, the intention of that
part of the Act is defeated ; for it is just as im-
portant to know who authorised the agent as who
authorised the newspaper. For instance, in the
Kingston-Parsons’ Leg. Council duel —the warmest
the province has known for years—the principal
advts. on both sides, in some cases one answering
the other, were “ authorised ” by the same agent.
The Westralian Government has intro-
duced a Bill for the abolition of the meat duties,
yet in introducing it the Treasurer gave it as his
fixed opinion that it would not reduce the price
of meat in the least, and considering the ways of
the Perth Meat Ring this seems quite probable.
In any case, the meat duties have nearly abolished
themselves. Under their influence local supplies
have increased so rapidly that the duties on im-
ported meat dropped from £59,737 in 1897 to
£14,460 in 1899, and this year they promise to be
only about £BOOO. If it is a case, as the Treasurer
alleges, of abandoning the advantages of Protec-
tion, and losing at least a little revenue, and
gaining nothing in cheapness, then, by his own
showing, he is a fool of a Treasurer.

“A British schoolboy,’ it is printed,
has written from King’s Lynn, Norfolk, Eng., to
“the Premier, the first Federal Parliament,” c/o.
the Vic. Premier, suggesting Albury as the
site and “ Phillipsburg ” as the name of the

be chosen, and as to the name suggestion, the odds
are overwhelmingly against its adoption. “As
America honored her first great citizen, do like-

dignity to the great and coming nation of the

TONSORIAL ARTIST (rubbing customer's head): “Getting a
bit thin on top, sir?”
CUSTOMER: “Yes.” (Irritably.) “Goon.”
T.A.: “ Tried my patent chloroform hair-wash , sir?”
Customer (crushingly): “No! it wasn't that —it came out
on its own account.”
Washington was truly a great citizen—he wasn’t
the custodian of a moral dungheap, the keeper of
a quod. Tne U.S. didn’t name Washington after
its first British Governor.
Westralian Assembly, while cordially en-
dorsing the Conciliation and Arbitration Bill in a
general way, decided with emphasis that it should
riot apply to civil servants. This is a ridiculously
illogical attitude. The Forrest Govt, says that it
is fair and just to establish a tribunal with power
to compulsorily settle trade disputes, and that
both employers and employes should be subject
to its decisions. And it refuses to give itself (th©
biggest employer) and its servants (the biggest
mass of employes) the benefit of its own Act!
Does it know that its own methods as an employer
can’t stand investigation by its own court ? Or
doesn’t it want to prevent railway strikes, though
it is so anxious to abolish other strikes ? The
individual who advertises an article that should be
m every home, while he indignantly kicks it out
of his own, is an anomaly.
Already a Westralian M.L.A. has arisen to
propose that every member of the W. A. Parliament
which passed the Federal address to the Queen,
should have a free railway pass for life by way of
celebrating the event. The average politician has
strange ideas of celebration. The Westralian
people (largely disfranchised by the system which
gives a member to 42 electors and many cattle in
one place, and a member to 3,000 or 4,000 electors
in another) had to fight their pocket-borough Par-
liament for a year or more before they could get a
chance to express an opinion on the Federal
question. They had to kick their legislature
into passing the Enabling Bill. And now the
Parliament which resisted the people’s will to
the very last proposes that it should be specially
rewarded at the expense of the people on the
ground that it did the whole business !
Softgoods Sargood has evidently devoted
much of his valuable time to thinking out his
blessed scheme for putting all the Vic. State
schools under the immediate protection of th©
British Flag. He says, among many other wise
remarks on the subject—
An important point will be to ensure the simultaneous
hoisting of the flags. . . . It may be urged that this plan
can only be carried out in such localities as have telegraph
stations, but this difficulty can be easily overcome by a
pre-arranged chain of signals which could be seen at long
distances.
At the present moment, probably, Sargood is
seated with a damp towel round his head, ponder-
lng oyer the Imperial importance of so arranging:
the signal system that a great wave of infant
‘ loyalty ’ will sweep across Victoria at lightning
speed the moment the Duchess of York, in Mel-
bourne, presses a telegraphic button, or puts a
royal penny in the slot.
Education Minister Drake bids fair to be
remembered as the man who did the most of all
m the way of breaking down the secular side of
Queensland’s education system. Recently (conse-
quent on a Parliamentary motion) a notice was
gazetted to the effect that State School Scholar-
ships might be enjoyed in certain Catholic insti-
and at the present time candidates for
Govt. Scholarships from Catholic schools are pre-
senting themselves for examination. The Pro-
testant denominations, jealous of these advances,
are protesting more or less vigorously by deputa-
tion and otherwise. At the same time Protestants-
should remember that by agitating for the intro-
duction of Bible-reading into State Schools they
are giving the Catholics some justification for
their counter-action. Bible reading is another
name for practical Protestantism. If the State is
to subsidise (indirectly) the one cult it may as well
go the whole hog and subsidise the other —or all
th© others if need be. Honesty doesn’t dwell in
half-way houses.

The woman who recently, in Sydney,
poisoned herself with the phosphorus obtained
from'six boxes of vastas, selected for herself a
cruelly-painful ending. The Bulletin’s one-
time “Hints to Young Suicides” had practical
humanity behind them.
President Baker of S.A. Leg. Council was
thrown from his horse on Sep. 29, and so much
injured that he could not take the chair when the
House met again. Parties are so equal in the
propertied Chamber that no one from either side
would agree to temporarily fill the vacancy, so the
Council adjourned for a week to allow the
President to get well. If he had been killed the
Tories would probably have desired to adjourn for
ever as their ranks are dwindling.
S.A. manufacturers look upon it as
peculiar that although Premier Holder has for 13
years drawn all his political emoluments and
his Ministerial office which amounts to
years out of that period—from the Protectionist
party, he now announces that he will run the
Federal elections in the Freetrade interest. He
posed when elected for the Burra in ’B7 as
a Freetrader and a Land Nationalist, but
he has allowed both those aspects of his political
character to lie perdu ever since. Holder being
anxious to know which side his bread is buttered,
butters it on both sides.
Vic. R.C. Archbishop Carr raises the
same old protest against the new commission’s re-
port on religious instruction in State schools. To
adopt the report, and give the lessons recom-
mended, is, says the Archbishop, in effect, to pro-
nounce sentence of exclusion upon Catholic chil-
dren. And as the State schools are for the
whole community, Protestant and Catholic and
heathen, Vic. Govt, must leave religious instruc-
tion to the priests and parsons, whose business it
is. Up to now, the parsons seem to be too lazy
to attend to the business of teaching religion to
children ; so they seek to make the State do their
work.
Concerning the lost Cobar-Wilcannia rail-
way bill:—
Seldom has such a fight taken place in N.S.W. Leg.
Council as over the Cobar-Wilcannia Railway Bill.
Headed by Dangar and Want, M’s.L.C., the Tories, with
some Freetrade members, fought the Bill desperately.
The second reading was carried by 2 votes, and the Bill
only got through committee by the concession to the
opposition of £lOO,OOO off estimated cost. This amend-
ment led to a constitutional quarrel as to whether the
Council had the power to amend a money-bill. Having
got past this snag, the Bill came up for the third reading.
When the question was being put the Dangar party
quitted the chamber in a body, leaving the House
quorumless. This nearly led to a crisis, for Vice-President
Suttor tendered his resignation to the Govt. Things
were, however, patched up, and the third reading came
on again. Suttor, however, finding the Bill party in a
minority, postponed the notice on the business-paper.
Dangar and Co. had got all their side together and so,
naturally, stormed round the usually placid chamber.
But the anti-Bills could not go so far as to take the order
of business out of the hands of the Govt, representative,
so Suttor merely smiled and went on with the next item.
For four successive days did this happen. When the
motion was called Suttor postponed it. On the fifth day
he thought his chance had come and, postponing all
other business, he started to bring on the Railway Bill.
Want, Dangar and Millen vigorously objected to this at
sufficient length to enable their men to arrive. Suttor
had no option but to go to division, and lost the Bill by
24 votes to 22.
Dear Bulletin. —Kingston’s 271 majority
for the Central District seat in S.A. Leg. Council
was a great victory—not the small affair which
some correspondents would suggest. What King-
ston told these electors was, in effect, this: “ I
am the enemy of your exclusive power to elect a
Chamber which represents your interests only.
You are a privileged class. You have the same
voting power, so far as concerns the Assembly,
that other citizens have, and you have an extra
vote that they haven’t. I propose to reduce you
to the level of your fellows, and to destroy your
special privilege and monopoly. So far as it is
concerned lam here as your enemy.” And such
was Kingston’s influence that the monopolists
elected the enemy of their monopoly, and threw
the friend. Parsons, into outer darkness. Try and
imagine an uncompromising Protectionist carry-
ing a purely Calico Jimmy electorate. That was
what Kingston did.—J.
