Maoriland is trying its hand at a large
order. It has been decided to adopt penny post-
age all round—not only inside the province but
also with England—and in view thereof Post-
master-General Ward instructed an English artist
to plan out a design “ emblematical of Maoriland
diffusing the benefits of reduced postal rates
throughout the civilised world.” This seems a
pretty considerable lot to get on one postage
stamp, but P. M.G. Ward said the occasion was a
special one, Maorila’ud “ being the first colony in
the British dominions or in any other country to
go in for universal penny postage,” and the
emblem had to get on the stamp somehow, even if
it was a trifle crowded. Nobody will know, till
the first consignment of stamps arrives, what kind
of horror the province is committed to, and nobody
can guess what the emblem will be like ; it may be
a full-length portrait of Henniker Heaton in that
belltopper and frock-coat; or a naked Maori with
a Id. stamp on his back standing on the steps of
the Bank of England. Meanwhile Ceylon kicks
Maoriland firmly with a naked brown foot, and
points out that it adopted penny postage with
England long ago, and that its inland postal rate
is actually less than a penny. Therefore it says,
very reasonably, that Maoriland’s emblem *is
bunkum, and that it, not Maoiiland r diffused the
blessings of reduced postage. Therefore, if
P.G.M. Ward doesn’t feel foolish, he ought to.
The best thing now is to drop these stamps over-
board, and get up somediing as quickly as possible
with a scantily-clad brown girl on it.
A lady tells of life on board a German
liner :
Everything happens with a band. We dry our tears of
farewell to the tune of “ God Bless the Day,” and eat our
dinners to the “ Emperor's Hymn.” On Sunday our
songs of praise are accompanied by the brazen trumpets
of a German orchestra, and at any crisis whatever the
vessel is liable to break into melody. In the various
dishes sours and sweets are indiscriminately mixed to-
gether. Turkey is eaten with cranberry sauce and
plums. These latter pervade many dishes, and are
poured over your meat or into your soup, while
sour-cabbage goes with strawberry jam, and is
served last in the feast. We eat slowly, steadily, from
6 a.m. till midnight, no one article of food by any
possibility harmonising (until you get used to it) with the
one with which it is intermingled. In my own Australia
we have two breads, brown and white ; but here we have a
conglomeration of half-breeds, the genuine nigger black
gradually shading from every tint of brown and grey to
genuine white, each with the favorite taste of sour and
sweet. One dish that caught my fluttering appetite and
later ruined my reputation as an excellent sailor, was a
mixture of odds and ends of steak, green corn, haricot
beans, oil, butter, parsley, -garlic, and dried peas. There
is one child on board this vessel ; we know it for a
“ kind ” by the tucks in its dress, otherwise we should
think it a miniature woman, so frau-like is its aspect.
The tucked dress is meant to last eternally ; by means of
these reefs to be let out. The “ kind ” is as stolid as a
table. Its face is 20, but its tongue proclaims itself four
and a-half.
“ P. Malthus ” tells about a dog :
On a trip from Sydney 1 shared a deck cabin
with Brown. One of our fellow passengers was a
hard-faced woman of uncertain age. She had a pet pug,
an unhealthy-looking sore-eyed little beast. She had
somehow got round the skipper to allow her to keep it
on tne saloon deck, where she dossed it in an elaborate
box lined with pale blue silk, and furnished with a large
supply of wadding. At night it was secured to its box
with a strong silken cord. The box stood near our cabin,
and the first night it kept us awake for two hours with
its whining. Most likely it suffered from overfeeding.
Next night it started again, and its moans made our flesh
creep. “By G ,” said Brown, “ I’ll stand no more
of this. If it doesn’t shut up in ten minutes
I’ll deal with it.” And he did. Ten minutes
later there was a pug swimming in the Tasman Sea, and
peace. Early in the morning we were awakened by a
woman’s screams, mingled with a shower of blows on our
cabin door. I looked out, to find myself confronted by
the owner of the dog. She appeared mighty dangerous,
and was armed with a piece of stiff-looking rope. “Is
this the man?” she screamed to one of the crew who
stood behind her. “ No,” said he, it was the other
chap.” (It appears the sailor had witnessed the midnight
episode, awl'when tackled had given Brown away to save
his own skin.) “Inhuman wretch!” she screamed,
“ come out this instant, or 1 shall come in.” Brown went
out looking haughty and independent ; but he no sooner
saw the look in her eye than he made a dive for the
cabin. He just got in and locked the door in the nick of
time. It took us two more days to reach port, and
during that time Brown never stirred out of his cabin ex-
cept for a little while at night, when I assured him the
coast was clear. As we steamed up the harbor he said to
me : “ Look here, old-man, 1 want you to look after my
luggage. I must give that old virago the slip. lam
going to make a holt as soon as we touch the wharf. I
know a farmhouse a few miles out where I can hide until
you send me word that it’s safe to come back.” By dint
of good luck and agility he gave her the slip, and got
away. Next day I saw her in the street, and from the
look in her eye and the bit of rope she still carried, I
knew that she still wanted to see a man about a dog.
Chief Secretary See has not yet intimated
his intention of introducing a Bill to help the
authorities in making the well-to-do relatives of
the inmates of N.S.W. Govt, institutions assist
the State in supporting them. Sunday Brunker
intended to grapple with the delicate subject, but
the downfall of Reid prevented him. It is a
delicate subject. Not so long ago a N.S.W. legal
light made some caustic remarks from the Bench
on the fact that time and again he had seen orders
for the admission of persons into these places,
whose relatives, he knew, were well able to sup-
port them. Soon afterwards a brother of this
very legal light, a ne’er-do-weel whom he had lost
sight of for years, drifted into one of the institu-
tions, and, for all the trouble the 1.1. took, might
be there yet.
When General Knox was industriously
chasing De Wet over the veldt he one day blun-
dered up against the wily one’s homestead. His
horses were knocked up, his men were reeling
with fatigue, and the comparatively fresh Free
Staters were just disappearing over a ridge about
four miles distant into the gathering night.
General Knox was angry ; he flung a good hearty
curse at the vanishing Boer commander, and set
fire to the homestead to light him on his way. A
week later Knox was back in Kroonstad and re-
ceived a letter:
Dear Mr. Knox.—You burned my house last week.
Well, it was only worth £6OO, but it will cost you a mil-
lion before I have finished. Hoping to meet you soon.
Yours respectfully, Christian De Wet.
Up to September De Wet had run it up to about
half-a-million, and was still going strong.

Lord Roberts has scored another point in
the affections of the “ Tommies ” by giving them
the chance to draw “Kruger quids” instead of
British gold for their pay. The “Kruger quid”
will represent much more than its face value as a
curio if Tommy holds it awhile, so the teetotal
Commander-in-Chief practically advises the men
to keep sober and hang on to their coins of the
conquered realm.
Six months or so ago N.S.W. Govt., under
Parliamentary pressure, appointed a Board of
three high officials to report upon the subsidised
Agricultural Societies. The report came to hand
some weeks ago and, without any beating about
the bush, advised the Govt, to withdraw the
subsidies from all. The Govt., some members of
which are officials of such societies, announces
that it rejects the advice tendered.
The greatest curse of War is the poems
that are written about it. This, done by a
Masterton (Maoriland) citizen, and distributed
along with the bard’s photograph and a few
London steal-papers to the members of the second
M.L. contingent, is something extra:

tons for 41460 z ; 3173 tons tailings cyanided 1803 oz. ; 4614
tons slimes, 16680 z. ; 30 tons sulphides, 102 oz. : and 65
tons concentrates, 5900 z. ; total, 83090 z. More tailings
and slimes treated than ore crushed, as usual.- Gt.
Boulder, 2198 tons oxidised ore, 20230 z. ; 2239 tons sul-
phides, 21990 z. by amalgamation and 23090 z. by cyanide ;
6079 tons oxidised tailings, 41590 z. ; total, 10,7200 z.
Noticeable that if the sulphide-plant has been running
all the month at its fullest capacity it is not doing
nearly the work expected! Boulder Main Reef,
1089 tons sulphides, 14660 z. ; 558 tons slimes, 2790 z. ; and
4400 z. from concentrates—total, 21550 z., an increase of
nearly 200 oz. on Aug. yield, and the record production of
the mine. Brownhill : Oxidised ore, 4898 tons, 203!0z. ;
sulphides, treated at the Hannan’s Star by Diehl process,
1867 tons, 45540z.—t0ta1, 65840 z. Oxidised ore now giving
less than lOdwt. per ton, so it is .evident that the good
ore is gone. Sulphides, over 2ioz. —good enough, if
there was an unlimited quantity, which there m not.
Hannan’s Reward : 302 tons, 2960 z. Worked principally
by tributors, who make good wages for themselves and a
little profit for the co.
* • * *
Croesus Proprietary: Has had one more develop-
ment ; bulk assays going over an oz. per ton ; one assay
went 4oz. sdwt., and “ the assayer was instructed to take
out any free gold showing.” Other assays were good,
while the boss was “careful to send stone showing no
visible gold,” and reports : “ The lode, in my opinion, is
a middle-class body of ore, with gold shoots, and rich
veins and bands which can only be proved when opened-
out and driven upon,” and he does not advise this until
the 500 level is reached. The Croesus Prop, was always
a duffer, surrounded by duffers. In the very early days
some gold was got on it from surface veins. Same tlVng
can be said of scores of other leases which have nothing
to warrant their being called or developed as mines.

A far Northern correspondent : —Manager
Provis, of Woodlark Proprietary, was over in Cooktown in
Sept, recruiting cheap mining labor—at £4 per Week.
He picked up 15 men in Cooktown and another d ei
came up per S.S. Arrawatta. Fancy £4 a week in ; p ice
where for six months out of the 12 you are suffering rom
fever ! Cost to “ live,” 255. ; another 10s. for medicine,
and a life-long complication of diseases thrown in. And
considering the expense of getting to a dece’ll wn, £6
per week is next to nothing. But they don t seem I o
want practical miners—they are picking up seamen, fire-
men, and all sorts. ... At Cooktown they a e trying to
get men to go to Noble Island '3O miles north) for 1 3 a
week and find themselves ! I saw a man off* rtol et big
money that they couldn’t show him a ton of wi lfram on
the island—which offer may or may not have been bluff.
• * * *
N. Queensland’s biggest tin mine, the Vulcan,
at Irvinehank, which owes so much to John Moffatt’s
nursing, has got into good lode stuff at 750 ft.—believed
to be Australia’s deepest for this metal.
* • * «
North Chillagoe mining scandal. A Northern
politician puts it that it was an attempt to prevail on a
Govt, official to exceed his duty in assisting to deceive
the public, and a Minister has declared his intention to
sift the whole business and to punish the responsible
person. Even if a swindle, it is a very meek and mi’d
little affair contrasted with the Taranganbar, Last Call,
and other big “ haves ” of Bananaland.
* * * •
On dit that that hang-fire mine, Vesuvius,
Charters Towers, may shortly blossom into an English co.
The late legal manager and two of the Board, with power
of attorney, are now in London with an eye to business.
* * * *
A Bulletin correspondent re Ebagoola (N.Q.)
goldfield (about 40 miles from Port Stewart and 200 from
Cooktown) :—“ The population is fluctuating, and rarely
exceeds 500. There have been some good finds, but where
one gets it 30 get nothing. From an alluvial point of
view, things are not over satisfactory. Some lucky ones
get a slug from loz. to 4oz. One party recently got
40oz. in 11 days by the dry-shaker, another a slug weigh-
ing Boz. 6dwts. On Sept. 10th I saw a 16oz. slug un-
earthed, followed by several smaller pieces. Next day a
131 oz. piece was found in a paddock near the main road,
and during the week the party won 70oz. The field is
scattered, the various patches being a few miles apart.
Want of water is the drawback, and the dry-shaker is
much in evidence. The reefs look fairly well, and some
good crushings will take place when the battery is ready.
The All Nations, Trafalgar, Golden Treasure, Caledonian
P.C., May Queen, Hit and Miss, Last Hit, and others all
have stoneatgrassshowinggoodgold. At the Trafalgar (re-
ward claim) they have large blocks of stone placed outside
the tents for seats; these are dotted all over with gold and
weigh about a cwt. each. This lot, when crushed, will
realise handsomely. The ‘ Trafalgar ’ has 500 ft. along the
line of reef, and has three shafts, the deepest 60ft. The
‘Last Hit’ sent 4 tons to the Coen, which gave 940 z.
smelted gold. The new township is being surveyed on a
good site, but a mail and telegraph service is badly
wanted. Freights from Cooktown to the field are high ;
wharfage rates at Port Stewart £1 per ton on goods
landed and stored, freight from Cooktown £2 per ton, and
to the field £5 10s. per ton. Passage from Cooktown to
Port Stewart, £l. Provisions are dear. Gold of poor
qtialitv realises £2 14s. on the field and £3 at the Mint.
Up to date 10,n00oz. has been won in six months. The
end of Nov. will be time enough to g0. —W.M.8., Cook-
town.”
* * • *
Wallaroo and Moonta, S.A.’s big copper pro-
ducers, now amalgamated, yielded 24,405 tons ore in the
last eight months, Wallaroo giving 15,906 tons 6f this.
The refined copper amounted to 3405 tons. The co. now
carries on a pretty big custom smelting business, too-
treated 10,500 tons in the last eight months. Men em-
ployed—Wallaroo 931, Moonta 926, smelting works 668.
* * * •
Dear Bulletin. — Dredge-carpenters on the
M.L. West Coast do not get 18s. a day; 12s. being the
average for all ship-carpenters’ work, except for bosses,
who run up to 15?. per day. Further, one-half of the
carpenters here are not employed.—M.W., Westport,
M.L.

Bit of valuable information from Sydney Tele-
graph's financial column (9/10/’00) :—“ On the nine
months the Tasmanian revenue has been £1,152,569, an
increase of £145,573.” Little Tasmania never yet
managed to raise quite £1,000,000 revenue in 12 months.
Urinary troubles speedily relieved.
By taking Row’s Bucnu Bitters.
To be obtained at Hotels and Chemists.
Maoriland is about to try an interesting
light-railway experiment. Line is to be built from
Stratford to tap the fertile Ohura district. A 2ft.
guage, a speed of 8 to 10 miles an hour, and an
engine that can go up 1 in 27.
SPORTING NOTIONS.
Sydney season’s first grade cricket opens this
Saturday. Four or five best (on paper) will meet in
initial round. Most interest centres in Cumberland-
Paddington and N. Sydney-Waverley matches.
• • • •
Best joke in Sydney cricket circles just now is
against international and inter-state man Harry Donnan,
who took a hand in second-grade t’other day—playing for
St. George—and was out first ball, a creaser.
* * * •
Looks as if present cricket season will witness
the advent in inter-state matches of another of the
always-coming Gregory family—young Charlie, to wit.
He’s in great form just now, and promises much better
later. His Eight-Hour Day’s innings of 124 was almost
flawless.
• • • • •
Sydney second-grade cricket has a coming top-
notcher in Herbert Deery, son of deceased metallician
Jack Deery. He isn’t quite out of his teens yet, and is a
well-built boy of over 6ft., who inclines to vigorous play.
On opening day of second-grade matches he totted up the
biggest score of the afternoon —129, in which there were
six s’s and seventeen 4’s.
• * * •
Frank Iredale is now finally included in North
Sydney Cricket Team. Central Cumberland claimed
him as he lives midway between the two districts, but the
association decides in favor of N. Sydney. Waverley is
compensated for his loss by the addition of A. G.
M'Kenzie and Chas. Gregory.
• • • . •
Melbourne Cricket Club’s membership-roll has
closed with 2590 members, and over 100 applicants are
waiting to get in. Want of room prevents their admis-
sion. As a solution, the Australasian seriously proposes
that a list be opened for 100 life members at (say) £5O
each, the money tooe used in increasing the club’s accom-
modation. Fancy such a suggestion to the trustee-
managed Association Cricket Ground ! The Sydney body
regularly advertises, in July of each year, that new mem-
bers paying the stereotyped £4 4s. will be allowed the
three months, July, Aug., and Sept., without extra
charge. The reason of the disparity would seem to be
local. The V.R.C. just as completely eclipses the A..1.C.
as the M.C.C. overshadows other Australian cricket orga-
nisations. Indeed the V.A.T.C. with its Caulfield course,
£ll,OOO reserve-fund, and roll of 2500 members, is quite as
important as the Randwick Club.
* • * *
Lawn Tennis premiership of N.S.W., contested
at Strathfield, (Sydney), Saturday, went to Rice, who beat
Poidevin, 6-3, 6-2. Rice’s fore and back-hand volleying
shone out, while accurate side-play and correct serving
were Poidevin’s strong points, but he showed no dash.
Neither was brilliant. Rice might have been but for
anxiety to be safe, still his game was sound and confident
all through. Winner now owns the Challenge Cup
absolutely having secured it thrice in succession.
• »
Some of the things that happened at Fleming-
ton last Saturday have given new interest to the big
Caulfield meeting which opens next Saturday (13th). The
easy defeat of Sagacity, Kinglike and Co. by Fleet
Admiral, who went out at 20 to 1, suggests the probability
of more surprises to follow. Sagacity meets the winner
of the Stand Handicap on better terms in Caulfield Cup,
but all the same there will be many good judges with a
fancy for Fleet Admiral's chance now that the handsome
horse has shown them a fairly handsome performance.
The Stand Handicap “ form ” is worth considering, for
last year La Carabine pulled off the race from Ballistite.
• * • •
Hautboy took the October Stakes brilliantly.
The son of Haut Brion has won three races in succession
since he lost the A.J.C. Derby to Malster, so it seems
more than possible that he didn’t show himself at his
true speed in the classic event. Anyway there is a doubt
about Malster’s present condition, whereas Hautboy,
being evidently in the best of health, looks a good invest-
ment for the Caulfield Guineas.
• «• • *
Barbarossa carried money again at Flemington
last Saturday, and finished nowhere. After one or two
more failures it will be time for punters to abandon Ike
Earnshaw’s nag as a hopeless case.
* •» • *
■_ Grandmaster —First Love gelding Chillagoe,
which annexed Paddock Handicap (6fur.) at Flemington
on Saturday, has been long fancied by punters. Correct-
ness of this estimate was evidenced in the easy' manner
with which a good field, made up of (among others)
Songbird, Dozon, Phillibeg, Lord Rudolph, and Yellow
and Black, were beaten. Chillagoe had heaps of admirers
when sold as ay earlingat Randwick in ’9B. Mr. Sam Hordern
gave 340 guineas for him, and, a little later, when break-
ing up his stable, got rid of the gelding at same figure.
Subsequently the then purchaser, without giving his nag
a fair trial, passed him over to present owner, Mr. Gore,
for 250 guineas. Judging by appearances and the fact
that Chillagoe was the most-promising looking colt
Grandmaster had sired for a long time, it seems a big loss
to the turf that he should have been added to the list.
* * • *
The Caulfield Stakes entries include, as usual,
all the makings of an interesting event, but for some
undiscoverable reason the Stakes “ form ” is very apt to
prove a delusion and a snare. Last year the over-rated
Australian Star went out a hot first favorite and won in a
canter from Contrast, Dewey being third. This year
Kinglike—presuming, that Hautboy is saved for Guineas—
promises to be the best of the youngsters in the Caulfield
Stakes. Some of the old ones ought to beat him. All the
likely ones among the top-weighted Cup horses are
possible starters, and whether they come out on business,
or merely for an airing, the 9-furlong race should be an
interesting display. Seahorse, from Maoriland, may go
to the post. If the long voyage and the change of air
have not upset the beautiful Maoriland “crack,” this
scribe reckons that he should win the Caulfield Stakes,
and perhaps capture the Melbourne Cup indue course.
. * - » * •
The Toorak Handicap (600 sovs.) looks a pretty
good thing for Fleet Admiral, “ on paper,” supposing
that his owner lets him start for the one-mile event, and
run the risk of a further 71b. penalty in the Cups. This
is usually a jostling struggle, though. Fancied horses
are very subject to interference, and a strong, rough out-
sider stands a better chance of getting comfortably into
the straight. Present writer would have a weakness for
the big grey gelding, Reaper (at long odds), in a race like
Toorak Handicap.
Among latest comers into Caulfield Cup betting
is Strathjoy, on the strength of a win last week. Four
years back Strathjoy’s form attracted notice, and an offer
of £5OO for him was’ refused. He did no good afterwards
till t’other day. Writer doesn’t hanker after long-striding
nags like Strathjoy for Caulfield Cup, but, being an old
’un, 6.9 should be a treat to him.
* * » *
Last year’s Caulfield Cup sensation, Mora, has
been freely backed in Melb. and Sydney, and there’s
ample indication that the right people are on, that they
fancy her chance much (despite additional 171 b. up), and
that this time there will be no shortness of work through
a desire to keep her powers dark. But the mare couldn’t
be allowed to go along without some mystery surround-
ing her. Report says she’s been sold to a Sydney pen-
ciller. All the same, Mora has gone over in charge of
E. Knight’s trainer—Dick O’Connor.
• * * *
Another closely-kept Kogarah “sure thing” in
Irene, by British Lion from First Love, went down at
Canterbury (Sydney) Saturday in Park Stakes. Heavily
backed by those in the know from an outside price to first
favorite, ’ she was among the last to get going, as the
result of a bad start, and, though making up much lost
ground in the 6 furlongs, her chase was hopeless. The
winner. Theorem, carried little money and was ridden all
the way by L. Kuhn in most pronounced Tod Sloan
fashion—so much so that to the unaided eye the nag
seemed riderless for some time.
* • • *
Queensland Labor member Jenkin3on’s Bill
restricting use of tote in that State is exciting much
interest amongst sporting men. Measure provides that
one club may only use machine 12 days in the year. As
Q.T.C. races 18 days ihis will probably be amended, for
’tis plain only proprietary clubs (which race every week
in the year) are aimed at. Brisbane Jockey Club has
been particularly referred to in newspaper controversy.
This body is made up of a syndicate of five, who run
races every week at Albion Park, and attract big attend-
ances. Prize money for the year totals £6OOO or there-
abouts.
• * • •
“ J.J.H.” (Prairie, Q.): Fastest mile trotted by
Maud S. was 2.8 J. Record is now a shade under 2min.
* * * *
“Q.”: See W.A. Tatts. consultation man (J.
Charles) advertises a long list of numbers of tickets
representing a heap of unclaimed money for Jan. to June
this year. Why not give ticket-holders’ names—he has
them. Any odds most tickets have been lost, otherwise
they’d have turned up in due course.
* * * »
The meeting between Melb. sable pug Pluto
and “ Cocker” Tweedie, of Sydney Rocks, at Gaiety A.O.
(Sydney) Friday last week was anything but a match.
Tweedie lOst. (every oz.) —though his poundage was
announced as 9.3—loomed up huge alongside t’other, who
was inches shorter and at least 281bs. lighter. Also the
Sydney man was in pretty good fix while the Vic. couldn’t
have been much worse. AH Pluto’s vim petered out in
first 3mins. Perhaps 1000 people paid to see the thing,
and every man of them must have gone home “ full up.”
Initial round saw Pluto make a good show, next round
“ Cocker ” got all over the black and had him anyhow
before “corners.” Third round was a farce. Pluto
could barely stand up—though there had been nothing in
the way of vigor or pummelling—and while in his corner
he skied the rag. Pluto certainly didn’t show that he
had trained an hour, and evidenced a blissful carelessness
of possibilities that called for some explanation.
« » * •
Quite 1200 people saw the match between
“young Nathan ’’ (Melb.) and Frost (Sydney) at Gaiety
A.O. (Sydney) Monday night. It was scrap, rush
and grunt from beginning to close of 21 hot
rounds. Nathan did clean work all the time, but
Frost fouled often, though never badly enough to give
the referee a fair chance of intervening till Lhe last
3min. He punched in “holts,” gripped Nathan round
the neck and sort of jerked him to the boards (upper-
cutting the while) dozens of times, till Nathan staggered
under the roughing but still came up game as a bull-
terrier. Frost once deliberately gripped Nathan round
the back and tried the wrench act. The crowd was
in a constant state of howl through like infractions
of rules. Nathan, undeterred, battled grind}' on,
swung a heavy right over Frost’s left eye, and, later,
treated the other optic similarly ; then he jabbed a stiff
hotly-charged left on Frost’s nose, sending latter’s head
back to the verge of kink. Frequently Nathan was up
against the ropes, and Frost tried all he knew to force
him through. Frost was full of the tricks of the trade.
Field-lighting, where he could do as he liked, would be
more in his line. The “ house” was boilingly excited for
fully an ho ;~ and a-half. At the end of 20th round
referee O. 8....; couldn’t separate the pair on points
(though writer would have had no hesitation in picking
Nathan), and ordered another round, during which Frost
lpst his head and fouled so badly that the fight was given
against him.

Ike Stewart’s? i

The defeat of Mick Dunn by Otto Cribb sug-
gests a match between the latter and 13st. Melb. Fred
Preston, who fell before Dunn a few weeks back. On
paper, the fight looks a foregone conclusion for the Maori-
lander ; but fights are curious things, and the prevailing
opinion in Southern pugdom is that Preston would have
bustled Dunn considerably had he been in form. Again,
Hegarty made a draw with Cribb, and to pit him against
a man of Preston’s weight and hitting power appears
utterly ridiculous to Melt), eyes. Anyhow, the mill will
be a willing one, and, if Cribb chooses to cross the border,
large masses of shekels and enthusiasm await him.
• • • *
“ Old Timer ” : Looks as if Mick Dunn and
Cribb will meet again. Former suggests it and latter is
not unwilling. Writer’ll have the Cribb end this time as
latter fought the first battle handicapped with a big gash
on inside of left thigh and consequent stiffness, caused
through a bike accident. Besides, good and all though
Dunn may be as a boxer, he doesn’t own the fighting-
instinct of which Cribb has such a big helping. In their
previous mill though the “bigger man “ drew ” Cribb 20
times, he couldn’t make the most of the situation. Dunn
“ draws ” from the waist up instead of with the feet, as
did Foley and Jackson. Consequence was that when
Cribb came over he was almost on top of Dunn—much
too close for latter to do anything effective. Had Dunn
acted the master and treated Cribb as a rough pupil, the
result might have been different.

* * * *
Whilom Westralian Tommy Cox, a very inflated
personage, who—a heap taller and heavier —barely beat
“ the Kaffir ” in Sydney last year, has had his second
fight at ’Frisco with one Irwin. The thing lasted 20
rounds and was very dull. Reports tell that Cox forced
the pace and landed at least as many as the other man,
but referee McGinley (an ex-Australian amateur) gave it
to the local bruiser.’ ’Frisco Chronicle says “ possibly
McGinley saw through the eyes of the gallery which was
distinctly pro-Irwin. At best the bout was a better dis-
play of dancing than boxing."
* * * •
Details of match between Australian Tim
Murphy and Californian Tremble at ’Frisco (Aug. 23) are
here. ’Twas an exciting battle all the way, and looked a
cert, for the Yank up to round 13, when one of Murphy’s
dangerous swings landed on the point, and Tremble was
down for seconds over the regulation 10. Said that
Murphy has gone back in form and skill since his first
and only previous battle in the States.
* * * *
“Old-Timer” : The showing of Tim Murphy
(champion middleweight of Australia) in U.S. gives us a
line on the quality of present-day Australian pugilism,
which is evidently none too good. Tim has been long on
t’other side of Pacific, and had only two fights, mainly:
because he didn’t “ take ” in his first with Neill. Neill
beat him handily, and subsequently went under to one
Moffat. ’Tis said that Tim evidenced deterioration in the
match with Tremble, and but for a fluke would have suc-
cumbed again, as Tremble hit him almost any time and
anywhere he wanted to. Writer never thought Murphy
a champion as the term used to be understood here. He
is a hard hitter who depends on landing a knock-out
punch more than jabbing, and leaves himself very open
every time he whirls the right, which is often. Hear that
Otto Cribb talks of going to Yankeeland. Hope he won’t,
as he’ll find a wide difference between the inferior article
we have with u? now and the class he is likely to run up
against there. The club promoting Murphy-Tremble
affair lost considerably over the venture, and as Tim is
hardly likely to get another show soon, he’ll probably re-
turn early. !
* * • *
Veteran pigeon-shot Mr. Fred Gannon, at
Tempe (Sydney), t’other day, undertook to “ grass ” 50
inside boundary within 20min. A big crowd went out to
watch. Birds had to be thrown up 30yds. from shooter,
who was allowed both barrels, as many guns as he liked,
and what birds he could shoot at in the time. Four guns
were availed of, and two attendants looked after loading
and unloading. Two men handed birds to a third, who
threw them up. A strong wind caused the birds to act
very erratically, hut the gun was deadly for some time,
and many clever kills resulted. Towards the end, the
strain told. Required 50 were brought down in 13)min.,
and, as 46 got away, 96 were fired at. This isn’t a record
—the best is 9£min., put up by same shooter. Said
that the “ book ” who laid against the gun is prepared to
wager evens that the 13iiuin. is not beaten by anyone
else.
• • » *
Editor Bulletin. —Your issue of 29/9/’OO gives
a portrait of the champion pigeon-slaughterer of this
canting, snuffle-busting, Boer-baiting country. Shame on
The Bulletin for soiling its pages with the portrait of a
man who can extract keen pleasure from the pain and
torture of harmless birds. I once lived near a brute who
bred pigeons for the gun-trap, and I have often seen
wounded birds return to the pigeon-house with bills
blown away, others with legs shot off or mutilated, poor
mute martyrs to the inhuman cruelties of brutal men.
Has The Bulletin so little sympathy for suffering
birds that it cannot find a word of protest and
condemnation for a fiendish sport which encourages
so much the lust for blood and destruction, now strongly
permeating the devil’s spawn who left these shores to
destroy their fellow-creatures in South Africa. The Bul-
letin also opens its pages to recitals of flesh-batter-
ing exhibitions called boxing-contests. Will it deny
that these contests serve no other purpose than that
of calling together an aggregation of rascality, thugdom,
and scum which is a menace to good order and a disgrace
to a community whose supersensitive sanctity revolts at
the mild insinuation that its glorious Protestantism was
cradled in kingly lust and wet-nursed in polygamy.—
John Maclaciilan.

Westralian Amateur Ped. Premierships were
decided at Perth Harriers’ Sports t’other day. Previous
holder of 100yds. (Law) gave others no hope, and snapped
the worsted 4yds. to the good in 10i. Last year he showed
just as fine form, and in lacrosse players’ 120 yards
supremacy led a dozen others. Five miles cross-country
event saw Syd. Smith go down for first time since running
in W.A. Quarter-mile champ., Reg. Burt, finished 10yds.
ahead after a game struggle all the journey. Time, 3om.
10 l-10s.
* * * *
A late letter from Australian crack swimmer
Fred. Lane says he leaves London middle of Oct. Other
Sydney top-notcher Jack Ilellings comes about same
time, and so does Lindberg. This swimming season
will he full of interest, as “ Mud ” Bishop, Dick Gavili and
Bob Craig will set-Fred and Jack a lively go if they don’t
actually beat them. Lane got the 200 metres world’s
championship at Paris. At Jersey', on the way r back to
London, he won a 220yds. race, and, subsequently, at
Guernsey, a 100yds. event—both off scratch.
# ■ - * * *
League of Wheelmen’s Annual Carnival began
at Sydney Cricket Ground last Saturday, and will finish
this Saturday. Attendance didn’t reach expectations by
a long way and demonstrated that, as is the case all over
the world, the popularity of cycle-racing is waning fast.
Sixteen lined up for chief event —the five-miles’ supre-
macy of the continent. Final struggle was between
Sydney champion (Joe Megson) and Melbourne’s crack,
the dusky Gordon. Midway in last lap Gordon,
sprinting brilliantly, went to the front with a
two-lengths’ lead, and a little later it seemed as if the
race was surely his. Already scores of throats yelled,
“ Gordon wins ! ” but Megson, spurting phenomenally,
wiped off the intervening space inch by inch, and just
pipped the colored man on the post amid intense
excitement. Hunt (M.L.) was a good third. Time,
11.7 2-5. Last lap (J mile) was done in 32 2-ssec. Many
riders (notably Walne, Martin, Beauchamp, and M'Donald)
didn’t reach previous form through winter’s inertia, but
intervening week may make some difference.

N.S.W. Cyclists’ Union 50-miles road race for
Massey-Harris cycle was contested by a big field on
Parramatta course, Saturday. R. Lawrence (Bm. 30sec )
won, his riding time being 2h. 52m. 16s. W. Marr (7m.)
was second, riding time 2h. 50m. 48s. —an amateur record.

M.L. premier cyclist Sutherland at World’s
Championship meeting in Paris, writes that the Fiench
style of riding is a crawl, with a 200yds. sprint. He
enthuses over Jacquelin, owner of world’s professional
mile supremacy. Sutherland did no good himself—
Yankee cracks McFarland and Cooper gave him 70yds.
in a mile. Letter says these two may come to Australia,
per Britannia (which left London 20th ult.), and compete
at Austral meeting.

Mr. C. A. Proctor, secretary of the Dunlop
Tyre Co., arrived back in Melbourne from London early
this week. Mr. Proctor, who looks well after his flying
visit to the Old Country, secured for his company the De
Dion Motor agency for the majority of the Australian
States.
An Engineer Crippled.
Mr. John Boyce, of Hunter-street, Stockton, a
suburb of Newcastle, N.S.W., writes as follows:
“I write to inform you of my case. I am well
known in this district. For some months I was
laid up with rheumatism, which confined me to my
house, being practically a cripple. I could not
lift my hand to my head. I suffered untold agony.
I tried several remedies, but of no avail. I lastly
tried your Dr. Morse’s Indian Root Pills, and was
astonished at the result. The first bottle gave me
real relief ; I then took a second bottle, and was
then able to resume my daily work.” Mr. Boyce
is a well-known citizen, and by occupation an
engineer on the Stockton ferries. Dr. Morse’s
Indian Root Pills stand alone as a perfect blood
purifier, and positively cure biliousness, indiges-
tion, dyspepsia, headache, sallow complexion,
liver and kidney troubles, piles, pimples, and
blotches. Price, Is lid per bottle, or six bottles
6s 6d. Sole proprietors, The W. H. Comstock
Co., Ltd. (Australasian Depot), 58 Pitt-street,
Sydney. They are packed in amber bottles, and
the full name blown thereon.*
Mrs. Wilson, of the Blue Mountains Hotel,
Lawson, who is 82 years of age, lately tripped and
fell against a piano, receiving a serious wound.
She has held a license on the mountains for 62
years—a record, surely.
Evidence by prosecutor in a recent Sydney
assault case:
Accused then jammed witness on to a couch, and,
catching him by the throat with both hands, attempted
to choke him. While he had witness so held, accused
caught hold of his nose three times with his teeth till he
succeeded in tearing it off, at the same time punching
him till he became stunned.
The affair occurred in a pub., and a constable
afterwards picked up on the spot the severed nose.
The defence was the “ remember nothing about
it ” one, and accused goes up for trial at quarter-
sessions.

“Banjo” Paterson.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
■ I asked “ Banjo ” Paterson what is the sound of
a bullet at close range.
“ W’sh-sh-sh-sh ! the force and pace of the
bullet gives you the feeling that it would have
gone through the side of a house. Oh-h-h ! you
never get used to it; the men who say they do
are liars, I think. And when the bullets hit the
ground near you ! —Rp-rp-rp-rp ! —you imagine
they are screwing themselves right into the earth.
It does frighten you ! it’s worse than the bullets
going overhead. I was standing holding a horse,
when a bullet shot past my shoulder and gave him
a smack as if he were hit by a hammer. It struck
him through the hips. It puts the fear of God
into you when you hear that whistle and that
fearful staggering blow. No, it didn’t bring the
horse down ; a bullet seldom does. They rode
that horse the rest of the day. It takes a good
number of bullets to bring an animal down unless
you hit him just right; I’ve fired 15 bullets myself
into a buffalo in the Northern Territory before he
dropped. And over in Africa I saw a man with
10 bullet-wounds —the entrances and exits of
five bullets in his arms and legs—and yet
he got all right. A bullet doesn’t kill a man
unless it goes through his head or abdomen, and
not then always. I was standing a couple of
yards away from a Major —a friend of mine—when
a bullet hit him a fearful whack. He cried,
‘Oh!’ and staggered. I caught him. ‘Have
you got it?’ I asked. ‘Yes,’ he replied, ‘through
the liver—l’m a dead man ! ’ I held him, and
called a Tommy who was passing. He came, and
helped me to carry the Major, and we took him
along 100 yards; then the bullets were coming so
thick round us that we had to put him down.
The three of us made too big a mark for them to
fire at. We lay beside him and looked after him
till the ambulance took him. He was shot
through the stomach, but he got quite well again.
I was hit myself when we were to the west of Pre-
toria. A bullet glanced from a rock and hit me
a rousing bang in the ribs. It frightened me
horribly. I fancied I could feel the next one
coming through my back. Osborne caught me as
I reeled in the saddle. But I wasn’t really in-
jured ; the bullet bruised me badly—though it
did not enter it shook me up properly. I wouldn t
let them class me as wounded, as I didn’t want to
scare people over here.
“ When first I went under fire I was excited
with the novelty of the thing—the novelty over-
masters you. Hm-m-m-m-m sings the first bullet
high and wide. ‘ Oh ! ’ you say to yourself quite
cheerily, ‘that’s a genuine bullet!’ But after-
wards, when you’ve seen fellows smashed up
ugh !—the more you’re under fire the less you,like
it. It’s the sense of utter helplessness that s so
distressing—the feeling that away over there be-
hind a rock some fellow js_ lying down in
security and carefully sniping you. And
after being under fire you come out very over-
strained, and very limp. It’s a terrible
feeling—that helplessness under fire ! Whish.
whish ! —the bullet goes so fast! —and one with a
bit of loose lead on it!—you can hear it squealing
long before it comes and after it passes; no
gammon, it’s a nerve-smashing thing. Some of
them make the noise of a small express
train. And every wsh-sh-sh, rp-rp, hm-m-m of
the bullet says to you: ‘ You’re only a target
here ! The sooner you get out of this the better ! ’
But I wouldn’t run, when the troops didn’t. I
used to go up with the men in the firing-line—
when other men go into fire, you go too. And
our Australian fellows seemed cool under
the very hottest fire. All the time I was
there I never saw one man on the British side cut
it without orders. I had the sentiment of comrade-
ship for all the troops on our side, but I used to
get very excited and anxious when the Australians
were going in. A feeling of horror shook me
every time one of our men or horses dropped.
And all the time I’d be watching them I’d be say-
ing to myself: ‘There are the English, the Irish,
the Scotch ; the bullets have got to hit somebody.
There are plenty of fellows beside the Australians
to be hit.’
“ The grandest picture of war that I saw was
the charge of the Seaforth Highlanders. My
God ! it tightened your breath to see them go
across a quarter-of-a-mile of open. There are no
bugle-calls in battle now. You see the men lying
prostrate on the ground—the bullets squealing and
humming oyer them. When the officer rises—
that’s the signal for the men to rise and charge
with him. And these fellows, led by a subaltern,
6ft. 4in. high—a big Highlander in his kilts—rose
with him in a flash. There was no waiting, no
watching, as with a bad regiment, for someone else
to rise and first draw the fire. They all sprang up
and dashed forward. It was a cruel thing to
watch—all your nerves were saying: ‘ one of
those men will go down any moment, any moment,
down ! ’ And they went down ! down ! down !
pitching heavily, helplessly forward, for all the
world as if their feet had caught in something and
they hadn’t tried to save themselves from falling
Oh ! every feeling—even the admiration for their
heroism —was lost in the feeling of horror ! But
they rose and dashed forward ! rose and dashed
forward ! rose and dashed forward ! and the Boers
never waited to give them their revenge.
“Each man has his own set of emotions in
battle. Some men get excited and laugh, others
get very white and grim. Some again say noth-
ing; others again talk volubly. And when a
man’s hit he drops, as a rule, silently; too dazed
with the blow to cry out. The shot jars all his
nerves and he lies there helpless, conscious only of
a numbing pain.
“From what I could learn, a soldier in action
knows extremely little of what is going on. I
found a chap one evening lying exhausted after a
day’s fighting. He was asleep in the dust and I
gave him a nudge and said ‘ Hey, wake up! you
don’t want to be here, whoever you are.’ He
opened his eyes, shook himself, sat up, and after
staring at me for a second or two in semi-
unconsciousness, opened his eyes still wider
and said, ‘ Good Lord! it’s Banjo Paterson.’
Then I had a yarn to him about the fight. He
said : ‘We were ordered up to them hills and we
kept getting up and running forward and burying
our heads in the sand till we got where they
wanted us. I couldn’t see any enemy. But I
knew there was a rifle fire going on ; and after a
bit I said to myself, ‘ Well, I’ve lugged this rifle
a devil of a way. I think I ought to let her off.’
So I ups and at the hill. Gawd ! she brought so
many bullets back that I waited half-an-hour
before I decided to have another shot at the hill.
Then I let her go again. This time a bigger
volley came back, and I nearly got hit by several.
And while I was thinking hadn’t I better lie quiet
there, an officer comes along and, standing near
me, said, ‘My boys, the Boers have retreated.
You have behaved very bravely ! ’
“Out of that nine months’campaign, crowded
every day with new experiences and fresh im-
pressions, the faot that impressed me most of all
was the absolute self-reliance of ‘the old man.’
Roberts lifted up, promoted, or destroyed the
army generals—men as big almost as himself in
the public mind—as if they were little children.
Fancy sending a full-blown general Home without
a word of explanation ! Yes; Roberts had des-
potic control of 200,000 men and £70,000,000.
“ Another thing that impressed me was that no
actual battlefield in the slightest degree resembled
the battlefield of the painter and artist. In a
picture, a gun is shown as it appears on the
stage. The gunners are all posing. The officer is
there in a heroic attitude, with his mouth wide
open. In a real action, you will see fellows lying
asleep beside the gun, while their mates carry up
the cartridges quick and lively, load and blaze,
while their officer is walking up and down very
rigidly. And when the bullets are coming at them
fast, you can see that the men are thinking hard
about the hotness of things, but they say nothing.
They do all their work quickly and quietly. And
a battery coming into action is a very different
spectacle from the artist’s conception. There is
no flourish, no style about the reality. The
animals are broken and jaded ; and you will see a
driver nursing one wretch, and chopping into the
other, to get the gun along.
“ The soldiery didn’t bother looking after
women ; they were looking for something to eat
all the time. Some of the hard-blowing fellows
boasted of their conquests, but I’m certain that the
gayest thing they did was to make passing love to a
Kaffir woman, and that’s all the romance that was
let into the soldier’s life in South Africa. The
gaiety of war belongs to those who stop at home.
Thus Mafeking day with the nation was celebrated
by champagne festival, brilliant pageants, and the
love and sparkle of pretty girls. But for us
over there in Africa —we didn’t rejoice—we ex-
pected Mafeking to be relieved. And the day we
got the news we didn’t even have a rum ration.
“I was struck with the English horses over
there. They are very fine. And the English
soldiers look after their horses very well ; it’s part
of their discipline.
“ I was surprised at the number of Australians
there were in the Afrikander regiments. Very
often I was hailed from their ranks. * Hey!
Paterson ! Hullo, Banjo ! ’
“ I didn’t get enteric fever, even at Paardeberg,
where the fever mowed men down in hundreds
and hundreds. I’d had typhoid pretty badly over
here, and the doctors told me that that experience
would, in a great degree, make me immune. I
didn’t hear that if a man has a weak spot in his
organs a battle will find it out; but I’m sure of
this —that the strain of being under fire badly
affects the heart. I don’t know whether I have
any special soldier-instinct in me, but my grand-
father was a captain in the Indian Mutiny, and
was mentioned twice for bravery.
“From what I saw of the Boers, it was clear
that they could not fight a trained regiment in
the open, though that’s a matter of discipline
more than pluck. And they very seldom waited
for close quarters.
“ The effect of the campaign on the Australians
will be to make them 50 times more English than
before. I’m sure that the bulk of our fellows
would, after a few months’ spell, assist in any
other English war, out of a feeling of comrade-
ship. And another effect of this war on the Aus-
tralians is, that it has killed inter-State
jealousy among them, and caused them to regard
Australians as one people with one destiny.”
S. A. MILLS.
At Poverty Point.
The three prima-donnas of Musgrove’s grand opera
venture have their juvenility vouched for by the manager.
They are all three of them under 24 years of age. Hiss
Lorraine, who studied under Marchesi, is an American
with a Oovent Garden “ star” reputation. Miss Crichton,
from Chili, has made her successes thus far in the South
American republics; and Miss Lilian Coomber is best
known for her Covent Garden performance of Michaela
in “ Carmen.” It was as Michaela, by the way, that poor
little Flo Graupner scored her first important hit during
Martin Simonsen’s unfortunate season at Melb. Opera
House —the season which ended in awful ructions.
Nellie Stewart, according to all the interviewers, looks
years younger than ever. Those whom the gods love die
young because they never grow old.
“ The New East Lynne ” staged by Johnnie Sheridan at
Melb. Royal on Saturday, was notable for an interesting
re-appearance, Emma Bronton, for a long time leading
lady to both Brough and Boucicault and Williamson and
Musgrove, popping up again in the character of the comic
aunt. Sydney ought to remember Miss Bronton as the
creator of the bright, particular female in B. and B’s.
farce, “ The Solicitor.”
The value of competition in things theatrical is shown
by the fact that the Martinettis had splendid houses at
Rickards’ Tivoli in Adelaide, while Nance O’Neil was
crowding the Royal every night. The former are still
going strong. The latter ended with “ Hedda Gabler,”
and held the crowd spell-bound (puzzled though it was)
while she shot herself “ beautifully.”
The outside of Melb. Princess’s, now that the painters
and gilders have done with it, gives an idea of the lavish
style in which the interior was treated. The chairs in
stalls, d.c., and boxes are re-upholstered with electric-
blue plush, the special patent velvet carpet to match
having an old-gold bordering to set it off. Way up in the
amphitheatre the seating accommodation has been
Axminster-carpeted and provided with backs for the first
time. Always beautiful, the Princess's is now more
gorgeous than ever. Pitt, the architect of the building,
did the redecorations, the whole scheme of upholstery
and so forth being carried out from specifications and
instructions by Wihnot Fink, who, though not an archi-
tect, appears to have supervised the work with an expert’s
eye.
Haskett Smith, the massive parson who found lecturing
more congenial than preaching, told an Argus interviewer
in effect that there was as much chance of settling the
Jews in Palestine as of establishing an ice-cream foundry
in Sheol. He said his friend, Lawrence Oliphant, had
once approached Baron Rothschild for support of a scheme
of this sort. The millionaire didn’t seem very enthusias-
tic, so Oliphant pointed out delicately that, as a good
Jew, he must believe in the ultimate rising of the Messiah
in Jerusalem. “ Yes,” said Rothschild, “ but 1 hope
when He does come. He’ll appoint me ambassador in
London.” Among other interesting things that Haskett
says is a grave assertion that the arrival of ihe Colonial
troops in South Africa, “and especially the Australians”
was practically the turning-point of the war. The Bulle-
tin wonders what Mr. H. will do when he gets on his
Canadian lecturing-tour.
The Bulletin’s London correspondent: “ ‘ That |fine
figure of a woman, Miss Florrie Forde, who, both as
regards appearance and voice, might be described as an
Australian Harriet Vernon,’ quoth London Referee —and
it is accurate in every word. The flat-chested, ‘ early-
grave ’ girl who used to comatose the spiders with ‘ I am
the idol of Melbourne (or Sydney) town’is now a fine,
imposing-looking woman. Harriet’s legs are as graceful
as an Arab steed’s neck and as solid as Tattersall’s sweep,
so it' is a great compliment to Florrie, and her legs
deserve it.”
A Bulletin Melb. notice of Miss Crane as Glory
Quayle referred to her Trilby as a “stone” deaf young
woman. The “s ” was only another instance of the com-
positor’s masterly foolishness. The copy had said “ tone ”
deaf.
Marshall Hall, speaking as President of the Orchestral
Musicians’ Union of Australia, protests against the need-
less “ rehearsal ” work demanded of theatre orchestrians
in these parts. The men are liable to a “ call ”at 10 a.m.,
and have, in some cases, to stay at the theatre for many
hours, with nothing better to do than an occasional tutn-
tum accompaniment for a comic song.
Irrepressible Clifford Ferley Is, nowadays, aulte •
serious-minded pusher of provincial shows. Ferley has
been associated with a record-breaking achievement, it
seems. He was business representative for .Anderson’s
co. at Broken Hill, where "The Ladder of Life’’ran six
nights at a stretch. The longest previous run for any
piece at the Barrier was five nights.
Wybert Reeve, whose lease of Adelaide Theatre Royal
now expires, states that he has paid £38,000 for
rent and lighting during the 10 years of his management.
He complains that since the inter-State railway to Mel-
bourne has been opened, Adelaideans have grown so fas-
tidious in dramatic entertainments that they will not
patronise any but the very best shows, and thus the ex-
penses of management are always greater than the pro-
fits. Then in the very hot weather they will not go to the
theatre under any circumstances.
A perky little actress guying her audience the other
night put a rather scarlet query point-blank to a Sullen
man in the stalls. “ Ask your youngest son ” was the
biting reply.
An interview with Cecil Ward in Melbourne’s latest
little weekly’ is rather amusing. Says the interviewer to
Cecil—“ 1 remember you as a slip of a lad some 13 years
ago. Have been delighted with your Tom Jones, &c., &c.
You were Armand D’Arcay in 1 The Village Priest Cap-
tain Hugh Ardale in 1 Mrs’. Tanqueray.’ I have nc for-
gotten the many, many others, but time is too short to
remind you of them.” Perhaps Cecil Ward did . play
Hugh Ardale in one of the revivals, but an interviewer
who forgets that he made his “ Mrs. Tanqueray ” success
in the part of Sir G orge Orreyd, the boozer with a genius
for breaking old china in a “ gentlemanly way ” by drop-
ping it on the floor, doesn’t pay much of a compliment to
the actor. And says Cecil to the interviewer —“ If I play
Napoleon later on it will be my first appearance in any-
thing that gives me a chance this season,” meaning the
Crane-Power season. He ignores the fact that the parts
of the two Defarges in “ The Only Way ” offer a good
chance for a strong melodramatic actor. Clever Cecil
Ward fails in this because he can’t curse his adversary
in the prologue, or play a gloomy-character part in the
play. He gets the chance all the same.
The internals of the old Melb. Opera House have now
disappeared, and the building of the new theatre, to be
ready by 14th May, 1900, will be started as soon as pos-
sible. Differences of opinion between the original joint
architects—Backhouse and Backhouse, of Sydney, and
Pitt, of Melbourne—threatened to delay the work inde-
finitely. At last the ground landlord grew restive, and as
Pitt was the architect he had nominated on his side, and
Pitt’s plans were already passed by the Board of Health,
the whole architectural job fell to Pitt, the local man.
Rickards had a natural leaning to the firm who planned
his beautiful Sydney Tiv., but all the same the architect
of Melbourne Princess’s seems good enough to swear by.
However, there is going to be a law-suit over the matter.
It was funny to see two old mummers of another
co. sitting gloomily through a matinee performance (at
Melb. Princess’s) of “ Manoeuvres of Jane,” or any
other bright comedy by the Broughs. At the end of each
act those two unappreciative old stagers would ask one
another what they thought of it, and each would drearily
reply, “O, I don’t know ” with an elevation of the eye-
brows that meant “It ain’t acting.” From their point of
view it really wasn’t acting. Nobody had been violently
unnatural.
Seems that the bank-’oliday cockneys who relieve their
feelings in song are now regarding the fruits of Patriot-
ism from a melancholy point of view. Their favorite
ballad is “Break the News to Muvver.”
Recent newspaper notices of Miss Eloise Juno’s career
must have surprised many young playgoers who had
never seen the old favorite until she played Nannie, in
“ Little Minister.” Miss Juno’s first engagement was
with Bland Holt’s father, at Dunedin, she says, though
she had previously been a pupil of Mrs. NVyndham, in
Edinburgh. Coming along to Australia, she took impor-
tant parts (“ with songs,” as the programmes used to say)
for the Harwood, Stewart and Hennings management at
Melb. Royal, and was engaged to support Charles
Matthew’s and Walter Montgomery. After a while, Miss
Juno went to England, and w r as leading lady to Creswick
in a round of Shakespearian plays ; then she toured “ on
her own ” with a co., and finally returned here with
Jenny Lee, figuring as Lady Dedlock in the first Aus-
tralian production of “ Jo.” Of late years, this admirable
actress has seldom had a place on the Sydney and Mel-
bourne stage, and the few characters she has touched
were almost invariably Scotch. Nowadays, one thinks of
her only as a specialist. Her pupils are comparative
experts in Scotch accent, and her recent Melbourne
benefit concert was arranged by the local Caledonian
and “ Thustle ” societies, but the record of Eloise Juno is
that of a good all-round player.
“F.” “Because Hall Caine’s, ‘The Christian’ is a
gloomy novel with an unattractively amorous fanatic for
the hero, and an impossible Glory for heroine, the men
who built a fairly strong drama with moderately good
material have got little credit for their work. Bernard
Espinasse presumably was principal part author of ‘ The
Christian ’ in its stage form, although Wilson Barrett’s
bigger theatrical name takes first place on the bill. Let
Espinasse, the local straggler, have his due. The play he
adapted from ‘ The Christian ’ compares favorably with
many adaptations of novels that gave the dramatist’better
chance. Hall Caine’s dialogue is poor stuff for the most
part, and the adapter had no forcible speeches provided
for him. One remembers nothing in the novel that has
been unwisely omitted from the play, whereas the play,
in the first act, is an ingenious bit of plot manufacture.
Again, John Storm’s interview with the Holy Gethsemane
person at the Hospital, likewise the girl’s signing of the
music-hall contract, are matters that had to be jammed
into the second act, and they seem to happen more
naturally than some of the important events of melo-
dramas which come to us from London. The fourth act
could have been improved, perhaps, by the addition of a
scene showing the outside of Glory Quayle’s domicile, and
the departure of the fleshly apostle therefrom at 3 a.m.,
‘ spotted ’ by the mobsman who subsequently leads the
attack on him. As for the last act it might easily locate
John’s decease within the tabernacle instead of out in the
street, and Polly Love could be sent to fetch Glory just
before the scene changed from the street to the tabernacle,
and Glory might come as a penitent music-hall vocalist,
not as one who had taken up slum salvation work at a
moment’s notice. But it is easier to make such suggest-
ions than to turn them into improvements. ‘ The
Christian,’ as it stands, is an effective, payable show,
admitting of more effective assistance than Miss Crane
gives it. Henriettta Watson played Glory Quayle in far
stronger style.”

Extracts from Letters.
J. T. GRESTY, Esq., Prof. Mus., Syd.—“ It has increased
my natural memory for music to a remarkable degree. To
musical students it will be a great boon, and a sure
stepping-stone to success.”
Mr. C. T. DAVIS, Dunedin, N.Z.—“ It has greatly
strengthened my natural memory, and given me the
power to readily connect and to lastingly memorise the
most abstruse, disconnected and uninteresting matter —
making study easy and delightful.”
STEPHEN. R. MILLER, Esq., Sydney.—“ Your rules
are perfectly natural, and tend as much to improve an
ordinary memory as to cure a bad one. In my position
as Chief Clerk of the Statistical branch of the Ry. Dept. I
have great scope for memory. Your system has been of
very great service to me.”

Maoriland is the first province to get the
war on to its postage-stamps. The new 1M.
sample is to show dismounted troopers in the
foreground and the rest of the contingent in the
distance. The Bulletin is beginning to grow a
trifle weary of this postage-stamp mania. The
calm, unmoved John Bull keeps the Queen’s head
on his stamps, in rain and shine, year in and year
out, and he doesn’t rush on every slight occasion
to put a new issue with a disembowelled Boer or
any such frenzy upon it on the market. His stamp
may not be artistic, but, anyhow, it is always
there and with enough gum on the back. Federa-
tion, among its other blessings, will wipe out five
Australian issues in one act. One people, one
destiny, one postage stamp !
There are other occasions for complaint re
the maltreatment of Scottish regimental legs in
Melbourne. For instance, at a recent display
several privates exhibiting a great waste of flabby,
dough-coloured, naked leg behaved in so unsol-
dierly a manner as to brirfg the regiment into ridi-
cule and contempt before a great gathering.
Whilst the Scotch warriors were “ cavourtin’” in
their ballet-skirts, the privates in question were
seen to be suffering from grotesque convulsions,
and they wriggled hideously and squirmed like
tickled eels, scraping their outraged calves with
their toes and their heels the while, and looking,
oh, so sad. At length Scotch human nature gave
way under the strain, and those tortured privates
settled down and scratched —scratched with both
hands ravenously and in the light of day. Inves-
tigation disclosed that some miscreant had daubed
the nude legs of the Caledonian heroes with cow-
itch.
“C.C.” to The Bulletin on a matter of
much matrimonial importance : —
In a recent N.S.W. custody-of-ohildren case it appeared
that a pre-nuptial agreement had been come to that any
child which might arrive should be trained in the faith
of the parent of the same Sex. It seems, however, that
promises of this sort are not binding in law. A man’s
promise to another man is more or less binding, but his
promise towards his wife, whom he may possibly gain for
his wife by the help of that very promise, is of no effect;
and whether with a promise or without a promise, the
children are entirely his. Old-fashioned people who do
not approve of the female franchise are fond of pointing
out to women how much power they have in religion, and
how that department is as peculiarly their province as
politics is that of man ; and after dwelling on woman’s
superior insight in matters of sentiment and religion,
they calmly hand over the religious training of these
women’s children to the sex of which 70 per cent, are
practically atheists. As for the Judge’s remark that he
would never sanction a man’s being obliged to
pay for the support of children that were being
brought up in a religion of which he disapproved—
the father of an illegitimate child, if known, is legally
obliged to assist in the support of the child, and is not at
all consulted as to its religion ; so that the law gives
complete parental rights to that class of woman which is
■ conventionally held to be the least fitted to exercise these
rights. _ The explanation of this inconsistency lies, of
course, in the fact that the illegitimacy laws are new-
made and therefore more in accordance with modern sex-
equality, while the marriage laws are old and theological
and tangled up with the notion that woman brought sin
into the world. A curious fact is, that while the majority
of men theoretically maintain that the power should be
wholly the father’s, almost every fairly sensible, fairly
vvell-meaning man shrinks from the thought of interfering
with his own wife in the religious training of his children,
even when said wife is not particularly intelligent and
merely reaches that level of commonplace goodness for
which Queen Victoria is so mysteriously extolled. So
that the power which the law gives the father is advan-
tageous only .to the fanatic and the spitefully-minded
husband who chooses this method of “ getting back on ”
his wife.
THE AUSTRALIAN GEM TRADE.
H. Newman, the well-known jeweller of 175
Elizabeth-street, Melbourne, is the largest buyer
of opal in Australia, and opa] miners will find it
to their advantage to send their opal direct to
him. The firm has a reputation for straight deal-
ing extending over a period of 40 years, which is
a sufficient guarantee to consignors that they will
be honorably treated. H. Newman is also a
buyer of all other Australian gems. *
“ Ian ” to The Bulletin on a much-
debated question:—
The fact that the Maoriland Ministers are over-worked
is as much the fault of the Ministers themselves as of the
country. In his farewell letter to Parliament, “Lands”
M'Kenzie referred to the practice inaugurated by mem-
bers of the Seddon Ministry, of going round the country
and becoming personally acquainted with the require-
ments of different districts. As a result of this, he
claimed that many districts had had roads and bridges
constructed which would never have been made but for
the Minister’s personal observation. Also, conversely, if
a Minister is loyal to fiis public duty, many a useless road
or bridge would remain unmade, which might otherwise
have been successfully pressed upon an ignorantly com-
pliant Minister by a “roads and bridges member.” So
far, good. But the members of the democratic Seddon
Ministry go farther, and seek to become veritable Pooh-
bahs, and ultimate referees upon all questions, even the
most trivial. New legislation every session tends more
and more to make the Minister the absolute controller of
all matters coming within the scope of his depart-
ment. M‘Kenzie himself, while retaining the dis-
trict Land Boards, has increasingly claimed and
exercised the right of reviewing, altering, or
vetoing their decisions, even on such trifling mat-
ters as the right of a 20-acre Govt, leaseholder to
transfer or mortgage his holding. Now, while a Minister
does well to become acquainted with the general necessi-
ties or capabilities of all districts, is he justified in ham-
pering his legitimate work with petty trivialities in this
manner ? He must either struggle wearily and with fail-
ing health against overwhelming duties, or else become
careless and sign documents without sufficient knowledge
of their contents, in which case his signature remains to
rise in judgment against him in matters which he might
well have left to subordinates. So, in this practice of re-
ferring everything to one man, does ancient history
repeat itself in the democracies of the Pacific, and the
servant of the people seeks to place himself in the position
of a dictator.
Nervous sufferers will find it worth their
while to peruse Herr Rassmussen’s advt. on nage

In order to cope with their growing busi-
ness in Australasia, the Northern Newspaper
Syndicate, _ Kendal, Eng., has appointed Mr.
E. 0. Amsinck, of Sydney, as their representative
for the whole of Australia and Maoriland. Mr.
Amsinck is an old pressman, has had considerable
newspaper experience, and is personally known to
most of the leading newspaper proprietors.
N.S. Wales’s butcher’s-bill in S’Africa to
date : —Officers killed, 7 ; died of disease, 1; non-
com. officers and men killed or died of wounds,
27; died of disease, 43; total deaths, 78. Wounded
—Officers, 9 ; N.C.O.’s and men, 87. Missing—
Non-commissioned officers and men, 39. Prisoners
—Officers, 4; N.C.O.’s and men, 13.
The sudden death, last week, of Aid.
William Dymock, the well-known Sydney book-
seller, was, no doubt, partly attributable to undue
mental excitement arising out of the current
Public Library inquiry, involving a trade dispute
in which Mr. Dymock was keenly interested.
Mr. Dymock was a native of Melbourne, and was
but 39 years of age when he died. He only re-
cently entered public life by defeating the late
Mr. Sydney Burdekin for the ward formerly re-
presented by the latter in Sydney City Council.
Civilisation is a bloodstained business.
Over in China the other day Russians murdered
and ravished and stole, as per mail; Germans and
French and English and Japanese stole; and
Americans grumbled because they weren’t allowed
to steal. All for the sake of civilisation, and the
glorification of civilisation’s God. The Chinese
suicided by families, too, rather than face the in-
vaders ; and (partly owing to the disorganisation
caused by the Allies’ attack) a big famine is pro-
phesied in Northern China this winter.
Premier Lyne wants to immortalise his
reign by making a road round the foreshore of
Sydney Botanic Gardens, to be called Hopetoun
Avenue by way of greasing the new Gov.-
General. Hopetoun won’t thank him, for the new
road will end the seclusion of Govt. House. The
idea finds favor with the Nicest People, who
dream of a Rotten Row Parade before vice-
royalty ; but it is really for the benefit of
the four-bob Robbo man who blasphemes
to Coogee Bay and through Centennial Park.
The Gardens are the only quiet, sylvan spot in the
city accessible to the multitude; and Premier
Lyne proposes to give their beautiful water-
frontage to the twin fiends of Dust and Noise.
But N.S. Wales is so used to public scandals that
the iniquity of this one will probably be realised
only when the job’s done. The Man who Mur-
dered Quiet —Long Bill Lyne.
Plain George Turner begins to get certifi-
cates of good character from unexpected quarters.
His inveterate enemy, Murray Smith, moaned,
the other day, “ I did not expect to have so soon
to regret the absence of George Turner and his
colleagues from the Treasury benches,” and God-
frey Downes Carter said that he helped to put
Turner out “in a weak and misguided moment.”
Yet the M‘Shiels Government has done nothing
hideously bad, and it has done some good things—
for one, it has started the resumption of large
estates for closer settlement. But it creates an
impression of being weak at the knees and
shambling on the feet somehow. If Shiels could
be confined in a bathing-machine and help to
govern the country by shoving written instruc-
tions under the door the effect would be better.
Shiels has ideas at times that would do credit to a
strong politician, but the sight of that tall, thin
elderly-young dudish personage talking in a thin
voice at great length on two lengthy thin legs is
disastrous.
N.Z. Loan and Mercantile Agency Co. reports
the state of things in the wool and wheat markets.
Judged by the European prices for Merino “ Tops,”
woolly prospects are not too good ; however, the London
sales commence on the 9th inst., and will more clearly
define the prospect. Unfortunately, at present a general
slump on all fine wools is anticipated, but there is a
chance that, owing to the low ruling prices and increas-
ing demand, coarse crossbreds may show an improve-
ment. Locally the inclination' is to wait and
see how the London cat jumps, and as the Sydney
selling season does not open till the 22nd, the
London cat will have been jumping for nearly a fort-
night and the local people will have a pretty good idea
of the animal’s tendencies. With the exception of a sale
ex trucks, Darling Harbour, at 2s. 9kl. per bushel, wheat
transactions have been very limited. Arrivals consisted
mostly of medium quality and the bulk of them were taken
into the store, as owners won’t part at prices offered.
London cables report a hardening to the extent of 6d. per
quarter on previous quotations, but freights are so high
and scarce that shippers cannot offer as much as the
local millers even at the bedrock prices ruling for the
manufactured article. Best samples are quoted at 2s. 9d.
to 2s. 9Ad. ; good F.A.Q., 2s. 8d., pinched and chick-
wheat, 2s. 3d. to 2s. 6d. per bushel.*

GENERAL
Two Years’
-A. ZHTRIETSTID
DEBILITY.
Sufferings.
ITsT TSTEIHTD.
The Care of
Mr. ROBERT GRAHAM.
(Reported for “ The Star.”)
The increased facilities for recreation
afforded to shopkeepers and their em-
ployees under the provisions of the Early
Closing Bill are often appreciated by people
who do not come directly within the
operation of the Act. It gives “ outsiders”
an opportunity of meeting folks enjoying a
half-holiday in the middle of the week who
are not at liberty at any other time, except
after business hours. Knowing that Mr.
Robert Graham, of No. 11 Wattle-street,
Chippendale, was always disengaged on
Wednesday afternoons, our reporter
thought it an opportune time to obtain an
interview in relation to his indisposition,
and was rewarded by finding the gentleman
mentioned sitting on his verandah.
“I have called to ask a few question
about your illness,” began the scribe.
“ You are quite welcome to ask as many
questions as you like, and I will freely
answer them.”
“ What was your complaint, and how
long were you ill ? ”
“ I suffered from debility, in conjunction
with indigestion, for fully two years.
I had been engaged in sewer work for
some time, and the bad air played havoc
with my constitution. My health had been
very indifferent for a long time before I
paid much attention to it. arid then it was
compulsory, for the disease had worked so
slowly and. so stealthily that I did not
give in till it had mastered me completely.
1 thought that the unhealthy feelings would
go away when the sewer work was finished ;
but I was greatly mistaken. I could not
go on working through the violent pains in
my head and chest, which afterwards ex-
tended .all over my body, and I got very
feeble in the legs. My appetite became
fickle, and gradually died away altogether.
Anything I did succeed in getting down had
a nauseous taste, and proved quite unpalat-
able. I could not sleep well at night. I
went to bed feeling tired and weary, and
after sleeping an hour or so I had to put
in the remainder of the night wide awake
When it was time to get up I had to drag
myself out of bed with much reluctance, not
having enough energy to dress. I could
not look at. my breakfast, and would start
the day with only a cup of tea on my
stomach. My strength gradually caved in,
as I could not take any food to sustain it,
and I lost all animation and vitality. Water
was my principal drink, being a ways
thirsty. My stoma, h was too much upset
to allow of my taking anything else, so you
may imagine how debilitated I was. There
was not an hour of the day or night that I
could say I was free from a depressing
languidness that made me melancholy be-
yond description. A sign of my weakness
was the way I used to perspire without any
exertion. Before I was taken iil I was as
hard as. nails, and could do a good day’s
work without raising a sweat. In other
ways the debility made itself manifest,
notably by the symptoms of extreme
nervousness, from which I suffered. I
used to think I had not got any nerves at
all, but now the least thing would startle
me, and set me all of a tremble. I fought
hard against my affliction, and often forced
myself to eat when I had not the slightest
inclination for food. The result was usually
very disagreeable, my enfeebled digestion
constantly turning on me. I experienced
dull aching pains between the shoulders
b ortly after these forced repasts, and it
was not long before the food came up again.
Ihe retching involved in the operation
made my headaches, from which I was
never free, increase in violence. My
tongue was coated with a thiol - , limy .sub-
stance, which made my breath di agreeable.
The fellows with whom I worked used to
remark how awfully miserable I looked,
and in truth I was frightfully thin and
pale ”
“ What medicines did you take during
your illness ? ”
“I think I must have taken every
medicine under the sun. At all events I
visited the hospital for six weeks, and
brought back all sorts of physic and pills,
but they did not benefit me in the slightest
degree. I did not get any relief from pain,
neither did they promote an appetite. At
the end of six weeks I gave the hospital
best, and consulted a doctor. Tie advised
me to take Clements Tonic. I felt rather
doubtful about his advice. After having
taken so many mixtures before, I became
very incredulous about medicines, and so I
sought the advice of another doctor, who
also recommended me to take Clements
Tonic. Without more delay I procured a
bottle, and the effect was most remarkable.
I had believed that my nervousness could
not be cured by any medicine, but to my
unspeakable delight I felt my nerves more
restful in three or four days after the intro-
duction of Clements To ic. Sound refresh-
ing sleep came back quite naturally, and
my terrific headaches were amongst the first
of my tormenting symptoms to be subdued.
The bodily pains left me one by one, and
my strength returned as my appetite got
better*, thus enabling me to eat the food
that my worn-out frame so much needed.
There was an entire absence of pain in the
stomach after eating, and I was never after-
wards in doubt about my meals being
properly digested and doing their work
thor uglily. Clements Tonic restored my
bowels to healthy action, and my tongue
lost its o' noxious roughness and became
clean and sweet again. The excessive
perspiration no longer remained to remind
me of my weakness, as I became stronger
and stouter every day. I took quite a
different view of life under my altered con-
ditions, and the feelings of melancholy
troubled me no more. When I had taken four
bottles of Clements Tonic I was quite strong
again, mv nerves were as sound as a bell,
and my digestion was all that I could wish it
to be.”
“Can I report these particulars in the
papers.”
“You may with pleasure. I owe my
restoration to health solely to Clements
Tonic, and if any people should happen to
read about my case who are suffering in the
same way they may be glad of my experience.
Publish how and when you like.”
STATUTORY DECLARATION.
I, Robert Graham, of No. 11 Wattle-street Chip,
ptndale, Sydney, in the Colony of New South Wale*,
do solemnly and sincerely declare that I have care-
fully read the annex*d document, consisting - of four
folios and consecutively numbered from one to four
and that it contains and is a true and faithful account
of my illness and cure by Clements Tonic, and also
contains my full permission to publish in any way my
statements—which I give voluntarily, without receiv-
ing any payment; and I make this solemn declaration
conscientiously believing the fame to be true, and by
\ irtue of the provisions of an Act made and passed in
the ninth year of the reign of her present Majesty
intituled “An Act for the more effectual abolition of
Oaths and Affirmations taken and made in the various
Departments of the Government of New South Wales
and to substitute Declarations in lieu thereof and for
the suppression of voluntary and Extra-judicial Oaths
and Affidavits.
