“ Yangan ” to The Bulletin on certain
Chinese characteristics:—
Spence Evison errs in thinking the Northern Chow
soft. The Shantung man differs from him of the Yangtsze
Valley, as he, in turn, differs from the Kwang Tong—the
typical Southerner. Lengthy residence in those parts has
taught me that the Kwang Tong man is shifty and cruel,
but brave and ever ready to quarrel and plunder. The
Chinese pirates and bandits are mostly Southerners, and
the Kwang Tong and neighboring provinces would be
more difficult to subdue than those in the Yangtsze
Valley, which is peopled by much more inoffensive agri-
culturists. The people in the North, from Shantung,
say, westward to Yunnan and Szechuen, are pastoralists
as well as tillers of the soil, and though not pugnacious,
are brave and readily amenable to discipline! and good
treatment. Perhaps the Northerner has more the attri-
butes of the Northern European, while the Kwang Tong
has the worst traits of the Southerner, leaving the better,
more peaceable qualities of both to the Yangtzse man.
The Southerners alone brave the chances of emigrating ;
the middlemen appear content with their lot, while he
from the North, being a poor hand at commerce, leaves to
his more pushing Southern compatriots the task of
growing cabbages at Rose Bay and washing dirt out and
leprosy in to the shirts laundried in Little Bourke-st.
The various Sunday-school authorities of
three Melb. suburbs lately took a local census as
to the attendance thereat of the suburban young-
sters. It was found that—excluding some 35
families- who were given as of no denomination,
though possibly of very real religion—no less than
80 per cent, of the children attended a Sabbath -
school of some sort. Whereby is evinced the
hollowness of the parson’s cry that he cannot
reach the children and that the State must under-
take the work. Why, the departmental reports
show that not far short of 20 per cent, of Victorian
ohildren are, at present, habitually “ playing the
wag.” The aforesaid census furthur brought to
light the fact that in 1200 families there were no
less than 20 different brands of religion, including
Salvarmy, Bible Christians, and plain, ordinary
Christians.

£100,000 subscribed for his scheme of an “ oversea
colony ” for London slumites ?

BILE BEANS ALWAYS SUCCESSFUL.
Another instance of the wonderful power of Bile Beans comes
from Brisbane. Mr. G. Graham, who resides at Lensae-street
Beaumont Estate, Highgate Hill, Brisbane, gave some interesting
particulars of his sufferings and cure by Bile Beans to our re-
presentative. Mr. Graham said :
“By occupation lam a carter, and my age is 31. During the past
ten years I have been a constant sufferer from biliousness, giddi-
ness and headaches, and sometimes through the biliousness I was
unable to move. During these ten years of misery I consulted
numerous doctors, who attributed my symptoms to my habit of
smoking strong tobacco, the discontinuation of which gave me no
relief. I had also tried many patent medicines but obtained no re-
lief whatever, so when a friend advised me to try Bile Beans I had
about lost faith in all medicines in general, but when mv friend
pressed me to give them a trial and accompanied his advice with a box
of the Beans, I promised I should do so. After the first box I felt
a decided improvement and continued a course of five boxes by
which time I felt completely cured. My appetite, which had been
very bad before, returned to an alarming extent. It is now
five months since my cure, and none
of the old symptoms have returned,
and I feel as strong and robust as a
horse.
I have recommended vour Beans to
my friends, and I should like my
recommendation to go further by
your making use of this statement as
I am only too proud to be known as
one of the many who praise Bile
Beans for Biliousness, the working
man’s friend.”

Awakened.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Mortimer burst into the dressing-room.
“ What d’ye think —Quirk has a real live girl!
Lend me some number three, somebody.”
He rubbed the grease-paint into his full-blooded
cheeks as ho detailed the news to the incredulous
actors.
The company was opening for a fortnight in the
town. Salaries had been miraculously regular,
satisfied publicans were left in each town, and
local mails were largely increased by missives in
feminine hand-writing, re-directed many times by
cursing post - officials. Assistant-stage-manager
Quirk—a lanky creature of toppling angles, tame
enthusiasm, and pretty delicacies —had been a jest
to Mortimer all along. But the antipathy became
pronounced in Brisbane, when Quirk refused to
make one in a Sunday-drive party. Mortimer’s
lady of the intended quartet had a duenna sister
to be diverted. Of the company, Quirk alone was
free, and could give no legitimate reason for not
obliging his fellow-man. He blushed as girls are
supposed to, looked unhappy, stuttered, and ran
away from the proposer. So Mortimer had a
wasted day, while Quirk remained in his hotel
with a Latin grammar and a missal. Hencefor-
ward Mortimer flung enlightening anecdotes at
Quirk as regards his ridiculous virtue, and
drew pointed morals from the appalling nervous-
ness that beset the assistant-stage-manager.
“He was talking hard in her ear all the way
down the street. I followed—ankles, boys,
beautiful ankles and a silk petticoat. They
turned into the Cathedral . . .”
“You can’t beat churches for dark corners,”
commented the heavy man—a veteran.
“ And sometimes the organ-loft is unlocked ”
added an experiencer of thirty years in the
business.
“But they were kneeling—where the light was
strongest—he’s working the religious racket, you
see.”
Quirk shambled into the room. He was made
up in a footman’s whiskers, and his wire legs were
more than usually irresponsible in stockings. At
his hilarious reception, his knees wobbled and
misery came into his face.
“Shake hands, old man,” said Mortimer with
genuine heartiness. “ I owe you a public apology
—you’re a man ! Why didn’t you say you had a
girl to be faithful to—we never guessed. You
took us all in.”
Quirk’s face grew more wretched under the
lining. The idea of sex always hurt him, he flew
from it and from Mortimer, who embodied half of
it. And the word “girl” from Mortimer’s red
lips had a significance that was a violation.
“ Three months since you’ve seen her, and not
looked at another since. You’re a man I admire,
though its beyond me. Shake hands, laddie,—
why, you can give points to us all.”
Quirk did not give his hand.
“ Is—er—it another of your obscene jokes ? ” he
quavered.
“We know all about it—that girl you were with
this afternoon.”
Quirk jerked his head like a fowl.
“Mr. Mortimer, I don’t want any of your
blackguardly insinuations—please.”
It might have been delivered by a pettish school-
girl.
Mortimer’s eyes wrinkled eloquently.
“’Twon’t do, Quirky. I saw you. Where did
you pick her up? You’ll introduce me, won’t
you ? ”
Quirk was beyond control. He struck Mor-
timer on the temple—with a most absurdly in-
effective right. Mortimer dodged some of it and
laughed, while the others held Quirk, who actually
sobbed. He appealed to them all, to their sense
of decency, to the memory of their mothers ; he
grew chaotic and unintelligible till they all
laughed. Then he called names, and they pushed
him out to get cool when he threatened broken
bottles for the next man that baited him.
“That’s the result of the girl,” said Mortimer,
re-blackening an eyebrow that Quirk had smudged.
“ He hadn’t ginger enough before. I told him so
all along. He’ll soon improve.”
But Quirk was more than usually blundering
that night. He nearly forgot to change and was
going on for a policeman in his shirt-sleeves; he got
his lights muddled and sent the.supers on too
soon. The manager talked volcanically, and
Quirk returned to liis hotel hopelessly miserable
and soon to be made more so. For the landlord,
with apologies, asked him, as the hotel was un-
usually crowded, would he mind sharing a room
with Mr. Mortimer? It was too late to go else-
where, and he had to assent for that night, though
the prospect of more Mortimer was dreadful. He
ate his supper of pickles and ginger-ale, and turned-
in and read his Latin Grammar in bed till the
laughter in the bar ceased and he heard Mortimer
on the stairs. Then he outed the candle and pre-
tended to be asleep. Mortimer lurched into the
room, fumbled, and lit the candle. He was only
three-quarters mellow, and apostrophised the
huddled-up Quirk.
“ Man’s a marvel ! . . . Faithful to one girl for
three months. You’re a freak, Quirk, a pheno-
menal freak. You’re a model in the profession—
why, ye could star yourself.”
He undressed —admiring his hairy chest-muscles.
“ What’s your religion been to you ? That’s what
bothers me. Doesn’t make you fat. You can
hardly carry yer own portmanteau. Ye’re a
rotten study. You ain’t happy.” . . .
He got into bed quite content with monologue.
“ Told you so all along. Get a girl of yer own —
or somebody else’s —no matter so long as it’s girl.
Stick to her as long as yer can—when you can’t,
get another.” . . .
He extinguished the candle, which was out of
reach, by throwing a sock at it.
“Goo’ night, Quirky, old chap . . . he’s
sleep. . . . Three months ... damn it, it’s a
record.”
Not even beer could make Mortimer a heavy
sleeper. He was awakened by someone whisper-
ing. Had he been dreaming ?—no, there it was
again. Surely he heard his own name —it sounded
like his, through the keyhole He certainly had not
expected any angels’ visits. He turned his head
on the pillow, and saw in the dimness the white
figure of Quirk kneeling by his bed, praying per-
fervidly to be saved from Mortimer, and for
Mortimer to be saved from himself.
Mortimer listened to the whispered invocation
with curious amusement. He heard various un-
known saints appealed-to on behalf of his soul,
and the proceedings appeared impertinent—but
at the petition that he should change his life—he
was annoyed, and began to plot revenge. He did
not want to change his life, and was not going to
allow a freak to pray for him.
* * * *
It was the last piece of the season, and Morti-
mer was finished in the first act. He had soon
forced an introduction to the girl—a Laura Ede-
son. Even in the cipher presence of Quirk the
.acquaintance deepened into intimacy—thanks to
the privileges of the profession. To-night she was
to be at the gardens, where there were to be music
and illuminations ; it was almost an appointment.
Of course she would have a companion—to
“prude” for her. Mortimer took a brother-
artist to talk to the companion—the heavy man
who was gouty and could not walk far.
He manoeuvred her up the hill to look at the
illuminations. A step further and the crest shut
them out; it was dark, and the voices and the
music were united to a murmur. The air was
tremulous and warm as a breath. Other couples
passed on into the darkness of the trees. Soft
words, low laughter, and eloquent silences went
with them.
Laura was animated and talkative. Mortimer
replied, throwing whole “balcony-scenes” of affec-
tion into every commonplace.
“ I’m sorry Mr. Quirk couldn’t get away. Poor
fellow, he works too hard. He’ll never save all
the money he wants to.”
“ P’raps not,” said Mortimer, lifting her elbow
over a bank and keeping it.
“There are so many expenses, travelling, and it
costs such a lot to become a priest.”
Mortimer was not paying much attention to her
revelations as such, He had not come out to dis
cuss Quirk’s prospects. His own feelings absorbed
him ; he was radiating tenderness ; the situation
demanded placid yielding to the spirit of the
scene, and her rapid prose was out of harmony
with a night created on purpose. Silence he
could have understood, but this made him doubt
himself.
“ And he’s so good—you’ve no idea of the trou-
ble he took to get Ma to turn .”
“ Turn —what?”
“ Catholic, you know ... he sent her books,
and introduced her to the bishop.”
Mortimer had won her waist without resistance
—or even notice, apparently. At no time did he
depend on his eloquence so much as on a timely
patience for developments. He was beginning to
hear a forced note in her talk—was it to allow
herself to appear unconscious ?
“ And he has been trying to convert me, too —
only I’m stupid.”
“ Why didn’t he commence with you—l would,”
he said.
“ Would you ? ” looking at him—her eyes in the
starlight all woman.
The necessary uncontrollable impulse came to
him, and he kissed her.
With a wild cry she flung herself on his breast.
“ I knew—l knew you would save me.”
She was his right enough ; she returned his
kisses and clung to him, but he was puzzled.
“ Save you from what ? ”
“From the convent —from their religion.”
“ From where ? ”
‘‘ Ma’s new one. She wants me to be a nun now;
when she was a Bible Christian she wanted me to
be a missionary. I get religion day and night. I
hate it. I hate everything. But you will save
me. I knew I couldn’t be mistaken when I saw
you. You will save me—you will ?”
Of course he would ; but he was troubled all the
same. He had expected intrigue, not romance.
Passion was expensive in entanglements. It
made him think, and thought is fatal. He found
she had plans elaborated, and he began to feel
angry.
“You will speak to the manager for me. lam
sure I can act.
“ It’s a hard life, and perhaps —”
“ Not with you—my husband ! ”
The word was a cold bath to him.
“ Damn it—no ! I’ve a wife and two children
in Melbourne.”
She dropped with a low moan at his feet, as one
dead.
Mortimer, bending over her, received a stagger-
ing blow. It was on the other temple, from Quirk,
who was before him, absolutely mad.
“ I knew you were here, and I rung the curtain
down too soon, and I’ve been discharged, and 1
don’t care if you do kill me.” . ,
Mortimer was angry this time and settled Quirk s
mad rush with a knock-out. Laura sprang to the
prostrate man, and Mortimer thought he had
better leave.
“ It’s a moral victory for me, anyway,” he mur-
mured—to his pride.
Quirk did not return to the business. He took
a fancy-goods shop and managed to marry Laura
Edeson. But he never thanked Mortimer.
VIC.
THE STROLDER.
Welland—the Newcastle (N.S. W.) butcher
against whom a Mrs. Pearson recently obtained a
verdict for £1250 for the seduction of her daughter
—has filed his schedule. Getting a verdict is a
very different thing from getting the cash.
“ Pedagogue ” to The Bulletin :
Suppose you are a public school-teacher with a griev-
ance, say, about salary. You interview “ The Depart-
ment”—in your best clothes. A mistake ! You ought
to put on your old patched pants (or, if a “ female,” the
dress with the frayed hem), and so give them an object-
lesson. The Department isn’t too bad, and you are not
kept waiting more than three solid hours, which time you
pass wondering what all the blue papers piled up in the
bare room are about, and speculating as to the age of the
fig-trees outside. There’s a permanent photograph of
those fig-trees on the brain of every public school-teacher
in N.S.W. They are synonymous with ancient—very
ancient—red tape.
Well, when you see the Department at last, it doesn’t
break your back with a stick. In fact, it is really sympa-
thetic, but is quite sure the Public Service Board -won’t
entertain your case for a moment. You suggest trying
it. “ Not a bit of use, my dear sir.” In spite of that
you bring your guns to bear on the P.S.B. over the street,
and you fondly hope things will be better when you lose
sight of those d d fig-trees.
“ Not in. Just gone out. You must have met him on
the stairs. Yes, Mr. is in ; will you see him?”
“ Yes, anyone,” you say in desperation. Mr. is a
gentleman ; you see that at a glance. He’ll do his best
for you, but “we generally leave these matters to the
Department. Mr. manages these things ; you should
see him.” “Saw him five minutes ago; said it was a
matter for the P. 5.8.” Silence! “Um! write a letter,
and we will do what we can for you.”
You go away gladly to write the letter, an ’ begin to
plan what you’ll do with that £2O rise which the De-
partment, you think, has been chiselling you out of for
the past four years !
An answer from the P.S.B. ; so polite and respectful ;
so different from the Departmental memos. Your heart
leaps—but only for a moment. The P.S.B. has referred
the matter to the Department. Later, a letter from the
Department to the effect that a communication has been
received from the P. 5.8., and it does not see the necessity
for granting your request. You feel yourself between
the lion and the bear, and you would like to know which
is the bete noire, and, also, though of secondary import-
tance, how matters are made a necessity for either of ’em !
The Crow and the Pitcher
A MORAL.
A certain crow, who had been haranguing a
ward caucus all the evening, had contracted a
large, importunate thirst.
As he was looking about to find some means of
relieving himself of this same thirst, he discovered
a pitcher which contained water.
But the pitcher was very large, and the quantity
of water it contained was small, and reposed
at the very bottom of the pitcher.
For anatomical reasons the crow could not dip
his bill into the water, and drink from the pitcher
as it was.
Nor was there any use in trying to pour out the
water, for the crow could not lift the pitcher.
Besides, he had no cup.
But he was a wise crow, and also persistent,_ and
he immediately resolved himself into a committee
of the whole on ways and means, and hatched out
a solution of the problem.
He picked a pebble from many lying around and
dropped it into the pitcher.
He kept on dropping pebble after pebble in the
pitcher, until the water approached near enough
to the top for him to drink, and he slaked his
thirst with ease.
What a splendid moral there is in the action of
that wise crow. Dear reader, see the moral in
our light. We are proprietors of the famous
Bile Beans for Biliousness. So many people try
one dose, one box, of Bile Beans, and expect to
be cured of an ailment that has taken years to
fasten itself in the system. Bile Beans, if given
a fair show, will cure nearly every ailment that
is the result of a sluggish liver’s action and a weak
stomach. The cost is only per large box,
and surely you can afford to make one, two, or
three trips, if necessary, to prove the moral of the
crow ana the pitcher, namely, that perseverance
in the right direction means victory. Bile Beans
persevered in give health—that great blessing
that should and could be possessed by us all if
we persevered, and not expect that the first few
Beans would enable us to drink of the pitcher of
health. Health was not lost in a day, therefore
it cannot be regained in a casual manner.
A HINT.
Summer sickness may be prevented by Spring
renovation, but any haphazard attempt at the
latter may only the more surely prepare the
system for the former. If “a stitch in time saves
nine ” when applied to our garments, it may apply
with equal truth and felicity to the body the gar-
ments envelop. Bile Beans for Biliousness wifi
prepare the system for the Summer; they are an
ideal spring medicine. The Summer will soon
be upon us, and at this time of year we should
take care to cleanse the whole human system and
fortify it to withstand the enervating effects of
the Australian Summer. The increasing Lot
weather of this period has an extremely debilitat-
ing effect on the system if it is not in perfect
order all through. It will thus be seen that a
medicine which will regulate and cleanse the in-
terior mechanism of man is especially valuable at
this time of the year. Such a medicine is Bile
Beans, and no better preparation can be found
for toning up the digestive organs, cleansing th,
blood, ana invigorating the liver.

An Ideal.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
She came with the delicate rose of the dawn,
Ere my soul had forgotten the night;
Her kiss was as sunshine that fell on my brow,
And the world was aglow with delight.
She danced in her daintiness, far from me then,
Yet her smiles made a sudden pulse start:
I sought her, with limbs all electric with love,
And the heat of desire in my heart.
She showed me a peak that was crested with fire,
And it tow’red like a Titan on high ;
I knew, when ’twas scaled, I should hold her again,
And no death should dissever the tie.
Then, red with the sense of the beauty of her,
As she guided, I followed the way,
And scorned all the dullards who delved in the
fields
That so tame and so tutored were they.
I slew the wild fears that arose and outcried
And would scare from the end to be won,
Nor yet did I faint in the stony ravines
That had borrowed the breath of the sun.
I conquered the thickets that hindered my hope,
Though they tore and retarded a while ;
My spirit was spurred to its purpose again
By the light of her wonderful smile.
I knew never a fear of the forest-bred shapes,
Nor of turbulent torrents to pass—
But soon was betrayed by mine enemy, Earth,
To the clutch of a giant morass :
Then darkened the fires that had filled me before,
In the evil embrace of the snare ;
Till, wrestling no more with the depths of the
slime,
I surrendered my strength to despair.
No longer she beams, for the graces have gone
That were witching and radiant to see ;
She sorrows apart with the tears on her face,
And her eyes are a torment to me.
I laugh at the hopes that outbalanced my power
With a scorn that’s too bitter to speak ;
Like blossoms polluted they rest in the mire,
While the glory still flames on the peak.
But yet she is near me, though dim and forlorn,
In the face of the hurrying night;
And, fast though it bind me, the mind cannot mar
All the vision I have of the height.
So, therefore, I know she’ll be gracious again,
And that, flushing and .smiling and sweet,
She will stoop in her beauty to kiss me once more
When I’m ragged and dead at her feet.
n.s.w.
CECIL POOLE.
The Return.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
“No change after all the years!” He looked
back at the white-roofed station where the grey-
haired porter still stared from the door of the
lamp-room. A yellow dogcart rattled down the
stony lane where the dark poplars loomed so
straight against the sky.
He stood unnoticed near the station parapet.
Across the fields on his left a tiny footbridge
spanned the Avon, giving it the appearance of a
cross-hilted sword. He was waiting for the wind
to lift across Thorndyke’s meadow so that he
might again feel the sharp ecstasy of briar and
hawthorn and thedelicate sting of new-mown hay.
A sail-less boat wound up stream; the rooks caw-
ing in the poplars brought back the memory of
George Eliot’s house, its dark eaves, its rook-
infested shadows. A mackerel cloud dappled the
purple silence above the close-packed meadows.
The desired wind came, filling him with the odour
of nuts, and broom, and earth that had drunk
deeply of rain. How sweet it was after the reek-
ing eucalypt, the stench of hides, and the vile
atmosphere of the woolsheds !
“Home again! home again!” Here in this
pebble-strewn English lane he shouted it from
hedge to hedge where the drooping red - may
trailed like women’s skirts over bank and ditch.
The harebells nodded ; the nettle-choked hollows
gave up their incense. It was good, and his suffo-
cating heart answered with a leap.
Beyond the grey palings of Kenyon’s mill he
espied the russet angles of a wide-eaved Eliza-
bethan house. The scullery smoke wound in white
ropes over the slate-roofed outbuildings.
He stayed for a moment against Kenyon’s
palings, and the throb of the mill brought to mind
the beat of the liner’s engines that had borne him
years ago to Australia. The passage-out had
been full of tropic wonders and new friends, of
nights when the black glamour of the sea reflected
the roof of a heaven deep and wide and flowered
with stars. He had stayed in Egypt for one mad
week to initial many wine-cards, and to fraternise
on Shepheard’s verandah with the posing Chasseurs
d’Afrique. He had feasted his eyes upon naked
humanity, passed with well-bred insolence the
veiled women near the citron-shaded seraglios.
He had heard the click, click of shuttered windows
when Zuleika peeped to admire his cream-white
skin and to compare him with the light of her
days—the querulous, pock-marked Ben Abdullah.
There were days of hand-shaking on the Palace
steps with pashas who spoke of Arabi, and remem-
bered Plevna and the Red Day at Kars.
The past was the past; but his seven nights in
Egypt stung him yet with the witchery of purple
darkness, and the desert that gave up a white
Asian moon to gorge his senses with the brick-red
splendor of the East. The rest of his life had been
spent amongst cattle-men and shearers in the
back-blocks of Queensland, where, in eight years,
he learned to sweep a woolshed floor and sit a
squealing buckjumper before the crack riders from
the Maranoa and the Castlereagh. Also, he had
humped a swag and cadged his way from the Dia-
mantina to the Murray Bridge. Tar and grease
had smeared his gilt-edged pride. Sick of the
solitudes, craving with a fierce nostalgia that
burned in every vein, he had hied himself to Sand-
ringham Pier and plotted with the stokers of
many ocean-tramps. His home-coming had been
by way of a wool-ship, and the story of his passage
round the Cape would make profitable reading for
intending stowaways.
* * m *
He glanced again at the brown-gabled Eliza-
bethan house, and down at his wire-gabled boots.
If he would reach the side-door unperceived he
must scale the boundary wall, and creep along
under cover of the hedge before the dogs scented
him. If the servants were new they would stop
him cheerfully on the threshold and send for the
local policeman. If they were those who knew
him in the old days they might recognise him
through an inch of beard ; but the betting seemed
to favor the arrival of the policeman before he
could deliver his opening statement.
Two well-dressed Americans passed him without
a glance. One of them twirled an orange stick
until he seemed to invest it with his own over-
flowing vitality.
“Yes, sir; this country was made for Shake-
speare. Isn’t that Judith’s place? Ah, well, I
guess it’s Ann Hathaway’s. Well, we’re here,
anyhow, and I opine that it’s greener than Ken-
tucky. Shakespeare’s land. Yes, sir.”
They passed towards Stratford with gesticu-
lating arms. Once more he stole past Kenyon’s
palings, eyeing his shabby clothes and smiling
grimly. In the years of his absence he had
dreamed how he might return in splendour to his
people —something neat in the way of a silver-
mounted brougham and three Jap postilions had
filled his eye. In the warm Gulf nights he had
planned stealthy, indefinable tricks in finance
whereby he would scoop much cash and the bitter
hatred of capitalists. But his altitudes of
midnight imagery were always dissolved by the
fierce light of the sun, and evening found him
with the lotos-eaters on some palm-lined beach,
or tramping wearily in quest of the Happy Station
where the overseer spoke kind words to the
famishing swagman.
* * * *
His defeat seemed colossal, and his triumphant
return was by way of the side-door. Nothing in
the world distressed him so much as the thought
of what his father would say.
But there was one at least in this very respect-
able house who would look at him through tears.
He braced-up.
The cawing of rooks depressed him a little ; it
recalled the quorking Australian crow, and he
wanted very badly to forget the naked plains, the
silence-haunted jungles of North Queensland —he
wanted to forget them for ever.
It was nearly eight o’clock now, but the light
held all along the green vistas of Stratford. A
smell of burning larch hung about the shadows.
Eave and roof and spire caught the light from the
western sky.
The wall scaled, he crouched nearer under the
hedge. Suddenly a crimson stain illumined the
oak-shadowed window ; he saw a hand clasp the
crystal bowl of a lamp and move it into the centre
of a table. The sharp gravel bit his thin soles as
he slunk across the drive. For a moment he stood
in the porch with a trembling hand lifted to the
bell. What would they say ? Would they re-
member him ? He drew a long breath and dragged
fit the brass handle as though it were a tooth ; and
wires and bell answered with the tongues of a fury
that had awaited his home-coming for centuries.
The sound pealed and raged, and the atmosphere
of respectability was shaken to shreds. He
braced himself against the portico and waited for
kinship and love to spring at him from the
silence.
* * * *
The door opened; a strange man in plum-
coloured livery stood, like the embodiment of
Rebuke, in the yellow avenue of wainscot; his
thin sharp nose suemed to retreat from the in-
truder as though he were an abominable smell.
“What do you want?” he said quietly. In his
hand was a bunch of cellar-keys which he rattled
and spun around the tip of his little finger. He
was contemptuously amused. “ There is a scullery
door, my man ; why didn’t you go round to the
scullery door ? ”
♦ * * *
In the mind of the home-comer there leaped a
sudden hatred against this sneering image, a
hatred that fought down to his finger-tips until he
controlled a desire to catch the liveried body and
fling it against the respectable wainscot. Instead,
he took off his battered Australian hat and crossed
the threshold.
“I’m the long-lost son,” he said steadily.
“ There now, you’d better let me in.”
The plum-coloured image of respectability
smiled. “What do you want, please?” He
made a weary gesture with his keys as though it
were his duty to be bored. “ Please stand off the
oilcloth, young man ; oilcloths have to be cleaned.
Now, what do you want? ”
“Neither money nor food,” said the home-
comer cheerfully. “ I want to see mother. Don’t
stare because I happen to look like the old man
of the sea. I’ve been playing with the pigs, and
I’m dirty. I’ve arrived. I’m prodigal Jimmy.
You’d better tell mother. Afterwards I’ll kick
you, so help me God ! ”
A finger-plated door opened noiselessly. A man
with a white forehead and thin lips stood craning
forward in the tinted lamp-glow. He made no
gesture, but his narrow eyes struck a lightning
gleam of recognition. This was the man who
haunted the home-comer’s dreams, looming always
through the misty years like a frowning Bona-
parte ; smileless, austere, and with a brain and eye
that overlooked pyramids but remembered the
microscopic sand-shells at the pyramid’s base.
Like the Corsican he stooped forward, while the
unuttered words that hung on his lips took lan-
guage in his eyes.
“ Ah, you have returned at last—you who
bragged ! Do you remember how you bragged ?
Oh ! you failure ! ”
The butler twirled his keys mechanically ; he
even advanced half a pace to adjust the screen of
the incandescent lamp.
Some fair-haired children and three strapping
boys stole from a near room and regarded the
home-comer critically. The words “Australia . . .
Jim . . . comeback” seemed to freeze on their
lips.
And Jim the wanderer straightened himself un-
consciously. He said: “Well, I’m Jim. I’ve
come home . . . I’m Jim!”
Nobody answered. The butler twirled his keys.
“I’m Jim,” he reiterated. He had become
wildly certain that no one had heard him. “ Well,
I’ve come back.”
The man with the white forehead and thin lips
turned hastily into the lamp-lit room. His part-
ing words seemed to come through iron walls
away back from the beginning of things. “You
bragged . . . you failure ! Bah ! ”
An inner door closed with a snap. The butler
stared at the antlers over the coat-rack and twirled
his keys.
* * * *
The home-comer stepped forward and paused.
He wanted to rush after the man with the white
forehead and cry out, “Father ! father ! I tried ;
but the world beat me ! ”
The children retreated with fear, disappoint-
ment, panic, in their eyes.
High above him were winding balustrades where
griffins gaped at the prancing unicorns over the
stair-head. The dark curtains moved from the
touch of a hand; a grey-haired woman came
slowly, very slowly, along the corridor and peered
do.wn. “ Children, children,” she said gently ;
“go back to your lessons; you must not annoy
your father.”
One of the boys mounted a stair in frantic haste,
gesticulating with both hands.
“Mamma! . . . it’s Jim, Jim from Australia!
. . . He’s come back ! . . . Quick ! . . . mamma,
it’s Jim ! ”
For a moment Jim quailed. HI saw the white
hands open and shut, saw the two brown eyes
spring at him like hungry stilettos. The distance
between them was not great, just a stair and a
hand-rail. He made an upward step slowly, then
caught the words on her lips, and the years of
black shame struck him again. There was nothing
between them ; he looked for the fierce reproach
in her eyes, but her hands were upon him before
he could fling himself upon his knees.
“James, James ! ... So you have come back :
Ah, God has been good to me at last ... I
thought. . . And you wrote twice in eight years—
twice in eight years. Back from the South . . .
My dear, I welcome you; indeed, indeed I wel-
come you. Oh, James! . . . twice in eight
years ! ”
“Mother, I failed!” He dropped his hands
and shut his eyes. “• The world doesn’t want me
. . . And I got left.” His wild hair, yet matted
with the salt of three oceans, was caressed by her
hand. “I’ve done with the world. I want
nothing, nothing; yet I came here . . . from the
outside of things . . . mother, where there was a
jungle deep, so deep, and a plain . . . Red ca-ttle
... oh yes, red-eyed, white-mouthed—tramping,
tramping, tramping. There were shags and
lags, blue grass, palms, and blackfellows that hid
in scrub and watched us . . . all about the lagoons.
This England is green and cold. Let me think !
let me think ! Oh, mother, I don’t want any-
thing, I don’t, I don’t! ”
“ James, James, you need not ask; the measure
shall be thrice filled. You have come.” She
stood up, resting on his shoulders, as she turned
with sudden menace to the wide-mouthed group
below. “Children, this is your brother James.
He has come from oversea. Some of you do not
remember. He is my first-born son. This land
and house are his to inherit. He is not poor;
he is rich because he is my son. Children ”...
Like an unleashed pack they hustled forward
and fell upon him with the ferocity of young
brothers.
“ Hulloa, Jim ! . . . Come away, you, Reuben,
let go his hand ! Jim . . . Oh . . . Say, Jim,
you didn’t bring any boomerangs . . . Hal’s got a
spear . . . from a Lascar . . . Albert Docks . . .
Great Spear. Come and look . . . Jim, what’s
the highest mountain in Victoria? . . . You let
go our Jim’s hand, d’ye hear, let go ! . . . Well,
I’ve seen a nully-nully in a museum, anyhow . . .
Isn’t he brown? Say, Jim, the Murrumbidgee
isn’t half as long as the Amazon, you know. Jim
. . . Oh, we’re glad you’re home . . . Mother
used to cry . . . Said you’d perished over there
. . . No fear ... he wouldn’t . . . Good old
Jim !”
• * * *
The bedroom in the west wing smiled at him
with white, familiar walls. The garden under-
neath, with its may-burdened hedgerows, smote
him with a fear of unreality.
He struck himself severely on the breast.
With all his vaunted bohemiamsm he felt that he
was coming under the vile influence of mullioned
windows.
A gentle tap at the door sent him hastily across
the room. The plum-colored butler was standing
outside, staling into the depths of some unfathom-
able abyss. There were no keys in his hand, but
the parched, unrelenting lines still held his mouth
in an iron curb of respectability. Jim faced him
half desperately, feeling that here at last was an
opportunity to get level with the oppressive
guardian of conventionalities.
“Well?” he said, bitingly. “What do you
want ? ”
For a moment the butler seemed to relax ; his
plum-coloured waistcoat took a gentler shade.
“Any orders? My lady . . . insists . . . your
commands .. . carried out immediately. Hot
water at eight. You will shave? Hot or cold?”
A frown darkened Jim’s brow. “ S’pose I can
shave if I like. S’pose I can have hot water if I
like. Is there anything in this house that I can’t
have?” he said, bitterly.
The butler wiped his lips delicately with a piece
of cambric; his frozen mien was fast thawing ;
feudal abasement lurked in his voice and joints.
“ Certainly. Sorry shaving not my duty though.
Saunders will be your man. Will tell Saunders
now. My lady orders . . . everything possible
. . . to be done. Breakfast at eight. Two gongs.
Good-night—sir.”
“ Oh, well; oh, well.” The frown relaxed from
Jim’s brow; he watched the respectable figure
dawdle down the corridor. “Oh, well . . . good-
night, old chap ! ”
ALBA DORION.
“A sink of iniquity” is little Parson
Mullineaux’s verdict on Johannesburg. Parsons
say that of all big cities —present scribe heard it
of Sydney and Melbourne before he came to Aus-
tralia—but the Rev. Mulli. is a man and a foot-
baller who doesn’t look at things with the eye of a
Jeremiah. And what he says about Johannes-
burg is what they all say.
The old R.C. Cathedral at Christchurch
(M.L.), a brick structure 108 ft. by 86ft., was lately
moved bodily 400 ft. away to make room for a new
cathedral. All the contents were taken out, the
stained-glass windows removed and the slates
taken off the roof. Then the building was raised
on sleepers, and three traction-engines and all the
screwjacks in the city did the rest.
The slow, slow grind of the legal mill.
Some time in the long, long ago one Hudson
sued Vic. railway department for damages over
something or other, and this was the gradual
course of the mill as it ground out the question at
issue between Hudson and the Vic. railway de-
partment. First trial, jury disagreed. Second
trial, Hudson got verdict for £750. Third trial,
(appeal to higher court) ’Hudson lost his £750, and
was left with nothing but costs. Fourth trial,
(appeal to still higher court) Hudson got back his
£750 —mostly gone in costs by this time. Fifth
trial to come yet; God knows what will happen
to Hudson. Sixth trial also in the far distance.
